Thesis Guide

Misaligned Narrative of the Ecological Role of Vachellia caven in Eastern South American Savannas Vachellia caven is a thorny leguminous shrub to treelet, widely distributed throughout the Neotropics and spanning across the Southern Cone from Bolivia, Paraguay and Brazil to Tierra del Fuego in Argentina and Chile. Most of the contemporary research focuses on the western range of the species distribution, in Chile, though this review emphasizes the species' long-term and widespread ecological importance in the eastern range, particularly within the Pampas of Southern South America. The Pampas is one of the most threatened open vegetation ecosystems, rapidly losing native habitats due to intense conversion for agriculture and livestock production. Driven by historical land use practices and the vast areas designated for livestock, xeric and spiny vegetation has long been suppressed and actively removed, as part of pasture management strategies. This review challenges the prevailing narrative that portrays V. caven as a controversial or ambiguous species, frequently labeled as "invasive woody encroacher". This designation, often documented in regions severely affected by conversion and overgrazing, reflects a socio-economic bias that emphasizes its purported negative impact on forage production while overlooking its ecological significance. The observed woody encroachment is fundamentally perceived as a symptom of anthropogenic degradation, including the historical removal of woody vegetation and chronic overgrazing. In contrast to this exploitative view, this xeric species acts as a crucial pioneer and "nurse" plant, essential for ecological succession and for sustaining a unique assemblage of native plants and animals. It facilitates the recruitment of other woody and herbaceous species by improving microhabitat conditions, including nitrogen enrichment and enhanced soil properties. By reframing V. caven’s encroachment as an indicator of ecosystem degradation rather than species invasiveness, we challenge the prevalent narrative that supports active removal of V. caven while underestimating its natural distributional range. Recognizing this species as fundamental to Pampas’ resilience and recovery is therefore essential for effective conservation and the development of sustainable restoration strategies.

Supports Woody Encroachment DynamicsGrazing and Land Use ImpactsSoil and Biogeochemical Cycling

Woody encroachment and related soil properties in different tenure-based management systems of semiarid rangelands

Alexandra Sandhage-Hofmanna, Dirk Wundramb, Lutz Weihermüllerd, Roland Papeb, Chris C. du Preezc, Wulf Amelunga, Jörg Löfflerb, Elmarie Kotzéc, Stef Weijersb, Vladimir Wingatee (2020)

Abstract

Woody encroachment is increasingly threatening savanna ecosystems, but it remains unclear how this is driven by different land tenures and management systems. In South Africa, communal land is mainly managed under continuous grazing, while commercial land is under rotational grazing. We hypothesize that woody encroachment has increased since the end of the Apartheid era in 1994, when rotational grazing systems in communal land switched to continuous grazing. To test this hypothesis, we sampled six subsites in each of three replicates of the two tenure systems in the savannah biome, South Africa, and monitored the degree of woody encroachment and effects on soil using remotely-sensed normalized difference vegetation index (NDVI), dendro-ecology, and grid-based soil analyses. The results confirmed that there has been a positive greening trend over the past 25 years in communal areas due to an increase in woody cover, particularly for tree in the 50–150 cm height class. These trees corresponded to Senegalia tree ages between 10 and 25 years. Greater woody cover in communal areas was accompanied by height-dependent elevated nutrient and organic matter concentrations that increased in the topsoils by a factor of up to 1.5, relative to the freehold systems. Isotopic analyses identified debris of C3 bushes and trees as main carbon input into the soil of communal areas, where the δ13C value of −20.1‰ was significantly lower than it was at private farms (−19.2‰). Isotopic values also indicated that this carbon input took place beyond the edges of the bush canopy. We conclude that the tenure-based management system in the communal areas has promoted woody encroachment, with the associated changes in soil properties homogenizing the savanna system.

Main Points

The study utilized three independent replicates (villages/farms) of two tenure systems in the Kuruman savanna biome, South Africa: communal areas (continuous grazing post-1994) and freehold farms (rotational grazing). The research aimed to test if the shift to continuous grazing in communal areas promoted woody encroachment and associated changes in soil properties.

Methods included monitoring woody cover using annual Landsat time-series (1984–2015) for NDVI trends, high-resolution octocopter images for current land cover, and grid-based soil sampling (0–10 cm) for nutrient analysis (Ca, Mg, K, Na, metals), SOC, N, and stable isotopes ($\delta^{13}$C and $\delta^{15}$N).

The results confirmed the core hypothesis: communal areas showed a positive greening trend (increased NDVI) over the 25-year period, attributed to a significant increase in woody cover, particularly *Senegalia mellifera* trees in the 50–150 cm height class, dating back 10 to 25 years (coinciding with the end of Apartheid and the shift to continuous grazing). This confirms that non-adapted management practices promoted encroachment.

Regarding soil properties, communal areas with greater woody cover exhibited height-dependent elevated concentrations of nutrients and organic matter, increasing in the topsoils by up to a factor of 1.5 compared to freehold farms. Isotopic analysis further identified C3 bushes and trees as the main carbon source in communal areas (significantly lower $\delta^{13}$C values: $-20.1\textperthousand$ vs. $-19.2\textperthousand$ on private farms), demonstrating a fundamental shift from C4 grass dominance. The isotopic data also suggested that C input extended spatially beyond the immediate tree/bush canopy edges.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that the tenure-based management system implemented in communal areas, specifically continuous grazing after 1994, acted as a primary local driver promoting widespread woody encroachment. This confirms the critical role of specific land management practices, rather than just global drivers like elevated CO2, in facilitating vegetation change at the regional scale.

Crucially, the study determined that this woody encroachment, while often perceived as purely detrimental (threatening livelihoods due to loss of palatable grass), resulted in ecological benefits in terms of soil quality. The increased woody cover led to the accumulation of nutrients and organic matter in the sandy Arenosols. The associated changes in soil properties, driven by C3 woody litter input, are actively homogenizing the savanna landscape structure. The paper implicitly supports the argument that encroachment driven by overgrazing does not necessarily equate to total ecosystem function loss, particularly regarding biogeochemical cycling.

  • "The results confirmed that there has been a positive greening trend over the past 25 years in communal areas due to an increase in woody cover, particularly for tree in the 50–150 cm height class."
  • "Greater woody cover in communal areas was accompanied by height-dependent elevated nutrient and organic matter concentrations that increased in the topsoils by a factor of up to 1.5, relative to the freehold systems."

  • "Among these, non-adapted rangeland management practices with overgrazing and associated spreading of bush seed by animals has been assumed to particularly promote woody encroachment (Reid et al., 2008; Teague et al., 2011; Sandhage-Hofmann et al., 2015)."
  • "Loss of grass cover provides new spaces and resources (nutrients, water) for the establishment of woody plants (February et al., 2013; Archer et al., 2017; Hempson et al., 2017)."
  • "Woody encroachment is mostly seen as a degradation process, because it threatens the livelihood of the rural populations. However, the increase in woody cover does not necessarily imply that the ecological functions of an ecosystem are lost (Eldridge et al., 2011; Archer et al., 2017)."
  • "For example, studies in rangelands of the Kalahari have shown that woody encroachment led to an increase of soil fertility with higher carbon, nitrogen, and nutrient concentrations in encroached areas compared to conditions under perennial grasses (Mureva et al., 2018; Thomas et al., 2018)."

Contradicts Woody Encroachment DynamicsGrazing and Land Use ImpactsConservation and Restoration Strategies

Contrasting effects of grazing on the early stages of woody encroachment in a Neotropical savanna

Lucía S. Mochia,b,*, Noemí Mazíac, Fernando Biganzolia, Martín R. Aguiard,e (2022)

Abstract

Woody encroachment in savannas represents an ecological process of current global interest given its negative impact on ecosystem functioning, particularly on forage production. Traditional savanna models propose competition and niche differentiation as the main mechanisms allowing tree-grass coexistence. Demographic models, instead, propose abiotic and biotic factors as bottlenecks controlling vital rates and transitions from seeds to adult trees. The role played by domestic grazing on woody encroachment is yet controversial. Here, using a multistage tree life approach, we combine both models and evaluate the role of grazing and herbaceous vegetation on woody recruitment in a Neotropical savanna dominated by Vachellia caven, a successful and widely spread encroacher tree species. We performed three experiments to evaluate seed predation, seedling emergence and survival of V. caven by manipulating cattle grazing (grazed and ungrazed areas) and herbaceous vegetation presence (vegetated and unvegetated). Finally, we combined the results of the three experiments to estimate the probability of plant recruitment across these experimental factors. Grazing decreased seed predation by half, did not modify seedling emergence and decreased seedling survival. Herbaceous vegetation did not affect seed predation nor seedling emergence rate, but increased seedling survival. Overall, the net effect of grazing on V. caven recruitment was neutral since the increase in seed availability due to the reduction in seed predation rate was compensated by the negative effect of grazing on seedling survival. Our analysis revealed that cattle grazing and herbaceous vegetation had contrasting effects on the seed and seedling life stages. We propose that in order to restrain the early stages of encroachment, cattle grazing pressure could be managed following the seasonality of demographic tree transitions. Through rotational grazing amongst paddocks, stocking rates could be relaxed during the primary dispersal stage to maximize granivory, and then increased to enhance the chance of seedling consumption and trampling.

Main Points

The study aimed to understand the contrasting effects of domestic grazing and herbaceous vegetation on *Vachellia caven* recruitment, integrating both competition-based and demographic models. The research was conducted in a Neotropical savanna in Entre Rios Province, Argentina, where cattle grazing is managed rotationally at a moderate stocking rate (~0.4 LU/ha).

Three experiments were performed using a split-plot design that manipulated two factors: cattle grazing (grazed vs. ungrazed exclosures) and herbaceous vegetation presence (vegetated vs. unvegetated cleared subplots). The experiments tracked different demographic stages: seed predation, seedling emergence, and seedling survival.

Key results demonstrated contrasting effects across life stages. Grazing significantly decreased seed predation by half, likely due to the reduction of herbaceous biomass serving as refuge for seed predators (granivores). However, grazing subsequently decreased seedling survival, probably due to trampling or direct consumption. Grazing had no measurable effect on seedling emergence. Conversely, herbaceous vegetation did not impact seed predation or emergence but significantly *increased* seedling survival. The authors note that this contradicts the assumption of widespread tree-grass competition and suggests the herbaceous layer might offer a protective effect (though the general hypothesis anticipated competition).

When combining these vital rates, the overall net effect of cattle grazing on *V. caven* recruitment probability was found to be neutral. This neutrality resulted from the complete compensation of the positive effect (increased seed availability due to reduced predation) by the negative effect (reduced survival in the seedling stage).

Conclusions

The analysis revealed that cattle grazing and herbaceous vegetation exert complex and contrasting effects on the early demographic stages of *V. caven*, influencing recruitment through stage-specific trade-offs rather than a single linear impact. Although the net effect of continuous grazing pressure was neutral, the authors emphasize that this masks critical mechanisms affecting seed survival versus seedling survival.

Crucially, the authors conclude by offering a prescriptive management strategy explicitly designed to **restrain** woody encroachment. They propose that grazing pressure could be strategically managed according to the seasonality of the tree's demographic transitions. To achieve maximum suppression of *V. caven* recruitment, stocking rates should be *reduced* during the primary dispersal stage to encourage granivory (maximizing seed consumption). Subsequently, stocking rates should be *increased* during the seedling stage to enhance negative impacts like seedling consumption and trampling. This active intervention strategy prioritizes the control of *V. caven* to reduce its density.

Key Quotes

  • "The role played by domestic grazing on woody encroachment is yet controversial."
  • "We evaluate the role of grazing and herbaceous vegetation on woody recruitment in a Neotropical savanna dominated by Vachellia caven, a successful and widely spread encroacher tree species."
  • "it has been reported that increases in tree cover and density can decrease plant species diversity, herbaceous biomass, forage and livestock production and economic yield."
  • "Grazing decreased seed predation by half, did not modify seedling emergence and decreased seedling survival."
  • "Herbaceous vegetation did not affect seed predation nor seedling emergence rate, but increased seedling survival."
  • "Overall, the net effect of grazing on V. caven recruitment was neutral since the increase in seed availability due to the reduction in seed predation rate was compensated by the negative effect of grazing on seedling survival."
  • "Vachellia caven is a native species, and one of the main encroachers in the region."
  • "We propose that in order to restrain the early stages of encroachment, cattle grazing pressure could be managed following the seasonality of demographic tree transitions."
  • "Through rotational grazing amongst paddocks, stocking rates could be relaxed during the primary dispersal stage to maximize granivory, and then increased to enhance the chance of seedling consumption and trampling."
Supports Ecological Role and Nurse EffectsConservation and Restoration StrategiesWoody Encroachment Dynamics

Ecosystem process interactions between central Chilean habitats

Meredith Root-Bernstein a,b,∗, Fabián M. Jaksic a,c (2015)

Abstract

Understanding ecosystem processes is vital for developing dynamic adaptive management of human-dominated landscapes. We focus on conservation and management of the central Chilean silvopastoral savanna habitat called ‘‘espinal’’, which often occurs near matorral, a shrub habitat. Although matorral, espinal and native sclerophyllous forest are linked successionally, they are not jointly managed and conserved. Management goals in ‘‘espinal’’ include increasing woody cover, particularly of the dominant tree Acacia caven, improving herbaceous forage quality, and increasing soil fertility. We asked whether adjacent matorral areas contribute to espinal ecosystem processes related to the three main espinal management goals. We examined input and outcome ecosystem processes related to these goals in matorral and espinal with and without shrub understory. We found that matorral had the largest sets of inputs to ecosystem processes, and espinal with shrub understory had the largest sets of outcomes. Moreover, we found that these outcomes were broadly in the directions preferred by management goals. This supports our prediction that matorral acts as an ecosystem process bank for espinal. We recommend that management plans for landscape resilience consider espinal and matorral as a single landscape cover class that should be maintained as a dynamic mosaic. Joint management of espinal and matorral could create new management and policy opportunities. © 2015 The Authors. Published by Elsevier B.V. This is an open access article under the CC BY-NC-ND license (http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-nd/4.0/).

Main Points

The study investigated ecosystem process interactions and flow between three successionally linked central Chilean habitats: matorral (dense shrub), espinal (Acacia caven savanna), and shrub espinal (espinal with shrub understory). The core goal was to determine if matorral acts as an 'ecosystem process bank' that subsidizes espinal, thereby contributing to key silvopastoral management goals: increasing *A. caven* woody cover, improving herbaceous forage, and increasing soil fertility.

The researchers adopted a non-spatially and non-temporally explicit approach, using a convex hull measure to incorporate the abundance and divergence (range) of functional traits and other ecological variables (like soil properties and disturbance factors). They collected input variables (those expected to move between habitats due to successional processes) and outcome variables (those characterizing habitat states related to management goals) across 160 plots in matorral, espinal, and shrub espinal.

The results supported the hypothesis of subsidization. Matorral exhibited the largest sets of inputs to ecosystem processes, validating its role as a potential source bank. Crucially, the shrub espinal habitat demonstrated the largest sets of positive outcomes, suggesting it is a high-functioning recipient of ecological flow. These outcomes were aligned with management objectives, contradicting the view that espinal is a degraded dead end or ecosystem process sink. This functional linkage suggests that espinal is indeed part of a viable successional pathway.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that successional habitat linkages, specifically between matorral and espinal, enhance overall landscape functioning, supporting the prediction that matorral acts as a functional ecosystem process bank for espinal. The positive outcomes observed in shrub espinal, linked to increased potential for woody reproduction and improved soil fertility, reinforce the idea that the *Acacia caven*-dominated habitat is a dynamic successional stage, not a degraded dead-end.

Based on these findings, the paper strongly recommends a radical shift in conservation and land management policy. Since the three habitats (matorral, espinal, and forest) are successional linked but currently managed separately—with matorral and espinal often treated as marginal or 'orphan' habitats—the authors propose they be considered a single landscape cover class. Maintaining them as a dynamic mosaic under joint management is viewed as essential for long-term landscape resilience. This unified approach is predicted to create new opportunities for policy implementation and conservation strategies in human-dominated landscapes, moving away from fragmented management based on perceived degradation states.

Key Quotes

  • "Although matorral, espinal and native sclerophyllous forest are linked successionally, they are not jointly managed and conserved."
  • "This supports our prediction that matorral acts as an ecosystem process bank for espinal."
  • "We recommend that management plans for landscape resilience consider espinal and matorral as a single landscape cover class that should be maintained as a dynamic mosaic."
  • "One possible explanation for these contrasting outcomes is that successionally linked mosaic patches may increase overall functioning within the landscape..."

  • "Espinal can serve as nurse plant habitats, making them important for regrowth of sclerophyllous forests in central Chile (Hernández et al., 2015; Fuentes-Castillo et al., 2012; pers. comm. C. Peña)."
  • "If the successional pathway in Fig. 2 is correct, then the greatest subsidization effect should be between matorral and espinal with shrub understory. By contrast, if espinal habitats are degraded dead ends, they should act as ecosystem process sinks."
  • "We found that matorral had the largest sets of inputs to ecosystem processes, and espinal with shrub understory had the largest sets of outcomes."

Dispersal syndromes of Vachellia caven: Dismantling introduction hypotheses and the role of man as a conceptual support for an archaeophyte in South America

Nicol´as Velasco, Ramiro Bustamante, Christian Smit (2023)

Abstract

Vachellia caven has a disjunct distribution at the southern cone of South America, occupying two major ranges: west of Andes (Central Chile) and east of them (mainly the South American Gran Chaco). For decades, the species has been subject to various ecological and natural history studies across its distribution, but questions concerning its origin in the western range remain unresolved. Thus far, it is unclear whether Vachellia caven was always a natural component of the Chilean forests, and "how" and "when" the species arrived in the country. In this study, we revised the dispersal syndromes of the species and contrast the two main hypotheses of dispersion to the west of Andes that have been proposed in the 90’s, namely animal versus human-mediated dispersal. For this, we reviewed all scientific literature on the species and explored the available information on morphology, genetics, fossil records and distribution patterns of closely related species. Here we illustrate how the collected evidence provides support for the human-mediated dispersal hypothesis, by including a conceptual synthesis that summarizes the outcomes of different dispersal scenarios. Lastly, and regarding the positive ecological effects this species has in the introduced area, we suggest reconsidering the (underappreciated) historical impacts of archaeophytes and rethinking the role that indigenous human tribes may have had in the dispersion of different plants in South America.

Main Points

The study conducts a comprehensive scoped review to investigate the origin and dispersal history of *Vachellia caven*, focusing specifically on its presence in the western range (Central Chile) versus its native eastern range (Gran Chaco). The primary methodological approach involved reviewing existing scientific literature—133 sources in total, compiled from pivotal articles and complementary searches across various topics related to historical patterns (phylogeny, fossil records, varietal morphology) and dispersal mechanisms (hydrochory, endozoochory).

The results strongly reject the vicariance hypothesis (splitting due to the Andes rising), favoring a historical long-distance dispersal event. This conclusion is based on molecular timing suggesting a relatively recent diversification (6 My BP), lack of natural fossil records in the western range before recent times, and the existence of six morphological varieties in the eastern range compared to only one (*var. caven*) in the western range. The review contrasts natural dispersal mechanisms like hydrochory and endozoochory (animal dispersal) against the human-mediated hypothesis for trans-Andean transport. While cattle are the predominant current dispersers in the west, hydrochory is deemed implausible for many Chilean hill-slope populations. Ultimately, the evidence gathered is presented to support the human-mediated dispersal hypothesis, suggesting that indigenous human tribes were responsible for bringing the species across the Andes.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that the presence of *V. caven* in the western range (Chile) is best explained by a long-distance dispersal scenario, rather than a vicariant process. The accumulated evidence—spanning phylogeny, fossil records, and morphological patterns—lends robust support to the human-mediated dispersal hypothesis, thereby classifying the species as an archaeophyte (an ancient introduction) in Chile.

Crucially, the study shifts focus from merely identifying the dispersal agent to advocating for a broader conceptual re-evaluation. Acknowledging the documented positive ecological effects of *V. caven* in the area where its status is debated (Chile), the authors propose that researchers must "reconsider the (underappreciated) historical impacts of archaeophytes" and fundamentally "rethink the role that indigenous human tribes may have had in the dispersion of different plants in South America." This conclusion challenges traditional native/non-native binaries, urging recognition of the complex historical interplay between human activity, plant distribution, and resulting ecological benefits.

  • "In present times, Vachellia caven seeds are predominantly dispersed by cattle, specially in the western range [18]."

  • "All in all, from the above, we can conclude that there is more empirical support for a historical long-distance dispersal scenario than for a vicariant process for V. caven."

LOS PASTIZALES DEL ESPINAL ENTRERRIANO, MANEJO Y PRODUCTIVIDAD

Ing. Agr. José Casermeiro* (1997)

Abstract

Para esta exposición he seleccionado lo más importante y que pueda ser de interés para ustedes. Pasaré algunas diapositivas que intentarán reflejar las características de los pastizales del espinal entrerriano. This presentation focuses on the management and productivity of the natural pastures of the Espinal Entrerriano, specifically addressing the challenges and characteristics observed in the northern region of Entre Ríos (Departments of Feliciano, Federal, La Paz, and parts of Paraná, Villaguay, Federación, Concordia). The goal is to highlight key features, such as the landscape types (closed forest, savanna, high elevation floodplains), the botanical composition, the impacts of poor grazing management, and the resulting low availability and quality of forage, aiming to inform sustainable practices for improved livestock production.

Main Points

The analysis is based on a descriptive conference presentation utilizing field observations (slides D1-D11) regarding the livestock-intensive northern region of Entre Ríos, focusing on the Espinal ecosystem.

Key landscape types observed include Monte Cerrado (closed forest), characterized by high tree density (ñandubay, algarrobo, espinillo) and a high presence of tender winter grasses, and Sabana (open forest), noted for its high productivity due to shade-loving (umbrófilas) species. The study also noted Bañados de Altura (high-elevation floodplains), heavy clay soils highly productive when managed through systems like Voisin or intensive rotation.

The text highlights the benefits of woody cover on soil parameters, noting that forest soil (monte) showed 7.84 ppm of phosphorus, compared to 4.80 ppm in areas cleared 26 years prior, demonstrating the role of trees in nutrient cycling (a key limiting factor in the province).

The primary 'result' of the existing land use practices is severe degradation. The author identifies the most serious issue as the lack of forage availability, directly attributing this to 'pastoreo irracional' (non-rational grazing), continuous systems, and excessive stocking rates (carga animal).

This poor management leads to 'sobrepastoreo' (overgrazing), resulting in bare soil, absence of protective litter (mantillo), and subsequent invasion by aggressive weeds and woody species (e.g., espinillos, chilcas, caraguatá). This process dramatically lowers the carrying capacity (receptividad ganadera) and causes delays in the recovery of vital winter and summer forage species. Furthermore, high stocking rates on clay soils during wet periods cause soil churning (amasado), destroying the herbaceous layer and impeding later recovery. The observed reality (Curve 'b') is a low, flattened forage production curve, far from the ideal potential (Curve 'a'), leading to low profitability and high risk for producers.

Conclusions

The author concludes that while the northern region of Entre Ríos possesses significant ecological potential, evidenced by its rich forage diversity (including desirable winter and summer perennial species) and favorable animal genetics, this potential remains largely unfulfilled due to fundamental failures in management.

The central conclusion emphasizes that the presence of high densities of shrubs and woody species, which severely impede management and forage productivity, is fundamentally a symptom of chronic land use malpractice, specifically high-load continuous grazing (sobrepastoreo). This degradation process leads to a severe lack of pasture availability and reduces the reproductive success of livestock, particularly the failure of seasonal breeding programs due to inadequate forage quality and quantity.

The conference implicitly urges a shift toward rational, rotational grazing systems to maximize the potential of the natural pasture year-round (preventing the 'bache forrajero'). Furthermore, the author stresses the need for holistic resource management that includes the native forest, recognizing its importance for diversity, nutrient cycling, and climate regulation, noting that the forest, like the pasture, is currently 'aged' and requires revitalization.

Key Quotes

  • "En orden de importancia los árboles más comunes son ñandubay, algarrobo, y espinillo."
  • "En un suelo con monte, como este, y otro desmontado ya hace 26 años, existe una diferencia de 3 ppm en fósforo (4,80 ppm en renoval y 7,84 ppm en monte) que es una de las limitantes en los suelos de nuestra provincia."
  • "El monte brinda protección a las vacas y ovejas evitando la pérdida de kg de carne y la muerte de la corderitos recién nacidos, están bien alimentados en invierno."
  • "Si comparamos con la diapositiva anterior esta diferencia no se nota demasiado porque cuando la densidad de árboles es demasiada alta la influencia de la copa o del polo de fertilidad que se produce debajo de ella no es tan clara."
  • "Esto es muy importante en cuanto a diversidad... [El bosque nativo] se favorece el manejo del rodeo y además mejora la productividad del pastizal."
  • "El potencial del norte de la Prov. de E.R. es muy importante pero estamos lejos del potencial, tenemos una alta riqueza forrajera, una excelente genética animal... [pero] Los productores están muy preocupados porque la rentabilidad de sus campos es muy baja."
  • "hay una alta densidad de arbustos que impiden cualquier tipo de manejo y productividad forrajera."
  • "En otros casos en situaciones de mayor deterioro predomina junto con el pastizal degradado, renuevos de espinillos, chilcas, caraguatá, etc. produciendo una superficie con baja disponibilidad de pasto, bajando abruptamente la receptividad ganadera."
  • "Este es el drama que tenemos presente entre nosotros, el sobrepastoreo que produce un alto porcentaje de malezas, de suelo desnudo, nada de mantillo generando un problema de sequía muy importante."
  • "Lo que hay que dejar en claro que el ganadero y los técnicos estamos preocupados por producir pasto y producir pasto de calidad, pero acá hay un bosque nativo de considerable importancia del cual se pueden sacar recursos y está envejecido, así como está envejecido el pasto también se envejece el monte."

Arbustización del Campo Natural: Un análisis de productores y técnicos

Ing. Agr. Gustavo Garibotto, Lic. RR. NN. Natalia Caballero, Ing. Agr. Marcelo Pereira Machín (N/A)

Abstract

La Mesa de Ganadería sobre Campo Natural Ampliada, sesionó por primera vez en Cuchilla de Laureles, departamento de Tacuarembó, reuniendo en un taller a productores y técnicos de la zona, quienes analizaron los problemas ocasionados por el fenómeno de competencia de especies arbustivas sobre el campo natural dedicado al pastoreo y las posibles opciones de solución.

Main Points

The study was conducted as a workshop (taller) organized by the Expanded Natural Field Livestock Board (Mesa de Ganadería sobre Campo Natural Ampliada) in Cuchilla de Laureles, Tacuarembó, involving 21 local producers and 20 technicians. The primary objective was to analyze the problem of woody encroachment (arbustización) and discuss potential solutions.

Producers reported an "incredible" increase in arbustized area, confirmed by comparisons between 1966 aerial photos and modern satellite imagery. This encroachment significantly reduces grazing surface and complicates livestock management. Ecologically, the encroachment is driven by competition between shrubs and natural pastures for key resources (water, light, nutrients). Critically, selective grazing by livestock, favoring pastures over woody species, indirectly increases the coverage and density of the shrubs.

Specific species identified in the succession include the pioneer species *Aloysia gratissima* (Cedrón del monte), followed by *Schinus longifolius* (Molle) and *Lithraea molleoides* (Aruera). Historical analysis suggested that the succession often initiated in old sheep bedding areas (dormideros de ovejas) from the late 1980s and early 1990s. The subsequent reduction in the ovine population, combined with high localized fertility in these spots, fostered the colonization by woody species.

Regarding control methods, the use of fire (quema) is a frequent local practice, although the workshop revealed that its primary goal is usually to tenderize pasture and eliminate old biomass, rather than effective shrub control. Producers testified that burning often exacerbates the situation by favoring the growth and regeneration of shrubs. This is ecologically explained by the increased fertility from ash deposits, the large and fire-resistant shrub seed bank, and the stimulation of germination by heat and smoke. Technicians unanimously advised against using fire, proposing alternatives such as controlled rotational grazing, strategic lanar/bovine ratios, chemical controls, and integrated mechanical/chemical methods.

Conclusions

The technical team and producers achieved strong consensus regarding the magnitude of the woody encroachment problem, identifying the same key species and causes. Technicians defined the problem as occurring when shrubs dominate the landscape, reduce grazing area, or are non-palatable.

While complex management strategies like dividing fields based on heterogeneity were suggested by technicians to allow for specialized technologies, these are often constrained by high costs and challenging geographical/topographical conditions in the zone.

Legal context significantly limits removal options, as the Director General Forestal (DGF) clarified that many species central to the encroachment process (*Schinus, Lithraea, Aloysia*) are considered native forest and protected by law (Law 15939/987), requiring authorization for clearance activities.

The overall conclusion highlighted the success of the technical-producer knowledge exchange but emphasized the critical need for research institutions to conduct location-specific studies to generate effective, pertinent, and sustainable management alternatives that address the area’s unique environmental and socio-economic particularities.

Key Quotes

  • "las especies arbustivas ejercen, en mayor o menor medida, algún grado de competencia con las pasturas naturales por los principales recursos (agua, luz y nutrientes)."
  • "A eso debe sumarse la selectividad del consumo del ganado en pastoreo con una marcada preferencia por las pasturas, lo que, en función de aspectos como la carga y su composición, indirectamente favorece el aumento en la cobertura y densidad de las especies arbustivas y sub-arbustivas..."
  • "El área arbustizada ha aumentado increíblemente, según los productores locales, cuyo recurso forrajero principal es el campo natural..."
  • "Este aumento en las especies arbustivas obedece a una sucesión que comienza con el Cedrón del monte - Garupá (Aloysia gratissima), la cual fue definida como una especie pionera..."
  • "Fue señalado que la sucesión que conduce a la arbustización, en muchas ocasiones comienza en los viejos dormideros de ovejas de fines de la década del ´80 y principios de los ´90..."
  • "Con la posterior reducción de la población ovina, probablemente la alta fertilidad generada en esos sitios, alentó la colonización por estas especies leñosas."
  • "Todo hace suponer que el uso de esta práctica [quema] persigue como objetivo fundamental el control de especies arbustivas y subarbustivas..."
  • "Algunos productores manifiestan haber observado que la práctica de la quema, en muchos casos, empeora la situación, favoreciendo el crecimiento y regeneración de las arbustivas..."
  • "Según algunos relatos y estudios, los arbustales, cuando se queman, vienen con más fuerza."

Persistent pods of the tree Acacia caven: a natural refuge for diverse insects including Bruchid beetles and the parasitoids Trichogrammatidae, Pteromalidae and Eulophidae.

D. Rojas-Rousse (2002)

Abstract

The persistent pods of the Leguminosae Acacia caven (Mol.) that do not fall from the tree provide opportunities for the appearance of a diverse group of insects the following season. Such pods collected during the spring of 1999 in Chile were indehiscent with highly sclerified pod walls. In contrast, persistent pods collected in Uruguay after a wet winter and spring (2002) were partially dehiscent, inducing the deterioration of the woody pods, and consequently exposing the seeds. These persistent pods are a natural refuge for insect species, namely two bruchid beetles (Pseudopachymeria spinipes, Stator furcatus), one scolytidae (Dendroctonus sp), lepidopterous larvae, ant colonies (Camponotus sp), one species of oophagous parasitoid (Uscana espinae goup senex), the gregarious larval-pupae parasitoid Monoksa dorsiplana (Pteromalidae) and two species of Horismenus spp.(Eulophidae). The patriline of M. dorsiplana is frequently formed by 1 son + 7 daughters.

Main Points

The study investigated the biodiversity of insects associated with *Acacia caven* persistent pods, comparing samples from Chile (1997, dry period, resulting in indehiscent pods) and Uruguay (2002, wet period, resulting in partially dehiscent pods). Pods were collected during spring (November) and monitored in a controlled environment (30°/20°C thermoperiod).

The results highlighted significant differences based on pod condition. Chilean pods were longer (mean 5.4 cm), indehiscent, and only yielded the specialized bruchid *Pseudopachymeria spinipes*. About 68% of these pods were attacked, but the emergence holes significantly underestimated the population size, suggesting most bruchids utilized the same initial exit hole. Uruguayan pods were shorter (mean 2.7 cm), partially dehiscent due to climatic deterioration, and supported a much more diverse entomofauna.

Uruguayan samples showed contamination by a second bruchid species, *Stator furcatus* (in 24% of pods), along with a scolytid (*Dendroctonus sp*), lepidopterous larvae, and ant colonies (*Camponotus sp*). The total bruchid attack in Uruguay was high, destroying 82% of the seeds. Notably, *S. furcatus* exhibited multi-adult development per seed (1 to 3 adults), unlike the specialized *P. spinipes* (one adult per seed).

Three groups of parasitoids were identified: the oophagous *Uscana espinae group senex*, and the gregarious larval-pupae parasitoids *Monoksa dorsiplana* (Pteromalidae) and *Horismenus spp.* (Eulophidae). The emergence of 810 parasitoids from 285 seeds confirmed their gregarious larval development. Laboratory studies on *M. dorsiplana* using a substitution host revealed that the most frequent patriline resulting from a single host attack was 1 male and 7 females.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that persistent pods of *A. caven* function as a crucial 'natural refuge' for a varied entomofauna, including seed predators (bruchids) and their natural enemies (parasitoids). The degree of pod dehiscence, influenced critically by climatic conditions (wet vs. dry winters), determines the specific insect guild able to colonize the seeds.

Indehiscent pods collected during dry conditions primarily restrict access to specialized bruchids like *P. spinipes*. Conversely, wet conditions lead to partially dehiscent and deteriorated pods, allowing secondary infiltration by generalized bruchids like *S. furcatus* and other insects. This deterioration facilitated massive seed destruction (82% attack rate) in the Uruguayan samples.

Significantly, the persistent pods, whether indehiscent or partially dehiscent, serve as a 'significant stock of adults' for bruchid beetles. These emerging adults are then able to infect the new, developing green pods in the following season. This mechanism provides a direct infestation pathway, bypassing the previously recognized necessity of seed transit through the digestive tract of mammals for seed exposure, thereby highlighting the ecological importance of persistent pods in sustaining both pest and beneficial insect populations.

Key Quotes

  • "A.caven is colloquially known as “espino” or “espinillo”, and is a prominent woody invader in over-grazed cow pastures or abandoned fields."
  • "The persistent pods of the Leguminosae Acacia caven (Mol.) that do not fall from the tree provide opportunities for the appearance of a diverse group of insects the following season."
  • "These persistent pods are a natural refuge for insect species..."
  • "It seems that the espinales are a recent phenomenon in the central valley of Chile, i.e. no more than 500 – 2000 years old, probably introduced from northern Argentina by guanacos, pack animals (llamas) in Trans-Andean caravans (Ovalle et al., 1990, Muñoz et al., 1993)."
  • "The ‘reversed’ phenological pattern exhibited by A. caven in Chile was probably a relictual development, which evolved in harmony with the climatic conditions prevailing in the subtropical, dry winter climate of the Chaco, prior to its migration to central Chile in Quaternary times..."
  • "However, as egg- laying behavior is inhibited by the presence of flowers on the host plant and is triggered by the presence of pods (Yates and Saïz, 1989), the females oviposit on the persistent pods of the previous season which have not fallen."
  • "As persistent pods of A.caven provide a refuge for the population of P. spinipes (Saiz, 1993), we investigated the presence of this species in the persistent pods, as well as the presence of other insects liable to compete with or to parasite P. spinipes."
  • "Such damaged pods allowed a diverse entomofauna to develop and/or to take refuge inside them."
  • "In Uruguay, the semi-dehiscent pods already attacked by P. spinipes showed that 24% were infiltrated by a second species of bruchid beetle, S. furcatus, presumably due to the deterioration of their woody walls."
  • "These two species, P. spinipes and S. furcatus, constitute a significant stock of adults able to infect legumes."

Rangeland Systems: Processes, Management and Challenges

David D. Briske (Editor) (2017)

Abstract

This book summarizes the current status of scientific and management knowledge regarding global rangelands and the major challenges that confront them. It originated from discussions to update the well-received book entitled Grazing Management: An Ecological Perspective that was first published in 1991. However, it became apparent that rangeland science had advanced so rapidly in the 25 years since this book had been published that its scope was no longer sufficient to accommodate the newly created knowledge. Consequently, it was decided that a new book with an expanded scope and greater relevance to contemporary rangeland challenges was required. This book also assesses why these major advances occurred so rapidly following a half century of limited conceptual change. Major advances have primarily been represented by scientific contributions such as nonequilibrium ecology and resilience theory, but socio-political events, including new policy and increasing societal demand for rangeland services, have also been important. Collectively, this book represents an attempt to achieve these broader and more contemporary objectives by emphasizing three major themes. The first summarizes recent conceptual advances for rangeland science and management. The second addresses the implications of these conceptual advances with respect to management recommendations and policy decisions. The third evaluates some of the major challenges confronting global rangelands in the twenty-first century. This book is intended to complement applied range management textbooks by evaluating the conceptual foundation of the profession and recommending changes to promote future development and greater effectiveness.

Main Points

The volume, *Rangeland Systems: Processes, Management and Challenges*, was developed to replace and expand upon the 1991 book *Grazing Management: An Ecological Perspective*. The need for this new work arose from the rapid conceptual advances in rangeland science over 25 years, particularly the adoption of theories like nonequilibrium ecology and resilience theory, alongside shifting socio-political contexts.

The book is structured around three major themes corresponding to three primary sections. The first section, **Processes**, focuses on recent conceptual advances in ecological knowledge concerning the function and dynamics of rangeland systems. Specific chapter topics include **woody plant encroachment**, ecohydrology, soil processes, structural heterogeneity, nonequilibrium ecology and resilience theory, and climate change consequences.

The second section, **Management**, details advances stemming from this new ecological knowledge, adopting a broad scope that considers landscape and regional scales, not just smaller 'pasture' scales. This section features chapters on rangelands as **social-ecological systems**, state-and-transition models, livestock production systems, adaptive management, and wildlife–livestock interactions.

The final section, **Challenges**, addresses high-profile, emerging issues impacting the provision of ecosystem services. These include **invasive plant species** and novel systems, ecosystem services and human demand, climate change adaptation, monitoring protocols, and rangeland systems in developing nations. The overarching methodological goal is to consolidate these diverse and complex concepts into a readily accessible volume to inform professionals, stakeholders, and policy makers, moving the field beyond traditional, narrowly framed approaches.

Conclusions

The core conclusion of the volume is that a more comprehensive conceptual framework is mandatory for effectively addressing the complex, contemporary challenges confronting global rangelands in the twenty-first century. Traditional range management methods have proven insufficient, primarily due to intensifying global pressures such as rapid human population growth, climate change, landscape fragmentation, and biodiversity loss.

The editor argues that previous approaches frequently failed because they either ignored complex problems or incorrectly reduced them to 'simple' problems addressable only through narrowly framed solutions. To overcome this failure, the book advocates for a fundamental shift: rangelands must be re-envisioned as integrated **social-ecological systems**.

This framework requires equal consideration of societal values, goals, and capacities alongside established ecological processes. By adopting this comprehensive approach, management agencies, educational institutions, research programs, and policy-making bodies can develop more effective strategies to assess the intricate nature of contemporary rangeland issues and develop appropriate, sustainable solutions.

  • "The second addresses the implications of these conceptual advances with respect to management recommendations and policy decisions."
  • "An ecological processes section includes six chapters highlighting major advances in ecological knowledge and theory regarding the function and dynamics of rangeland systems."
  • "Specific chapters emphasize woody plant encroachment, ecohydrology, soils and belowground processes, structural heterogeneity, nonequilibrium ecology and resilience theory, and potential consequences of climate change."
  • "Management is used in a broad context that references landscape and regional scales, in addition to smaller “pasture” scales, to optimize land use decisions for both landowners and society at large."
  • "A challenges section contains five chapters that focus on emerging, high profile issues that will substantially impact the ability of rangelands to continue to provide ecosystem services to human societies."
  • "Rapid human population growth, climate change, land tenure modification, landscape fragmentation, food security, biodiversity loss, and globalized markets represent some of the major challenges that have minimized the effectiveness of traditional range management."
  • "Many “complex” problems have gone unrecognized or have been inappropriately identified as “simple” problems that have been addressed with narrowly framed approaches."
  • "The complexity of contemporary challenges requires that rangelands be re-envisioned as integrated social-ecological systems, in which societal values, goals, and capacities are given equal consideration to those of ecological processes."
  • "This book is intended to complement applied range management textbooks by evaluating the conceptual foundation of the profession and recommending changes to promote future development and greater effectiveness."

La cobertura arbórea de Acacia caven sobre la calidad de la pradera y microclima en un sistema silvopastoril de Chile central

Alejandro Lucero Ignamarca, Fernando Muñoz Sáez, Jorge Cancino Cancino, Alvaro Sotomayor Garretón, Francis Dube, Katia Sáez Carrillo, Arnoldo Villarroel Muñoz y Mauricio Navarrete Torres (2019)

Abstract

El presente estudio tiene como objetivo determinar el efecto de diferentes coberturas arbóreas sobre productividad y calidad de la pradera y sobre variables microclimáticas, en un sistema silvopastoril con espino (Acacia caven). Para ello se estableció un ensayo con cuatro tratamientos de cobertura arbórea (0%, 30% - 40%, 50% - 60%, > 70%), en un diseño experimental de bloques aleatorizados completos y tres repeticiones. Además de variables relacionadas con la productividad y calidad de la pradera, mediante sensores se realizó la medición permanente de temperatura y humedad de suelo, temperatura ambiente y humedad relativa. El periodo de análisis incluye dos temporadas de crecimiento de la pradera. Para los análisis estadísticos se consideraron tres modelos; i) la cobertura arbórea sobre la productividad de la pradera; ii) la cobertura arbórea sobre las variables microclimáticas y iii) la cobertura arbórea sobre la productividad pratense, considerando las variables microclimáticas como covariables. Los resultados indican que, en la segunda temporada, la mayor productividad se alcanzó con el tratamiento sin presencia de árboles (0%). No se detectó efecto de la cobertura arbórea sobre la calidad de la pradera, ni sobre variables microclimáticas, para los periodos evaluados. Las variables microclimáticas que mejor explicaron el desarrollo de la pradera fueron la humedad relativa mínima y la temperatura ambiente. Transcurridas dos temporadas de crecimiento, no es factible dilucidar aún cuál tratamiento genera las mejores condiciones para la productividad de la pradera, por lo que se hace necesario continuar con las mediciones.

Main Points

## Methods
The study was conducted in a silvopastoral system utilizing *Acacia caven* (espino) in Cauquenes, Maule Region, central Chile, across 2.4 hectares of adult espinal. The experiment utilized a randomized complete block design with three repetitions, testing four distinct tree cover treatments: 0% (total removal), 30% - 40%, 50% - 60%, and > 70%. The tree cover percentages were established via selective cutting of trees or stumps. Data collection spanned two prairie growth seasons (September–December 2014 and September 2015–February 2016).

Variables measured included pasture productivity (PP), determined as total dry weight per hectare derived from samples harvested from 0.5 m² exclusion cages, and pastoral value (VP), calculated based on botanical composition and specific index values (IE). Microclimatic variables—soil temperature and moisture at 15 cm depth, ambient temperature, and relative humidity at 1.3 meters—were continuously recorded using sensors. Statistical analyses involved three models: assessing the effect of tree cover on 1) pasture productivity, 2) microclimatic variables, and 3) pasture productivity using microclimatic variables as covariates.

## Results
The results, after analyzing two growing seasons, indicated that the highest pasture productivity was achieved in the treatment with no trees (0% cover) during the second season. This finding contrasts with historical regional studies cited in the introduction, which generally show increased productivity under canopy projection. Importantly, the study found no significant effect of tree cover on either the quality of the pasture (Pastoral Value) or the measured microclimatic variables across the evaluated periods. The microclimatic factors that showed the strongest correlation with pasture development were minimum relative humidity and ambient temperature.

Conclusions

The primary conclusion is that after only two growing seasons, the data are inconclusive regarding which tree cover treatment (including total tree removal) generates the best overall conditions for long-term pasture productivity and sustainability in this silvopastoral system. While the short-term results favored the 0% tree cover treatment for productivity in the second season, this was not robust enough to suggest an optimal management strategy.

The authors highlight that, contrary to expectations based on previous literature, the different tree cover levels did not significantly impact either the quality of the pasture or the measured microclimatic variables within the two-year period. This suggests that the management impacts on the microenvironment require a longer time frame to become detectable. Consequently, the authors strongly recommend continuing the measurement process to gather more stable and long-term data necessary for developing sustainable management protocols for this crucial resource.

Monografía de ESPINO Acacia caven (Mol.) Mol

Susana Benedetti R. (Editora) (2012)

Abstract

The full abstract is not provided in the text snippet. The introduction states that the monograph, published by the Instituto Forestal, aims to provide background information on Acacia caven in the context of Non-Timber Forest Products (PFNM) to encourage sustainable exploration and conservation of this resource.

Main Points

This monograph excerpt details the taxonomy, botanical characteristics, distribution, ecological associations, and environmental requirements of *Acacia caven* (Espino), focusing heavily on the context of Chile.

* **Distribution and Characteristics:** *A. caven* is native to arid/semiarid South America (including Chile, Argentina, Bolivia, Paraguay, Uruguay, and Brazil). In Chile, it spans approximately 1,500 km (27º to 38º S), occupying vast areas (around 3.8 million hectares) of the central semi-arid zone. The species is characterized as a thorny tree or shrub, highly adapted to prolonged drought and poor, eroded soils, possessing a strong, extended root system suitable for erosion control.

* **Ecological Formation and Degradation:** *A. caven* is the dominant species in the 'Espinal' (or *estepa de espino*), a characteristic formation of the Sclerophyllous Forest type. Critically, the text emphasizes that the Espinal is largely a **secondary formation** and a result of human intervention. According to various cited authors, the Espinal represents a degradation of a more evolved climax community (*Lithraea caustica* or *Quillaja saponaria*) or is a direct consequence of chronic human intervention, including wood harvesting and overgrazing.

* **Human Impact:** The species is under intense exploitation. Historically dense xerophytic forests in the Central Valley have been severely reduced due to 'tala constante y permanente' (constant and permanent logging) for firewood and charcoal, and large areas have been cleared for agriculture (especially vineyards and olive groves) in the Valparaíso and Libertador Bernardo O’Higgins regions, leading to the current prevalence of multi-stemmed, shrub-like individuals rather than full trees.

Conclusions

Although a formal conclusion section is not present in the excerpt, the text strongly implies that *Acacia caven* is a resilient, foundational species crucial for ecological stability in arid Chilean environments, specifically due to its tolerance of dry, eroded soils and its potent root system for ground protection.

However, its formations (the Espinales) are widely recognized as degraded ecosystems, having replaced older, more developed climax forests due to centuries of intensive, unsustainable exploitation (firewood, charcoal production, and agricultural conversion). The overall narrative highlights the urgent need for conservation and sustainable management, underscored by the inclusion of a comprehensive *Marco Normativo* (Regulatory Framework) section, to prevent the continued decline of this ecologically significant species, particularly in regions facing rapid land conversion.

Neutral Conservation and Restoration StrategiesPampas Ecosystem Degradation and ResilienceGrazing and Land Use Impacts

Proposta de guia para a restauração de campos nativos no sul do Brasil

Ernestino de Souza Gomes Guarino, Ana Boeira Porto, Pedro Augusto Thomas, Sandra Cristina Müller, Leonardo Marques Urruth, Davi Chemello, Carlos Nabinger, Danilo Menezes Sant’Anna, Eduardo Vélez Martin, Gerhard Ernst Overbeck, Gabriela Coelho-de-Souza (2023)

Abstract

O guia técnico para restauração de campos nativos integra as ações de pesquisa e extensão conjuntas entre os projetos “Nexo Pampa” (Embrapa Clima Temperado) e o projeto “PANexus” (UFRGS). Ambos os projetos buscaram formas de promover as seguranças hídrica, energética e alimentar por meio da conservação pelo uso e pelos processos de restauração ecológica dos Campos Sulinos do Bioma Pampa e da Mata Atlântica. A elaboração deste guia, fruto de um trabalho conjunto entre pesquisadores, estudantes, gestores e extensionistas, visa suprir uma demanda latente por materiais direcionados ao fomento de ações práticas de restauração no Sul do Brasil. Assim, o objetivo desta publicação é apresentar de forma simplificada um guia técnico para restauração ecológica dos Campos Sulinos, descrevendo de forma sequencial as etapas e os respectivos processos técnicos da restauração, permitindo que diferentes públicos envolvidos com o manejo e a restauração de Campos Nativos tomem decisões para a adequação ambiental de Áreas de Preservação Permanente (APP), Reserva Legal (RL) e Áreas de Uso Restrito (AUR) nos Campos Sulinos.

Main Points

The methodology employed for constructing this technical guide was participatory, involving an expert workshop held in October 2019, organized by the Nexo Pampa and PANexus projects. The workshop gathered 10 participants (researchers, specialists, students, and managers) from key regional institutions (UFRGS, Embrapa, Sema/RS, ICMBio partners) who used a semi-structured questionnaire and discussion to define the sequential stages and processes for native grassland restoration. The resulting preliminary guide was then evaluated individually by invited experts and tested as an orientation protocol by the Secretariat of the Environment and Infrastructure of Rio Grande do Sul (Sema/RS) before final adjustments.

The guide outlines a four-stage process for ecological restoration focusing exclusively on the biotic component of Campos Sulinos ecosystems, excluding cases of extreme soil degradation (like post-mining).

**Stage 1: Local Diagnosis.** This crucial step involves evaluating the area's resilience (its capacity for natural regeneration), historical land use (e.g., intense agriculture vs. overgrazing vs. silviculture), and landscape context (presence of native remnants nearby). Higher use intensity and duration correspond to lower resilience, specifically due to the reduction of critical underground structures (rhizomes) and native seed banks. The diagnosis determines the necessary degree of active intervention.

**Stage 2: Establishment of Grassland Vegetative Cover.** Based on the diagnosis, strategies are chosen. For low-resilience areas (often following intensive agriculture), active introduction of native herbaceous species is necessary. Soil preparation is generally discouraged if the soil structure is intact (e.g., previously managed with no-till farming), as it promotes the entry of undesirable species. Fertilization is also advised against. When seeding, native species are preferred; if unavailable, non-invasive exotic forage species (like black oat or ryegrass) may be used temporarily. Critically, known invasive species like *Eragrostis plana* (capim-annoni) must be avoided, and targeted herbicide application or mowing may be required to control invasives.

Conclusions

The Campos Nativos (Campos Sulinos), spanning the Pampa and Mata Atlântica biomes, are ecologically significant but face accelerating degradation and conversion, highlighted by the Pampa biome's loss of 21.4% of its native vegetation between 1985 and 2020. Major drivers include conversion to silviculture (pínus, eucalyptus, *acácia-negra*) and intensive agriculture (soy, corn, rice).

Since established restoration protocols for these ecosystems are currently lacking, there is a critical need for innovative and context-specific actions that consider the ecological, cultural, and landscape characteristics of the grasslands. This guide serves as a necessary technical instrument to simplify the sequential steps of ecological restoration.

The overarching goal of the restoration framework is to reestablish an ecosystem close to its non-degraded state, recovering characteristic species composition, biodiversity, and importantly, restoring its potential for sustainable use, such as native grassland livestock farming. By providing this consensus-based framework, the authors aim to support various stakeholders—from producers and extensionists to environmental managers—in making informed decisions regarding environmental adequacy in protected and restricted use areas.

O Parque Estadual do Espinilho além de seus limites: integração de aspectos ambientais, sociais e econômicos

Jan Karel Felix Mahler Junior (2016)

Abstract

Durante a 10ª Conferência das Partes da Convenção sobre Diversidade Biológica (COP-10), realizada em 2010, na cidade de Nagoya, Província de Aichi, Japão, foi aprovado um Plano Estratégico com 20 metas, conhecidas como Metas de Aichi. Este plano estabelece ações para diminuição da perda da biodiversidade no mundo, fazendo com que os 193 países signatários, incluindo o Brasil, se comprometam a trabalhar juntos para implementar as 20 metas propostas até 2020. A Meta 11 desse Plano Estratégico estabelece que, até 2020, pelo menos 17% de áreas terrestres e de águas continentais e 10% de áreas marinhas e costeiras terão que ser conservadas por meio de sistemas de áreas protegidas. Estas áreas têm que ser ecologicamente representativas, satisfatoriamente interligadas e integradas em paisagens terrestres e marinhas mais amplas. Outras medidas de conservação complementares devem ser também planejadas. Para alcançar as metas da COP-10, terão que ser revistas muitas de nossas ações e construídas políticas públicas eficientes dos pontos de vista ambiental, social e econômico. Muitos modelos atuais de desenvolvimento econômico não respeitam a capacidade de renovação dos sistemas ecológicos, intensificando as alterações ambientais (UNDP, 1992) e contribuindo para a redução da qualidade de vida das sociedades humanas (WCED, 1987). Entre as principais consequências do desenvolvimento de atividades humanas destacam-se a perda e fragmentação de áreas naturais, estando estas entre as principais ameaças à biodiversidade em todo o mundo. Provocam declínio da diversidade biológica, alterações nas comunidades biológicas e empobrecimento de interações ecológicas (Primack & Rodrigues, 2001; Cerqueira et al., 2003; Fahrig, 2003; Watling & Donnelly, 2006). A criação e implantação de unidades de conservação (UCs) tem sido uma das ferramentas mais efetivas de conservação da biodiversidade (Bensusan, 2006). Os objetivos das UCs vão desde a proteção integral da biodiversidade e de ecossistemas até o manejo sustentável de recursos naturais específicos, sendo importantes também as possibilidades de turismo em contato com a natureza, educação e sensibilização ambiental e realização de pesquisas científicas (Milano, 1997a,b). Entretanto, as UCs brasileiras ainda estão longe de cumprirem seus objetivos de forma eficiente e, em seu conjunto, não estão integradas como sistema. Entre os principais problemas estão a falta de regularização fundiária de suas áreas, falta de pessoal capacitado, situações institucionais ainda precárias, processos distorcidos de elaboração e implementação de planos de manejo, além da pequena cobertura territorial e falta de conectividade entre áreas similares (Milano, 1997a). Nesse sentido, é muito importante que as áreas protegidas estejam integradas a diferentes estratégias de manejo, incluindo o fortalecimento das zonas de amortecimento das UCs e implantação de corredores. Para que as zonas de amortecimento cumpram efetivamente suas funções, as atividades realizadas em seu interior devem aliar preservação ambiental, questões sociais e atividades econômicas (Morsello, 2001), interligando-se ao desenvolvimento de sua região, tanto para obterem reconhecimento público como para possibilitar a construção de alianças em seu favor (IUCN, 1993). O Projeto Conservação da Biodiversidade como Fator de Contribuição ao Desenvolvimento do Estado do Rio Grande do Sul/Brasil (Projeto RS Biodiversidade) tem como objetivos a conservação da biodiversidade e a integração do setor produtivo com as ações de conservação e recuperação em áreas prioritárias, garantindo a função, a dinâmica e a evolução dos ecossistemas naturais. O Projeto deve fomentar, também, a conscientização sobre a biodiversidade e o desenvolvimento de instrumentos de gestão para o manejo eficiente e sustentável dos recursos naturais. Até o momento, o Rio Grande do Sul conta com 34 UCs federais e estaduais. Destas, 27 são de proteção integral (onde não é permitida a exploração direta dos recursos) e sete de uso sustentável (onde é possível o uso racional dos recursos). As UCs federais e estaduais totalizam uma área aproximada de 721.666 ha, o que representa somente 2,56% de toda a superfície do Estado. O Parque Estadual do Espinilho (PEEsp), com uma área de 1.617 ha, é a única UC no Brasil que protege a vegetação Savana tipo Parque, que no país está restrita ao extremo oeste do Rio Grande do Sul. Por sua importância e singularidade, a área da Formação Parque Espinilho é considerada pelo MMA (2007) como de extrema importância biológica e prioritária para a conservação de muitas espécies de flora e fauna de importância regional, nacional e global. Destaca-se, também, pela ocorrência de campos naturais, com uma vasta variedade de gramíneas ameaçadas pela ação antrópica local. Nesse contexto, a integração entre conservação e os distintos usos realizados nas propriedades particulares da região são de grande relevância. Para atuar na região, no conjunto do Projeto RS Biodiversidade, foram planejadas propostas de ação visando à “Restauração e Conservação dos Ecossistemas do Entorno do Parque Estadual do Espinilho”, com o intuito de sugerir novas formas de desenvolvimento que viabilizem a conservação de espécies ameaçadas. Ao mesmo tempo, devem ser consideradas as especificidades locais históricas, sociais e culturais, de forma a possibilitar o desenvolvimento econômico e a sustentabilidade de ambientes naturais a partir da percepção e valorização da biodiversidade e do ecossistema.

Main Points

### Methodology

The methodology employed a participatory planning approach focused primarily on the Buffer Zone (Zona de Amortecimento) of the Espinilho State Park (PEEsp). To identify environmental problems, threats, and opportunities, the authors conducted research and facilitated workshops that consulted stakeholders from 12 institutions, including municipal, state, and federal public bodies, research institutions, NGOs (Brazilian and Uruguayan), class entities, and public school teachers. Rural landowners directly within the Buffer Zone were also sought as potential partners.

A key workshop held in August 2013 in Barra do Quaraí solidified technical proposals and identified actors for implementation. The participants were divided into four groups, each focusing on a specific goal: (1) resolution of contamination and environmental degradation, (2) improved territorial planning for conservation, (3) implementation of research, education, and training, and (4) diversification of activities and production methods on local properties. The strategies were designed to be easily assimilated and sustainable, ensuring their continuity after the conclusion of the RS Biodiversity Project.

### Results

The discussions yielded four sets of crucial problems/goals and six subsequent strategic actions. Key problems identified included inadequate municipal waste disposal, illegal entry and application drift of agrochemicals (linked to extensive rice farming), poaching/abigeato, and roaming domestic animals. Territorial planning needed improvement, specifically the creation of biological corridors, full PEEsp land regularization, and the planned ordering of Legal Reserves (RLs) on private land. Research was deemed necessary to monitor exotic species (like *capim-annoni*, cervo-axis, and wild boar) and to assess the impact of the BR-472 highway on local fauna.

The resulting strategic actions defined were:

1. **Establishing Connectivity:** Utilizing Legal Reserves (RLs) and ensuring compliance with environmental laws (like CAR registration) to establish vegetation fragments and biological corridors linking the PEEsp to external, important conservation areas.
2. **Economic Diversification:** Creating and commercializing regional products, especially crafts, to diversify income associated with environmental conservation.
3. **Ecotourism Development:** Expanding and organizing bird watching tourism, capitalizing on the area’s singular species diversity.
4. **Addressing Rice Farming Impacts:** Combating the irregular entry of agrochemicals from Uruguay, strictly enforcing rules on aerial application, and identifying production systems more compatible with the environment.
5. **Strengthening Park Management:** Concluding expropriation payments to fully regularize PEEsp’s land area and establishing a Consultative Council to involve diverse sectors of society in park management.
6. **Improving Visitation Infrastructure:** Structuring the park for visitors, emphasizing that UCs can act as factors of development if planned as integrated economic potentials.

Conclusions

The analysis underscores that the conservation of the Espinilho State Park (PEEsp) and its surrounding ecosystems, representing the unique and nationally restricted Savana Park vegetation (dominated by Algarrobo/Espinilho), requires integrated planning that transcends the park boundaries, combining environmental protection with local social and economic realities.

A central conclusion is the necessity to overcome the socio-economic perception that conservation is detrimental to regional growth. The authors argue that Conservation Units (UCs), even those of integral protection, can function as catalysts for development—especially through regulated tourism and related economic activities—if properly planned and linked to local sectors. This aligns with the thesis that socio-economic bias drives negative perceptions of native ecosystems.

To achieve sustainability, the defined strategies focus on institutional strengthening (completing land payments and creating participatory councils), establishing essential ecological connectivity through biological corridors on private land via Legal Reserve management, and mitigating severe anthropogenic threats. Major threats include contamination from the intensive rice farming sector and the spread of invasive exotic species. Ultimately, the success of the conservation efforts hinges on fostering a regional perception that values and integrates the unique biodiversity of the Espinilho formation as an asset for sustainable development.

Plano de Manejo do Parque Estadual do Espinilho

Alexandre Gomes da Silva; Ivanor Antonio Sinigaglia; Paola Prates Stumpf; GEOPROSPEC (2009)

Abstract

This document is the Management Plan (Plano de Manejo) for the Parque Estadual do Espinilho (Espinilho State Park), published by the State Government of Rio Grande do Sul (SEMA/DEFAP) in October 2009. As a planning document rather than a traditional academic paper, it does not contain a formal abstract. Its primary purpose is to outline the legal, physical, biotic, socioeconomic, and managerial context necessary for the implementation and sustainable operation of the Conservation Unit (UC). The plan details the rationale for creating the park within the Pampa biome, focusing on the protection of the unique Savanna (Espinilho) formation. It includes comprehensive characterizations of the regional context, geophysics, climate, flora, and fauna, culminating in the definition of a detailed Zoning scheme (Zona Intangível, Zona Primitiva, Zona de Uso Extensivo, etc.) and specific Management Programs (Protection, Community Integration, Research, and Monitoring) necessary to achieve conservation objectives.

Main Points

The document, structured as a comprehensive Management Plan, focuses on several key areas necessary for the implementation of the Parque Estadual do Espinilho (PESP) in Barra do Quaraí, Rio Grande do Sul.

### Contextual and Legal Bases
The plan begins by establishing the legal foundation for conservation in the Pampa Biome, referencing the Brazilian National System of Conservation Units (SNUC) and the State System (SEUC). It details the geopolitical, demographic, and economic context of the surrounding region, emphasizing the challenges related to land use (Agropecuária and Comércio) and socioeconomic indicators (IDESE). The rationale (Justificativas) for establishing the park is provided, underscoring the critical need to conserve the characteristic ecosystem of the Espinilho formation.

### Environmental Characterization
Sections 4 through 9 provide a detailed characterization of the physical and biotic environment. This includes analyses of regional and local geomorphology, geology (mentioning the Paraná Basin, Botucatu, and Serra Geral formations), hydrogeology (Aquífero Guarani), pedology (Chernossolos, Vertissolos), and climate (Precipitation and Temperature data). Crucially, the biotic sections detail the **FLORA DO PARQUE** (Section 8), specifically classifying the dominant vegetation as **Formação de Savana** (8.1) and **Floresta Estacional Semidecidual Submontana** (Mata Ciliar, 8.2). The Savanna formation is noted as being typically dominated by *Prosopis affinis* (inhanduvaí) and *Prosopis nigra* (algarrobo), alongside *Acacia caven* (espinilho).

### Degradation and Management Implications
A significant visual finding (Figure 31) documents the historical land management practice of **actively removing** woody vegetation, showing a concrete roller used to suppress *Prosopis* and *Acacia caven* trees. This highlights the historical suppression of the woody component in favor of cattle pasture. Subsequent sections establish the **Zoneamento** (Zoning, Section 10) of the park into areas requiring different levels of protection (e.g., Zona Intangível, Zona Primitiva, Zona de Recuperação [ZR]), and mandate specific **Programas de Manejo** (Section 11) for protection, community engagement, and scientific research and monitoring. The inclusion of **Zona de Recuperação** (ZR) suggests areas impacted by past anthropogenic activities (such as abandoned rice paddies mentioned in Figure 37) need active restoration.

Conclusions

As a management plan, the document's conclusions are prescriptive, focusing on the actions required to ensure the long-term conservation and appropriate use of the Parque Estadual do Espinilho, thereby validating the reasons for its creation.

### Institutional and Operational Mandates
The plan mandates specific investment requirements for infrastructure (Section 12), defining the necessary staffing levels, organizational structure, and required equipment for effective park management. It details the necessity of demarcating the park limits, identifying critical points, establishing vigilance bases, and implementing fire prevention controls under the **Programa de Proteção**.

### Socio-Environmental Integration and Research
Crucial programs are outlined for fostering positive relationships with the local community (Programa de Integração com a Comunidade), including environmental awareness campaigns and the establishment of a consultative council. Furthermore, the **Programa de Pesquisa, Monitoramento e Manejo** emphasizes the need for ongoing scientific study of the ecosystem, including systematic monitoring of biodiversity (amphibians, reptiles, birds, mammals) and environmental parameters. The final zoning scheme ensures that areas critical for the **Savana formation** and other habitats receive maximum protection (Zona Intangível and Primitiva), while allowing for limited, controlled use (Zona de Uso Extensivo and Intensivo) compatible with the park's conservation goals. The presence of the *Acacia caven* formation (Espinilho) is the defining element of the park's conservation value, directly contradicting the narrative that the species is solely a weed or encroacher, and instead positioning it as fundamental to the endemic ecosystem.
