A VEGETAÇÃO NO PARQUE ESTADUAL DO ESPINILHO. 1- HISTÓRICO DA OCUPAÇÃO HUMANA E EVOLUÇÃO DO CONHECIMENTO BOTÂNICO E FITOGEOGRÁFICO SOBRE O PONTAL DO QUARAÍ.

JOSÉ NEWTON CARDOSO MARCHIORI, FABIANO DA SILVA ALVES, LEONARDO PAZ DEBLE, ANABELA SILVEIRA DE OLIVEIRA DEBLE (2013)

Abstract

A história da ocupação humana no Pontal do Quaraí, extremo sudoeste do Rio Grande do Sul, e da evolução do conhecimento botânico e fitogeográfico sobre a vegetação do Parque Estadual do Espinilho, são presentemente investigados, com vistas a contribuir ao embasamento científico regional. [The vegetation in the Espinilho State Park, Brazil. 1 - History of human occupation and evolution of botanical and phytogeographical know ledge about the area close to the mouth of Quaraí river]. The history of human occupation in the area close to the mouth of Quaraí river (Rio Grande do Sul State, Brazil) and the development of botanical and phytogeographical knowledge about the Espinilho State Park (Rio Grande do Sul State, Brazil) are investigated, in order to contribute to regional science.

Main Points

The study aims to provide scientific grounding by investigating the history of human occupation and the evolution of botanical and phytogeographical knowledge concerning the Espinilho State Park (Pontal do Quaraí, Rio Grande do Sul, Brazil).

The regional history begins with Indigenous Pampeanos (Charruas, Minuanos) followed by Jesuit settlements in the 17th century, culminating in the vast Estância de Japeju (1660), which introduced large numbers of cattle. The region later came under Portuguese/Brazilian control (early 1800s) and experienced a period where the cattle population multiplied freely after the Jesuits were expelled.

The authors delineate two main economic cycles impacting the region. The first, focused on agro-pastoralism and the charque (dried meat) industry, peaked around 1887 with the establishment of the "Saladeiro Barra do Quaraí." This cycle relied heavily on native fields and natural parks for grazing, and its environmental impact was deemed "relatively small" compared to the subsequent period.

The first cycle declined in the 1930s due to the rise of frigoríficos and protective federal legislation (Lei Federal de Desnacionalização do Charque, 1927). It was immediately followed by a second, highly transformative economic cycle: irrigated rice agriculture. Beginning around 1937, this activity expanded rapidly, driven by favorable conditions (flat lands, abundant water from the Uruguai and Quaraí rivers, and accessible transportation). This expansion caused the "drastic modification" of the landscape and the "suppression of great part of the native fields and arboreal vegetation of the natural parks."

Crucially, the authors note that the preservation of this unique natural heritage, specifically the *inhanduvá* (Nandubaysal) and *algarrobo* (Algarrobal) parks, was only secured through legal protections (Decree 42.099/2002 for *inhanduvá*) and the creation of the Parque Estadual do Espinilho (1975).

Conclusions

The study establishes that the Pontal do Quaraí region has undergone profound historical changes linked directly to land use practices. The initial centuries of extensive cattle ranching, while significant, exerted a comparatively low environmental pressure on the native fields and arboreal vegetation, as these were integral to the pastoral economy.

The true and drastic environmental modification occurred with the transition to intensive monoculture—specifically irrigated rice farming—starting in the late 1930s. This agricultural expansion led to the widespread suppression of the native *campos* and the arboreal 'natural parks,' which represent a unique ecological heritage of Rio Grande do Sul.

The final conclusion is an implicit plea for the recognition and protection of this unique ecosystem. The authors state that without the legal creation of the Parque Estadual do Espinilho and the protection of species like *inhanduvá*, the state would likely have lost the Nandubaysal and Algarrobal parks, reinforcing the view that modern, intensive land conversion poses the existential threat to these native woody formations, rather than natural ecological processes or species invasiveness.

  • "Sob o ponto de vista ambiental, o período que findava teve impacto relativamente pequeno sobre a paisagem e biota, comparado ao que estava por vir, uma vez que dependia da vegetação nativa dos campos e dos parques naturais para a atividade pastoril."
  • "Em poucas décadas, tanto a paisagem do Pontal do Quaraí como das planícies aluviais do município de Uruguaiana foram drasticamente modificadas pela supressão de grande parte dos campos nativos e da vegetação arbórea dos parques naturais, em decorrência da expansão da lavoura arrozeira."

Misaligned Narrative of the Ecological Role of Vachellia caven in Eastern South American Savannas

N/A (N/A)

Abstract

Vachellia caven is a thorny leguminous shrub to treelet, widely distributed throughout the Neotropics and spanning across the Southern Cone from Bolivia, Paraguay and Brazil to Tierra del Fuego in Argentina and Chile. Most of the contemporary research focuses on the western range of the species distribution, in Chile, though this review emphasizes the species' long-term and widespread ecological importance in the eastern range, particularly within the Pampas of Southern South America. The Pampas is one of the most threatened open vegetation ecosystems, rapidly losing native habitats due to intense conversion for agriculture and livestock production. Driven by historical land use practices and the vast areas designated for livestock, xeric and spiny vegetation has long been suppressed and actively removed, as part of pasture management strategies. This review challenges the prevailing narrative that portrays V. caven as a controversial or ambiguous species, frequently labeled as "invasive woody encroacher". This designation, often documented in regions severely affected by conversion and overgrazing, reflects a socio-economic bias that emphasizes its purported negative impact on forage production while overlooking its ecological significance. The observed woody encroachment is fundamentally perceived as a symptom of anthropogenic degradation, including the historical removal of woody vegetation and chronic overgrazing. In contrast to this exploitative view, this xeric species acts as a crucial pioneer and "nurse" plant, essential for ecological succession and for sustaining a unique assemblage of native plants and animals. It facilitates the recruitment of other woody and herbaceous species by improving microhabitat conditions, including nitrogen enrichment and enhanced soil properties. By reframing V. caven’s encroachment as an indicator of ecosystem degradation rather than species invasiveness, we challenge the prevalent narrative that supports active removal of V. caven while underestimating its natural distributional range. Recognizing this species as fundamental to Pampas’ resilience and recovery is therefore essential for effective conservation and the development of sustainable restoration strategies.

Main Points

This review aims to shift the geographical focus of contemporary research regarding *Vachellia caven* from the western range (Chile) to its long-term ecological importance in the eastern range, specifically the Pampas of Southern South America. The Pampas is characterized as a threatened open vegetation ecosystem facing intense habitat loss due to agricultural conversion and livestock production.

The central argument challenges the dominant narrative that labels *V. caven* as an 'invasive woody encroacher.' This negative designation is linked to historical land use, where xeric and spiny vegetation was actively suppressed or removed to maximize forage production, resulting in a socio-economic bias that prioritizes livestock needs over ecological significance. The text proposes that observed woody encroachment is not an inherent trait of invasiveness but rather a symptom of severe anthropogenic degradation, specifically historical woody vegetation removal coupled with chronic overgrazing.

Ecologically, the species plays a positive role. It is described as a crucial pioneer and 'nurse' plant that supports ecological succession. It enhances ecosystem functionality by facilitating the recruitment of other native species (both woody and herbaceous) through improved microhabitat conditions, including nitrogen enrichment and enhanced soil properties.

Conclusions

The conclusions emphasize the critical need to re-evaluate the status of *Vachellia caven*. By reframing the species' encroachment as an indicator of existing ecosystem degradation rather than the cause of invasiveness, the prevalent management practice of active removal, which underestimates the species' natural distribution, is directly challenged.

Ultimately, the authors conclude that effective conservation and the development of sustainable restoration strategies for the Pampas necessitate recognizing *V. caven* as a fundamental component of the ecosystem's resilience and capacity for recovery. This recognition requires moving beyond the current exploitative or biased socio-economic view.

  • "The Pampas is one of the most threatened open vegetation ecosystems, rapidly losing native habitats due to intense conversion for agriculture and livestock production."
  • "Driven by historical land use practices and the vast areas designated for livestock, xeric and spiny vegetation has long been suppressed and actively removed, as part of pasture management strategies."

EFFECT OF Acacia caven (Mol.) ON THE PHYSICAL PROPERTIES OF SOIL UNDER GRAZING EXCLUSIONS

Lely Soto, Esteban Leiva, Felipe Montoya, Óscar Seguel, Cristian Delpiano, Pablo Becerra, Inao Vásquez, Alejandro Miranda, y Cecilia Smith-Ramírez (2015)

Abstract

Cultivated land has increased in Chile as a result of a process in which native woodland sites were converted to agricultural production. This process had an intermediate step in which sclerophyll species were replaced by Acacia caven, prior to cattle grazing. This land use change presents environmental impacts resulting from carbon losses of ecosystems. Therefore, the main objective of this study was to evaluate the effect of A. caven on the physical properties of soil. The study was conducted in two sites of central Chile (Pirque and Peñuelas). Soil samples were taken at depths of 0-10 and 10-20 cm with and without A. caven cover. Organic matter, bulk density, pore size distribution and aggregate stability were evaluated. A marked difference was found in terms of depth in both study sites, also observing a process of densification of the subsurface horizon. A. caven cover has beneficial effects on the soil because it produces increased organic matter, decreased bulk density, and a higher proportion of coarse pores compared to soils without A. caven. However, available water holding capacity was higher in the site that had no influence of A. caven in Pirque. A. caven cover also promotes less mechanical resistance and higher structural stability, so that it is a good alternative to specific site protection towards the restoration of sclerophyll forest.

Main Points

The study analyzed the impact of land use change, specifically the intermediate replacement of native woodland with *Acacia caven* scrubland (espinal) subjected to grazing, on soil properties in Central Chile. The main objective was to quantify the effects of *A. caven* cover on soil physical properties one year after grazing exclusion was established.

The research took place at two sites: Pirque (loamy clay-sandy) and Peñuelas (franco limosa), both historically subject to grazing (bovines, equines, and camelids). Soil samples were collected from four exclusion plots at two depths (0–10 cm and 10–20 cm) in two positions: under the canopy projection (BE) and outside the canopy (FE). Measured parameters included organic matter (MO), bulk density, pore size distribution, aggregate stability, and resistance to shear strength.

Results demonstrated a marked difference in properties based on depth, noting a general process of densification in the subsurface horizon (10–20 cm). Crucially, MO content was significantly higher in the surface horizon (0–10 cm) and consistently greater under *A. caven* cover (BE) compared to open areas (FE), attributed to extra litter input. The presence of *A. caven* cover led to numerous beneficial effects on soil structure: decreased bulk density, increased total porosity, and a higher proportion of coarse pores (PDR, rapid drainage pores). This structural improvement resulted in lower mechanical resistance and greater structural stability (lower dispersion ratio). The only finding that did not favor *A. caven* cover was in Pirque, where available water holding capacity was higher outside its influence.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that *Acacia caven* generates direct and significant benefits to the physical properties of the soil, mitigating the negative impacts associated with land use change and historical grazing pressure. The enhanced properties—namely increased organic matter, reduced bulk density, and higher macroporosity—highlight the species' role in improving infiltration capacity and structural resilience.

The study emphasizes that the positive effects of *A. caven* (espino) on soil health, particularly its function as a reliable source of organic carbon input, are beneficial for ecosystem recovery. Therefore, the species should not be viewed merely as a secondary or transitional formation resulting from degradation, but rather as an active agent in remediation.

Finally, the authors strongly recommend utilizing the established *A. caven* scrubland as a sustainable management tool. They conclude that *A. caven* is "a good alternative to specific site protection towards the restoration of sclerophyll forest," suggesting that it facilitates the process of ecological succession and low-assistance regeneration of native vegetation in degraded landscapes.

  • "A. caven cover also promotes less mechanical resistance and higher structural stability, so that it is a good alternative to specific site protection towards the restoration of sclerophyll forest."

  • "The coverage of espino generates a direct benefit to the soil, which is evidenced by an increase in organic matter, lower bulk density and greater proportion of coarse pores compared to soils outside the espino cover."

Considering spatial constraints to identify areas for new species sampling: A species-specific prioritization approach

Giovani Carlos Andrella, Ingrid Koch, Santiago Jos´e Elías Velazco (2023)

Abstract

Currently, many regions of the world are under-sampled, which means our knowledge about biodiversity has gaps. Many studies proposed methods to identify areas for new specimen sampling; however, none considered the application of spatial constraints for this prioritization, which does not consider logistical issues. Here, we propose an approach to identify priority areas for new specimen sampling, considering distributional aspects based on species distribution models and kernel density estimation for species occurrences. We also used different species weights (e.g., endemicity degree) and spatial constraints (e.g., proximity to roads or land cover). Our method applies to one or several species since our approach separately considers the interaction between the distribution and density of occurrences of each species. We used the Gran Chaco biome as the study area and Leguminosae as a target species group to exemplify the use of our approach. We verified that Gran Chaco presents low sample coverage. Paraguay and Bolivia should be prioritized for new samples, especially considering the endemicity degree of the species. The Gran Chaco is one of the most extensive formations of dry forests on the planet and one of the regions with the highest levels of deforestation, exposing the urgency of field research in the region. Our approach identifies conserved priority areas for new specimen sampling accessible through roads, with a low density of occurrences and high suitability for different species. Our method will help scientists in the field work, reducing costs and facilitating the planning to prioritize regions for new specimen sampling.

Main Points

The authors propose a method to prioritize areas for new specimen sampling by combining species suitability (derived from Species Distribution Models, SDMs) and species occurrence density (Kernel Density Estimation, KDE). The core equation prioritizes areas of high suitability and low occurrence density, which can be further weighted by species characteristics such as endemicity degree.

A crucial innovation of the approach is the inclusion of spatial constraints (Section 2.2) to ensure logistical feasibility and ecological relevance. These constraints include proximity to roads (up to 5 km), a high proportion of natural cover (C > 80%), and focusing on unprotected regions. The study notes that ignoring these spatial constraints might result in prioritizing inaccessible or deforested areas. For the empirical application, the study focused on Leguminosae species within the Gran Chaco biome. Four different SDM modeling protocols were implemented based on the number of occurrences available for each species, utilizing spatial filtering to account for inherent sampling biases.

The results indicated that the Gran Chaco biome suffers from low sample coverage (Wallacean shortfall). Specifically, Paraguay and Bolivia were identified as high-priority areas, especially when prioritizing based on species endemicity. The final outcome is the identification of conserved priority areas that are accessible, possessing low existing occurrence density, and high biological suitability, thus making fieldwork more cost-effective and efficient.

Conclusions

The study concludes that integrating ecological data (suitability and occurrence density) with essential spatial and logistical constraints (accessibility via roads, land cover status) successfully generates efficient prioritization maps for field sampling. This method effectively addresses the biodiversity crisis by directing scientists to critical, yet under-sampled, areas, thereby reducing the Wallacean shortfall.

The authors highlight the extreme urgency of field research in the Gran Chaco, noting it is one of the world's most extensive dry forests but also one of the regions experiencing the highest levels of deforestation. This degradation necessitates targeted efforts to document biodiversity before it is lost. By identifying conserved priority areas that are also accessible, the proposed approach ensures that research efforts are focused on regions most prone to anthropic pressure (like agricultural expansion) but still contain natural cover, ultimately reducing the costs and facilitating the planning required for comprehensive biological inventorying.

Key Quotes

  • "Currently, many regions of the world are under-sampled, which means our knowledge about biodiversity has gaps."
  • "Many studies proposed methods to identify areas for new specimen sampling; however, none considered the application of spatial constraints for this prioritization, which does not consider logistical issues."
  • "The taxonomic and distributive deficits, also known as Linnean and Wallacean shortfalls, point to fieldwork's urgency to collect and describe species."
  • "Considering the efforts to build biological collections and databases consistent with biodiversity, accessibility factors such as roads, cities, navigable rivers, and research centers facilitate access to unrecorded organisms."
  • "However, this can lead to considerable biases in the emerging databases that challenge studies aiming at ecological, evolutionary, or conservation efforts."
  • "This kind of bias could be harmful to studies that use databases as a source of occurrences of organisms, whether on taxonomy, distribution, or time aspects, since the data can also bias spatial predictions of species richness and hinder species management and conservation actions."
  • "The Gran Chaco is one of the most extensive formations of dry forests on the planet and one of the regions with the highest levels of deforestation, exposing the urgency of field research in the region."
  • "The problem with not constraining sites for new samplings is that we can identify regions with few species' occurrences but inaccessible or deforested."
  • "Thus, spatially restricting the locations of interest helps to reduce costs and facilitates the logistics of scientific expeditions."
  • "Nevertheless, studying and documenting biodiversity in regions prone to suffer anthropic pressure is essential (e.g., expansion of the agricultural frontier), especially in those unprotected by public or private policies and actions."

Frequent ﬁres do not affect sexual expression and reproduction in Vachellia caven

VICTORIA MARQUEZ, LUCAS M. CARBONE, RAMIRO AGUILAR AND LORENA ASHWORTH (2019)

Abstract

In environments with high ﬁre frequency the impoverishment of abiotic resources may favour male sexual expression in plants as it is less costly than female expression. Also, ﬁre can modify pollinator communities and thus affect plant reproduction. Here we evaluate the effect of frequent ﬁres on sexual expression, pollination and reproductive success of Vachellia caven (Leguminosae), an andromonoecious tree that is highly dependent on animal pollination and is abundant in burned sites. We expect that increased ﬁre frequency will favour maleness but it will decrease reproductive success due to abiotic resource depletion in repeated burned sites. To test this, we selected focal plants in three unburned sites and three frequently burned sites and measured their sexual expression, basal diameter, pollination and fruit set. The proportion of male inﬂorescences per plant was not affected by ﬁre and it was negatively related with the diameter of the plant. The proportion of pollinated ﬂowers was not affected by ﬁre, and fruit set increased with maleness only in frequently burned sites. These results indicate that V. caven is adapted to regimes of high ﬁre frequency: not only was there similar fruit set in both burned and unburned sites, but more male plants had higher fruit set in burned sites. Despite the soil impoverishment triggered by repeated ﬁres, V. caven is able to maintain its sexual and reproductive functions, allowing it to persist and maintain viable populations in ﬁre-prone environments.

Main Points

The study assessed the resilience of *Vachellia caven*, an andromonoecious species, to frequent ﬁre regimes in the Chaco Serrano region of central Argentina. Researchers selected three unburned sites and three frequently burned sites (3–4 ﬁres in 25 years) with similar topography. They sampled 42 adult plants, measuring basal diameter, sexual expression (proportion of male inflorescences), pollination success (polyad deposition on stigma), and female reproductive success (fruit set).

Statistical analysis using Generalised Linear Mixed Models (GLMMs) found that the frequency of ﬁre did not affect the proportion of male inﬂorescences per plant. Instead, maleness was negatively related to plant diameter, meaning smaller plants were more male-biased regardless of the ﬁre history. Furthermore, fire frequency had no significant effect on the proportion of pollinated flowers, suggesting that pollination services remained robust even in frequently burned areas.

Crucially, overall fruit set (female reproductive success) was similar between the frequently burned and unburned sites, contradicting the expectation that resource depletion from frequent fires would limit reproduction. An unexpected finding was that fruit set increased with maleness (proportion of male flowers) specifically in frequently burned sites. These results collectively demonstrate that *V. caven* maintains high reproductive success and resilience under extreme disturbance.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that *Vachellia caven* possesses a high degree of reproductive adaptation to regimes of frequent ﬁre, a key disturbance factor in the Chaco Serrano. This adaptation allows it to sustain sexual and reproductive functions even when soil resources are potentially impoverished by repeated burning. The similarity in fruit set between burned and unburned sites shows that *V. caven* avoids the trade-off expected under resource-limited conditions, where resource allocation typically shifts towards the less costly male function at the expense of female output.

While the study hypothesized that resource depletion would favor maleness, the observed lack of change in sexual expression suggests that the species successfully acquires or reallocates sufficient resources for reproduction post-ﬁre. The positive relationship between maleness and fruit set found specifically in burned sites is interpreted as a compensatory mechanism, potentially by reducing sexual interference or enhancing pollinator visits. Ultimately, the capacity of *V. caven* to maintain viable sexual reproduction, alongside its ability to resprout, is the key mechanism enabling it to persist and form stable, dominant populations in highly disturbed, fire-prone environments.

  • "Here we evaluate the effect of frequent ﬁres on sexual expression, pollination and reproductive success of Vachellia caven (Leguminosae), an andromonoecious tree that is highly dependent on animal pollination and is abundant in burned sites."
  • "These results indicate that V. caven is adapted to regimes of high ﬁre frequency:"
  • "Despite the soil impoverishment triggered by repeated ﬁres, V. caven is able to maintain its sexual and reproductive functions, allowing it to persist and maintain viable populations in ﬁre-prone environments."
  • "Vachellia caven is a dominant species of the native ﬂora and a main component of the post-ﬁre communities in the Chaco Serrano (Herrero et al. 2015)."
  • "...not only was there similar fruit set in both burned and unburned sites, but more male plants had higher fruit set in burned sites."
  • "The Gran Chaco is the most extensive dry forest of South America... and ﬁre has been historically present as part of the natural dynamic of this environment."
  • "It is a very common pioneer species in disturbed sites (Giorgis et al. 2011; Ashworth et al. 2015)..."
  • "Beyond the capacity of individ-ual persistence, sexual reproduction is a key process that determines the long-term viability of plant popu-lations..."

BASES ECOLÓGICAS Y TECNOLÓGICAS PARA EL MANEJO DE PASTIZALES

A. Altesor, W. Ayala, J.M. Paruelo (2011)

Abstract

Si bien la historia y cultura de la sociedad uruguaya tiene sus bases en el campo natural, paradójicamente su importancia es subvalorada y sus características son desconocidas por la mayor parte de la población. Tradicionalmente se han descrito las características de nuestra vegetación por la negativa, «el Uruguay es un país sin árboles». En las descripciones de la flora sólo se resaltan los elementos arbóreos, incluso aquellos exóticos. Basta observar las imágenes asociadas al «Uruguay Natural» para darnos cuenta que los pastizales son un tipo de vegetación «invisible» desde el punto de vista estético o recreativo y con frecuencia también desde el punto de vista productivo. En el mundo es cada vez más común la alimentación del ganado a través del reemplazo de la vegetación natural o del uso de concentrados en «feedlots». Estos últimos son casos extremos de simplificación del sistema productivo y se enmarcan en una visión de intervención del tipo «comando-control» por parte de quienes manejan los recursos. La mayor parte de estas estrategias de intervención están asociadas a tecnologías apropiables y, consecuentemente de fácil comercialización en el mercado en forma de paquetes tecnológicos. Estas tecnologías empiezan a ser cuestionadas por su baja eficiencia energética, su dependencia de insumos y sus consecuencias ambientales y sociales. Una visión distinta del manejo de los sistemas productivos considera el aprovechamiento del potencial de los sistemas naturales en términos de su productividad como de su resiliencia. Manejar los pastizales naturales, plantea serios desafíos ya que implica tratar con sistemas más complejos, es decir más heterogéneos, con más componentes e interacciones. Las tecnologías para el manejo de sistemas complejos se apoyan mucho más en procesos que en insumos, lo cual las hace poco susceptibles de apropiación por parte del sector privado. Esto hace que el desarrollo de este tipo de tecnología sea una responsabilidad, fundamentalmente, del sector público. Uruguay reúne excelentes condiciones para destacarse como productor natural, por lo que el desarrollo de tecnologías que compatibilicen la producción con la conservación del campo natural debería ser una prioridad. Este objetivo es inalcanzable sin un sólido conocimiento de, por un lado, la heterogeneidad florística de los pastizales naturales y, por otro, de la estructura y el funcionamiento de estos ecosistemas.

Main Points

The book synthesizes research from the FPTA 175 project focused on developing ecological and technological bases for adaptive management of the complex natural grasslands (Campo Natural, CN) of Uruguay, which constitutes over 70% of the territory and forms part of the Río de la Plata temperate prairies.

### Structural and Compositional Analysis
The research first established the ecological structure by analyzing floristic heterogeneity. Chapter I describes how 13 vegetation units were objectively defined across four major geomorphological regions (e.g., Cuesta basáltica, Sierras del este) using phytosociological analysis of 313 vegetation censuses. Chapter II details the methodology for cartographing these units using supervised classification of high-resolution satellite imagery (Landsat TM), providing a spatial pattern of heterogeneity.

### Impact of Grazing and Ecosystem Function
The text addresses the functional consequences of land use. Chapter IV summarizes the evidence regarding the effects of domestic livestock grazing on vegetation structure (changes in architecture, species composition, and individual species attributes). Chapters V and VI focus on the below-ground system, with Chapter V synthesizing data on the effects of grazing on the biogeochemical cycles of Carbon (C) and Nitrogen (N), and Chapter VI analyzing the relationship between vegetation units and soil macrofauna composition, confirming the crucial role of biota in soil condition and vegetation characteristics.

### Technological Tools for Adaptive Management
A major component is the development of technology for monitoring Net Aerial Primary Productivity (PPNA), a key variable for pastoral systems. Chapters VII and VIII detail the theory and methodology for estimating PPNA using remote sensing, focusing on the calculation of Radiation Use Efficiency (EUR). Chapters IX and X describe the implementation of a pilot forage monitoring system for Uruguay. This system provides objective, monthly data on forage production per paddock, crucial for short-term decision-making (paddock rotation) and long-term evaluation of management impacts on forage productivity and animal production variables (weight, corporal state). This technological approach supports the shift away from input-dependent systems towards process-based, adaptive management.

Conclusions

The core conclusion presented in the introduction is that the long-standing management approach, often characterized by 'command-control' interventions, simplification, and replacement of natural vegetation, is unsustainable, inefficient, and detrimental environmentally and socially. Given Uruguay's excellent potential as a 'natural producer,' the development of technologies that successfully reconcile livestock production with the conservation of the complex natural grassland ecosystem is deemed a fundamental priority.

Achieving this compatibility necessitates a robust foundation of ecological knowledge concerning floristic heterogeneity, ecosystem structure, and ecosystem function. The research outlined in the book provides this foundation by characterizing ecological complexity (vegetation units, grazing impacts on C and N cycles, soil biota roles) and offering sophisticated technological instruments (remote sensing for PPNA) to enable objective, data-driven, adaptive management. The research is designed to shift management focus from easily commercializable input packages (appropriate for simplified systems) to process-based technologies, which are essential for managing complex natural systems and are primarily the responsibility of the public sector. This integrated approach ultimately aims to strengthen the national system of agricultural research and ensure sustainable development of the sector.

Key Quotes

  • "Si bien la historia y cultura de la sociedad uruguaya tiene sus bases en el campo natural, paradójicamente su importancia es subvalorada y sus características son desconocidas por la mayor parte de la población."
  • "Basta observar las imágenes asociadas al «Uruguay Natural» para darnos cuenta que los pastizales son un tipo de vegetación «invisible» desde el punto de vista estético o recreativo y con frecuencia también desde el punto de vista productivo."
  • "En el mundo es cada vez más común la alimentación del ganado a través del reemplazo de la vegetación natural o del uso de concentrados en «feedlots»."
  • "Estos últimos son casos extremos de simplificación del sistema productivo y se enmarcan en una visión de intervención del tipo «comando-control» por parte de quienes manejan los recursos."
  • "Estas tecnologías empiezan a ser cuestionadas por su baja eficiencia energética, su dependencia de insumos y sus consecuencias ambientales y sociales."
  • "Una visión distinta del manejo de los sistemas productivos considera el aprovechamiento del potencial de los sistemas naturales en términos de su productividad como de su resiliencia."
  • "Manejar los pastizales naturales, plantea serios desafíos ya que implica tratar con sistemas más complejos, es decir más heterogéneos, con más componentes e interacciones."
  • "Las tecnologías para el manejo de sistemas complejos se apoyan mucho más en procesos que en insumos, lo cual las hace poco susceptibles de apropiación por parte del sector privado."
  • "El desarrollo de tecnologías que compatibilicen la producción con la conservación del campo natural debería ser una prioridad."
  • "Piñeiro (Capítulo V) presenta una síntesis de la información obtenida... acerca de los efectos del pastoreo sobre los ciclos biogeoquímicos del C y N en los pastizales del Río de la Plata."

Biodiversidata: A novel dataset for the vascular plant species diversity in Uruguay

Florencia Grattarola, Andrés González, Patricia Mai, Laura Cappuccio, César Fagúndez-Pachón, Florencia Rossi, Franco Teixeira de Mello, Lucía Urtado, Daniel Pincheira-Donoso (2020)

Abstract

Background South America hosts some of the world’s most prominent biodiversity hotspots. Yet, Uruguay – a country where multiple major ecosystems converge – ranks amongst the countries with the lowest levels of available digital biodiversity data in the continent. Such prevalent data scarcity has significantly undermined our ability to progress towards evidence-based conservation actions – a critical limitation for a country with a strong focus on agricultural industries and only 1.3% of the land surface guarded by protected areas. Under today’s rapid biodiversity loss and environmental changes, the need for open- access biodiversity data is more pressing than ever before. To address this national issue, Biodiversidata – Uruguay’s first Consortium of Biodiversity Data – has recently emerged with the aim of assembling a constantly growing database for the biodiversity of this country. While the first phase of the project targeted vertebrate biodiversity, the second phase presented in this paper spans the biodiversity of plants. New information As part of the second phase of the Biodiversidata initiative, we present the first comprehensive open-access species-level database of the vascular plant diversity recorded in Uruguay to date (i.e. all species for which data are currently available and species presence has been confirmed). It contains 12,470 occurrence records from across 1,648 species and 160 families, which roughly represents 60% of the total recorded flora of Uruguay. The primary biodiversity data include extant native and introduced species from the lycophytes, ferns, gymnosperms and angiosperms groups. Records were collated from multiple sources, including data available in peer-reviewed scientific literature, institutional scientific collections and datasets contributed by members of the Biodiversidata initiative. The complete database can be accessed at the Zenodo repository: doi.org/10.5281/ zenodo.3954406

Main Points

The Biodiversidata initiative, Uruguay's first Consortium of Biodiversity Data, addresses the critical lack of digital biodiversity data in the country by focusing on vascular plants in its second phase, following an initial focus on vertebrates. The main outcome is the creation of the first comprehensive open-access species-level database for vascular plant diversity in Uruguay, encompassing 12,470 occurrence records from 1,648 species across 160 families. This substantial compilation represents approximately 60% of Uruguay’s known flora, covering lycophytes, ferns, gymnosperms, and angiosperms, including both extant native and introduced species.

Data mobilization was exhaustive, drawing from multiple sources: peer-reviewed scientific literature, institutional scientific collections (e.g., MVM, MVJB), and primary records contributed by Biodiversidata members. Online data was primarily harvested from GBIF, filtered rigorously for the Tracheophyta taxon within Uruguay, collected after 1900, requiring geographic coordinates, and excluding records with spatial issues. After quality control, 3,428 GBIF records were retained, with significant contributions from institutions like the Missouri Botanical Garden and citizen science data via iNaturalist.

To ensure data fitness, the authors implemented rigorous quality control following the Darwin Core standard and FAIR principles. This included automated processing in R to check misspellings, resolve synonymy, and standardize taxonomic nomenclature against sources like World Flora Online and Darwinion. Georeferences were meticulously checked, filtering out records occurring outside Uruguay's continental territory. Furthermore, the ‘establishmentMeans’ term was used to classify species as native or introduced. The resulting database exhibits high integrity, with all 12,470 records containing coordinate information and 89.2% including a date of collection, providing the necessary spatial and temporal precision previously missing in national databases.

Conclusions

The authors strongly assert that Uruguay must immediately transition its biodiversity information management system toward widespread digitization and open-access sharing of databases. This need is framed as critical, given that Uruguay faces accelerating environmental changes, holds one of the lowest levels of digitally-available biodiversity data in Latin America, has the least developed protected area network in the region, and is experiencing high land modification rates (over 36.2% of the territory already altered).

This new vascular plant database fills a crucial gap left by previous standard approaches in Uruguay, which relied on broad grid-cell maps that led to serious data leakage and loss of spatial and temporal precision. By providing a standardized, comprehensive, open-access resource, Biodiversidata enables the progress of evidence-based conservation actions, which is essential for a country dominated by agricultural industries. Although the data may exhibit historical sampling biases, similar to those found in the prior phase focusing on tetrapods, the compilation of vascular plant data, combined with the vertebrate data, establishes an unprecedented resource vital for supporting biodiversity studies and effective management, particularly within the vulnerable Río de la Plata grasslands ecosystem.

Key Quotes

  • "Uruguay – a country where multiple major ecosystems converge – ranks amongst the countries with the lowest levels of available digital biodiversity data in the continent."
  • "Such prevalent data scarcity has significantly undermined our ability to progress towards evidence-based conservation actions – a critical limitation for a country with a strong focus on agricultural industries and only 1.3% of the land surface guarded by protected areas."
  • "Under today’s rapid biodiversity loss and environmental changes, the need for open- access biodiversity data is more pressing than ever before."

  • "data on the country’s plant biodiversity remain limited and scattered across a range of small-scale databases, the majority of which are inaccessible."

Biogeographic regionalization of the Neotropical region: New map and shapeﬁle

JUAN J. MORRONE, TANIA ESCALANTE, GERARDO RODRÍGUEZ-TAPIA, AYLÍN CARMONA, MARCELO ARANA & JORGE D. MERCADO-GÓMEZ (2022)

Abstract

We provide a map and shapefile of the 57 biogeographic provinces of the Neotropical region. Recognition of these provinces is based on their endemic species, but their delimitation on the map is based on ecoregions combining climatic, geological, and biotic criteria. These provinces belong to the Antillean, Brazilian and Chacoan subregions, and the Mexican and South American transition zones. We provide a vector file of the biogeographical regionalization by converting the map into a polygon shapefile and a raster file with all provinces.

Main Points

The primary objective of this study was to generate a unified, precise, and standardized map and associated shapefiles for the 57 biogeographic provinces of the Neotropical region, addressing previous disagreements regarding boundary delineations and nomenclature.

### Methods

The authors synthesized various evolutionary biogeographic schemes (based on areas of endemism) with established ecoregional schemes (based on climatic, geological, and biotic criteria). The cartographic work utilized modified shapefiles from Olson et al.'s (2001) 'Terrestrial ecoregions of the world' as the base map, redrawing provinces using ArcMap v. 10.1 and QGIS v. 2.16.3. The process involved three key steps: 1) selecting Neotropical ecoregions and excluding Nearctic/Andean areas; 2) meticulously modifying boundaries using updated information from regional studies (e.g., incorporating specific shapefiles for Brazilian 'campos rupestres' and Colombian paramos, and modifying boundaries for Argentinean and Mexican/Central American provinces based on recent literature); 3) generating individual shapefiles for each province, including metadata on the subregion or transition zone, and finally converting the complete set into a raster format (30 arc-seconds resolution) to ensure compatibility with other environmental data sources.

### Results

The resulting regionalization recognizes 57 biogeographic provinces classified into three main subregions and two transition zones. The three subregions are: the Antillean subregion (7 provinces), the Brazilian subregion (27 provinces across four dominions), and the Chacoan subregion (11 provinces across three dominions). The two transition zones are the Mexican Transition Zone (5 provinces) and the South American Transition Zone (7 provinces, including high-altitude Andean areas and desert regions). The final products, including vector (shapefile) and raster files, were made freely available online, projected in SIRGAS2000 for utility in further biogeographic research.

Conclusions

The authors successfully produced a unified, high-resolution biogeographic map and associated digital files for the Neotropical region, resolving ambiguities in boundaries that previously hindered comparative analysis among different studies. By integrating evolutionary units (areas of endemism) with environmental ecoregions (combining climate, geology, and biota), they created a robust framework for regionalization.

This standardization facilitates future biogeographic research, including species distribution modeling and conservation planning, by providing strictly comparable spatial units across the entire Neotropics. The free availability of the map in widely compatible vector and raster formats ensures that this new regionalization can be readily utilized by the scientific community for diverse analytical purposes.

Key Quotes

  • "Biogeographic regionalization of the Neotropical region: New map and shapeﬁle"
  • "The Neotropical region comprises the tropical areas of South America, Central America, southern and central Mexico, and the Antilles (Morrone 2018)."
  • "We provide a map and shapefi le of the 57 biogeographic provinces of the Neotropical region."
  • "Recognition of these provinces is based on their endemic species, but their delimitation on the map is based on ecoregions combining climatic, geological, and biotic criteria."
  • "These provinces belong to the Antillean, Brazilian and Chacoan subregions, and the Mexican and South American transition zones."
  • "In addition to the different names that these provinces have received (see Morrone 2014), there is little agreement on their precise boundaries, especially when they span over different countries."
  • "To unify the different evolutionary biogeographic and ecoregional schemes into a single system, as done for Argentina (Arana et al. 2017, 2021) and Mexico (Morrone et al. 2017)..."
  • "Thus, we provide herein a map of the biogeographic provinces of the Neotropical region... based on the ecoregions recognized by Olson et al. (2001), and the corresponding shapefi le."
  • "Chacoan subregion: southeastern South America, with three dominions and 11 provinces (Morrone 2014, 2017, Colli-Silva et al. 2019, Arana et al. 2021)."

BIOGEOGRAPHY AND ECOLOGY IN SOUTH AMERICA (Volume 1)

The Volume Editors (1968)

Abstract

With ‘Biogeography and Ecology in South America’ as the general theme, a total of twenty-nine contributions by thirty authors is offered here in two volumes, being volumes 18 and 19 of the Monographiae Biologicae. Most of these discussions deal with decidedly specialist themes and the editors have been particularly concerned to ensure that the authors enjoyed the greatest possible freedom in the preparation of their work in order that different points of view and interpretations, together with some questions of controversy, may be clarified. This also applies, of course, to the several chapters in which general themes (geographical substance, climate, geology, vegetation, amongst others) are discussed.

Main Points

The text, serving as the introduction to the two-volume collection on South American Biogeography and Ecology, establishes the scope and methodology of the work. It notes that the 29 contributions serve as a ‘preparatory work’ for a complete continental description, allowing specialists freedom to discuss controversial topics and differing interpretations.

* **Geographic Complexity and Historical Context:** The editors stress the immense and unmistakable richness and vertical differentiation of South America, which results in regional complexity and sharply contrasting ecosystems (e.g., Amazonian hylaea, altiplano). Historically, despite this diversity, a cohesive 'South American space-consciousness' was impaired because intellectual and political orientation remained focused on Europe (Madrid and Lisbon) for centuries, delaying inter-continental relations and a necessary understanding of the continent's natural unity.

* **Methodology and Biohistorical Perspective:** The work integrates abiotic data (geology, climate) with specialist systematic studies. The distribution pattern of each taxon is identified as fundamental to its 'unquantizable character,' determined by genetic, ecological, and biohistorical destiny. This biohistorical perspective is critical for analyzing distribution patterns and understanding the continent's ecological structure, even if the data for many groups remain insufficient.

Conclusions

The introduction concludes by emphasizing that a realistic biogeographical picture of South America must acknowledge that the continent's fundamental biotic structure has been significantly transformed by humanity, referred to as the 'super-organic factor,' since pre-historic times. While man arrived late, his influence is now commanding and pervasive.

* **Anthropogenic Degradation and Sensitivity:** The history of colonization, particularly by Western peoples unfamiliar with local conditions, led to severe difficulties in 'adjustment' for both man and the natural environment. This lack of adjustment manifests as large-scale, detrimental modifications to the natural scene. The text explicitly lists symptoms relevant to degradation, such as **disafforestation, erosion, soil degradation,** and 'catastrophies due to weeds and pests,' underscoring the extraordinary susceptibility (sensitivity) of South America’s natural realities to human interference.

* **Call for Research and Conservation:** The editors assert that understanding these modifications and unresolved questions is essential. The volume aims to further an intensification of inter-continental relations and a necessary understanding of the continent's natural unity, thereby contributing to effective conservation and sustainable development, which requires moving beyond superficial observation of the degradation patterns.

Key Quotes

  • "The impressive and unmistakable richness of South America’s natural life and conditions has been stressed repeatedly since the time of A. v. Humboldt, CH. Darwin, TH. Haenke, E. Poeppig, Spix and Martius and other important investigators of the last century."
  • "The vast expanse of this continent and its pronounced vertical divisions considerably increase the regional differentiation over the whole area."
  • "Abruptly contrasting ecosystems often lie immediately next to each other, particularly in the region of tropical and sub-tropical high mountain chains and coastal mountains."
  • "As a result of this early history, the development of a South American space-consciousness was effectively impaired (arrested) until very recently."
  • "The natural distribution pattern of each taxon is an essential part of its unquantizable character."
  • "The biotic fundamental structure of the continent can only partly be reconstructed because it has been transformed in many ways since pre-historic times by the ‘super-organic factor’ (in the sense used by A. THIENEMANN), that is by mankind."
  • "While, in the Old World, during the course of a long history, he fitted in fairly well with the natural world which surrounded him, in South America especially the western peoples found themselves confronted almost everywhere with many conditions to which they were unaccustomed."
  • "The difficulties of adjustment on both sides, that is, on the side of man himself as well as of the natural life of the country, are greater than the superficial observer is inclined to assume..."
  • "These difficulties are perhaps most strongly shown as large-scale modifications to the natural scene, mostly disadvantageous (e.g. disafforestation, erosion, soil degradation, karst formation, catastrophies due to weeds and pests, and so on)..."

Were Dry Forests widespread in the Pleistocene and what is their fate under climate change? A modelling approach using a specialist plant

Mário Sérgio Duarte Branco, Paulo Weslem Portal Gomes, Laymara Xavier-Sampaio, José Luis Passos Cordeiro, Luciana dos Santos Dias de Oliveira, Eimear Nic Lughadha, Marcelo Freire Moro (2024)

Abstract

Seasonally Dry Tropical Forests (SDTF) are deciduous and semideciduous seasonal forests that are geographically distant from each other in South America, but share multiple species in common. How did these disjunct species distributions arise and what changes in their distribution can be expected under climate change? Gymnanthes boticario, a SDTF specialist, is a perfect model to test these questions.Occurrence data were obtained from herbarium sheets and confirmed by the authors and/or taxonomic publications. We selected bioclimatic variables for past, current and future scenarios and modeled the potential distribution of Gymnanthes boticario for three paleoclimatic conditions and two future climate change scenarios. We found an unexpectedly large geographical extent of climate suitability for G. boticario during the Last Interglacial (LIG), forming a possible connection between the Brazilian dry Caatinga forests and the dry forests of Bolivia. In the Last Glacial Maximum (LGM), our model shows a reduction in the extent of suitability compared to the LIG, making the suitable area more similar to that modelled for the current period. For all future climate change scenarios, we found a decrease in the total suitable area. The future scenario of SSP585 is most severe, with a loss of almost 60 % of G.boticario suitable areas. We show that during drier timeframes, dry forests likely became less geographically distant from each other, allowing migration between the two now isolated dry forests. This historic connectivity could explain the considerable number of species disjunctions between Caatinga and Bolivian dry forests. Our models indicate that the broadest distribution of SDTF may have occurred during the LIG, and not in the LGM as previously thought. Moreover, climate change models indicate that the future may be even more challenging than the current for SDTF habitats.

Main Points

The study utilized Ensemble Species Distribution Modeling (eSDM), incorporating three algorithms (Random Forests, Support Vector Machines, and Maximum Entropy), to analyze the potential range shifts of the specialist plant *Gymnanthes boticario* across South American Seasonally Dry Tropical Forests (SDTF). To manage collinearity among the 19 bioclimatic variables used for past, current, and future scenarios, Principal Component Analysis (PCA) was performed, yielding six predictors that accounted for 96.15% of the data variation. The models spanned three paleoclimatic conditions (LIG, LGM, MH), the current period (1981–2010), and future projections (2055 and 2085) under optimistic (SSP126) and pessimistic (SSP585) routes.

Results addressing the Pleistocene Arc Hypothesis showed that the Last Interglacial (LIG, 130 Kya) exhibited the broadest geographical extent of climate suitability for *G. boticario*, forming a potential continuous corridor connecting the Caatinga and Bolivian dry forests. This finding suggests the broadest SDTF distribution occurred during the LIG, contradicting older theories focused on the LGM. Furthermore, for future scenarios, a consistent and worrying decrease in total suitable area was projected. The most severe reduction occurred under the pessimistic SSP585 climate change scenario, predicting a loss of nearly 60% of the suitable habitat for the specialist species. This indicates that while past drier climates fostered connectivity and range expansion, current climate change driven by aridity and desertification will severely challenge the persistence of specialized dry forest species.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that the historic connectivity required to explain the disjunct species distribution patterns between the Caatinga and Bolivian dry forests likely occurred during the Last Interglacial (LIG, 130 Kya), when dry forests were less geographically distant, allowing species migration. This challenges the long-standing belief that the Last Glacial Maximum (LGM) represented the maximal expansion period for SDTFs.

Crucially for conservation, the study highlights the extreme vulnerability of SDTF habitats to future climate change. The models forecast that increasing aridity, aligning with predictions for the expansion of semi-arid and arid areas and accelerated desertification, will result in significant habitat loss. The anticipated reduction in suitable areas (up to 60% loss under SSP585) demonstrates that even specialist species adapted to dry conditions, governed by niche conservatism, will not be able to tolerate the drastic environmental shifts projected. Therefore, the future outlook for SDTF habitats is projected to be significantly more challenging than current conditions.

  • "How did these disjunct species distributions arise and what changes in their distribution can be expected under climate change?"
  • "We found an unexpectedly large geographical extent of climate suitability for G. boticario during the Last Interglacial (LIG), forming a possible connection between the Brazilian dry Caatinga forests and the dry forests of Bolivia."
  • "The future scenario of SSP585 is most severe, with a loss of almost 60 % of G.boticario suitable areas."
  • "We show that during drier timeframes, dry forests likely became less geographically distant from each other, allowing migration between the two now isolated dry forests."
  • "Moreover, climate change models indicate that the future may be even more challenging than the current for SDTF habitats."
  • "The biota of these areas is adapted to a much drier and unpredictable rainfall than that of surrounding biomes, with species adapted to many months of water restriction per year..."
  • "...the current climate crisis has increased the susceptibility of semi-arid and arid regions to desertification in Caatinga (Marengo et al., 2017)."
  • "Therefore, our hypothesis is that (b) there will be an expansion of semi-arid and arid areas in South America. However, the potential distribution areas of G. boticario within the dry forests of South America will be reduced in future scenarios..."
  • "Thus, the areas of suitability within the cores of dry forests will be reduced and shifted to regions close to humid forests."

Caracterização e dinâmica da vegetação de uma Savana Estépica Parque, Barra do Quaraí, RS

Luciano Farinha Watzlawick, Solon Jonas Longhi, Paulo Renato Schneider, César Augusto Guimarães Finger, Regis Villanova Longhi (2010)

Abstract

Abstract - This research was carried out at Parque Estadual do Espinilho, in Barra do Quaraí, Rio Grande do Sul State, Brazil, aiming to characterize the floristic, phytosociological structure and dynamics in Savana Estépica Parque. For such, within a permanent sample unit of 1 ha (100 m x 100 m), were identified and measured all individuals with diameter at breast height (DBH) ≥ 10 cm. The floristic is made up of Prosopis affinis, Prosopis nigra, Acacia caven and Scutia bixiflora, which presented a density of 141 and 145 individuals per hectare in 2001 and 2009. The mean annual increment was 0.027 cm ha-1 year-1, with ingrowth and mortality rates of 0.36% per annum. By the presented results, we may conclude that this vegetation is stagnant.

Main Points

## Methodology

This study aimed to characterize the floristic composition, phytosociological structure, and dynamics of the Savana Estépica Parque vegetation within the Parque Estadual do Espinilho, Barra do Quaraí, RS, Brazil. The research utilized a permanent sample unit measuring 1 hectare (100 m x 100 m), where measurements were taken on two occasions: January 2001 and April 2009 (an 8-year monitoring period). All arboreal individuals with Diameter at Breast Height (DAP/DBH) ≥ 10 cm were identified, measured, and permanently marked.

The horizontal structure was assessed using traditional phytosociological parameters (Absolute and Relative Density, Dominance, and Frequency, Cover Value, and Importance Value). Diversity was calculated using the Shannon index (H’). Vegetational dynamics were analyzed by calculating ingrowth (new trees reaching the minimum DAP), mortality, and Annual Periodic Increment (IPA) in diameter, standardized by the number of years monitored.

## Results

* **Floristics and Diversity:** The area exhibited very low floristic diversity (Shannon H' of 0.72 in 2001 and 0.71 in 2009). The arboreal layer was comprised of only four species: *Prosopis affinis*, *Prosopis nigra*, *Acacia caven*, and *Scutia buxifolia*. The family Fabaceae dominated, representing 99.9% of sampled individuals.
* **Structure and Dominance:** The density remained stable, increasing slightly from 141 individuals per hectare in 2001 to 145 individuals per hectare in 2009. Area basal also showed minimal change (4.20 m²/ha to 4.25 m²/ha). *Prosopis affinis* and *Prosopis nigra* were overwhelmingly dominant, collectively accounting for over 93% of the Importance Value (VI) in both survey years. The diameter distribution followed the expected inverted ‘J’ shape but showed no significant frequency shifts between the two sampling periods.
* **Dynamics:** Population turnover was extremely slow. Both ingrowth (new individuals reaching 10 cm DAP) and mortality rates were calculated at a low 0.36% annually. Only four individuals ingressed (all *P. affinis*), and four died (two *P. affinis*, one *P. nigra*, and one *A. caven*). The Annual Periodic Increment (IPA) was exceptionally low, averaging only 0.027 cm per year for the community.

Conclusions

The study concluded that the vegetation of the Savana Estépica Parque is **stagnant** (estagnada).

This conclusion is based on the minimal change observed across all monitored parameters over the 8-year period. The floristic composition remained constant, and the phytosociological structure, defined predominantly by *Prosopis affinis* and *Prosopis nigra*, showed no relevant structural variations in density, basal area, or Importance Value.

Crucially, the dynamic processes were characterized by extremely low rates: the annual ingrowth rate (0.36%) matched the annual mortality rate (0.36%). Furthermore, the annual diameter increment (IPA) was almost negligible at 0.027 cm year⁻¹. These findings demonstrate that the ecosystem is currently maintaining an extremely stable state, lacking significant successional movement or growth, suggesting it has reached a dynamic equilibrium or a state of stagnation under current local conditions.

Degradación y gestión sostenible del campo natural en el Uruguay - Resultados de una evaluación participativa en el norte del país

Gonzalo Cortés Capano; Fernando Coronel; Daniela Schossler; Daniel Formoso; Marcello Rachetti; Ramiro Zanoniani; Pablo Boggiano; Jimena Perez Rocha (2020)

Abstract

The full verbatim text of the 'Resumen ejecutivo' (Executive Summary) is not provided in the supplied text extract.

Main Points

This study, conducted in the northern departments of Rivera, Tacuarembó, and Salto, utilizes the participatory PRAGA methodology (Participatory Evaluation of Land Degradation and Sustainable Management of the Grassland System) to analyze the state of the natural grassland agroecosystem (campo natural).

### Methods
The evaluation integrated multiple data sources, including Geographic Information Systems (SIG) data on soil properties (CONEAT groups, organic carbon) and land cover (LCCS data comparing 2000 and 2015 coverage), alongside participatory mapping and workshops with local actors. The goal was to identify and map areas suffering from land degradation using national criteria developed for reporting to the UNCCD (United Nations Convention to Combat Desertification). The study also analyzed the current institutional and normative framework, and the socio-economic dynamics (e.g., HDI, poverty rates) influencing land use in the region.

### Results
1. **Extensive Degradation:** The assessment confirms significant degradation of natural lands in the northern zone, largely attributed to anthropogenic factors. Figure 28 maps the distribution of potentially degraded lands based on national criteria.
2. **Land Use Change:** There were notable changes in land cover between 2000 and 2015, with transitions classified as 'negative' according to criteria assessing soil use sustainability (Figure 26). The expansion of non-grassland uses (e.g., forestry, intensive agriculture) is a primary driver.
3. **Soil Health:** High levels of anthropogenic erosion were observed in the pilot zone (Figure 31). The study also monitored the status of hydrological resources and soil organic carbon.
4. **Biodiversity Threat:** Degradation leads to a loss of biodiversity, with specific communities of pastizales being threatened (Figure 34). The report also highlights issues with invasive species, such as *Capín Anoni* (Figure 38).
5. **Socio-economic Context:** The study notes that the pilot region suffers from lower socio-economic indicators (e.g., higher poverty and NBI rates) compared to the national average, which exacerbates pressure on natural resources.

Conclusions

The study concludes that the natural grasslands of Northern Uruguay are under severe pressure, driven by the expansion of intensive agricultural and forestry activities, coupled with persistent unsustainable management practices within the livestock sector. This pressure has resulted in widespread anthropogenic soil erosion and habitat loss.

The authors highlight a significant gap between the identified environmental challenges and the effectiveness of current policies and instruments designed to promote sustainable management. Existing frameworks often fail to incentivize the conservation of natural field ecosystems effectively or address the complex, localized nature of degradation.

Ultimately, the report emphasizes the necessity of shifting towards integrated territorial management strategies. These strategies must recognize the ecological value of the natural grassland ecosystem (e.g., the Bioma Pampa-Quebradas del Norte) and promote ‘buenas prácticas’ (good practices) that ensure resilience. Recommendations call for strengthening regulatory bodies and creating economic mechanisms that directly reward producers for maintaining the health and productivity of the natural field, thereby reversing current degradation trends.

Spiny plants, mammal browsers, and the origin of African savannas

Tristan Charles-Dominiquea,b,1, T. Jonathan Daviesc,d, Gareth P. Hempsone, Bezeng S. Bezengc, Barnabas H. Daruf,g, Ronny M. Kabongoc, Olivier Maurinc, A. Muthama Muasyaa, Michelle van der Bankc, and William J. Bonda,h,1 (2016)

Abstract

Savannas first began to spread across Africa during the Miocene. A major hypothesis for explaining this vegetation change is the increase in C4 grasses, promoting fire. We investigated whether mammals could also have contributed to savanna expansion by using spinescence as a marker of mammal herbivory. Looking at the present distribution of 1,852 tree species, we established that spinescence is mainly associated with two functional types of mammals: large browsers and medium-sized mixed feeders. Using a dated phylogeny for the same tree species, we found that spinescence evolved at least 55 times. The diversification of spiny plants occurred long after the evolution of Afrotherian proboscideans and hyracoids. However, it is remarkably congruent with diversification of bovids, the lineage including the antelope that predominantly browse these plants today. Our findings suggest that herbivore-adapted savannas evolved several million years before fire-maintained savannas and probably, in different environmental conditions. Spiny savannas with abundant mammal herbivores occur in drier climates and on nutrient-rich soils, whereas fire-maintained savannas occur in wetter climates on nutrient-poor soils.

Main Points

The researchers investigated the role of mammal herbivory in the origin of African savannas, using spinescence (stem spines) on woody plants as a marker for high mammal herbivory pressure. They analyzed the present distribution of spiny traits across 1,852 tree species in Africa, employing spatial models to link spinescence to herbivore functional types, abiotic factors, and vegetation types. Results showed that spiny communities were primarily associated with two functional types of mammals—large browsers and medium-sized mixed feeders—and occurred mainly in open environments (mixed savannas, shrublands), dry climates, and on nutrient-rich soils where fire frequency was low. The unique contribution of herbivores to spinescence distribution remained significant even after accounting for abiotic factors.

Phylogenetic analysis revealed that spinescence evolved independently at least 55 times across 29 families of African trees, indicating strong convergent evolution. The timing of this diversification was crucial: spiny clades radiated mostly within the last 17 million years (mid-Miocene). This timeline did not align with earlier African megaherbivores (proboscideans and hyracoids), but it was remarkably congruent with the diversification of bovids (antelope), the primary browsers of these plants today. This temporal match suggests a powerful coevolutionary link between the diversification of bovids and the evolution of structural defenses in woody plants.

Conclusions

The study concludes that mammal herbivory, driven particularly by bovids (medium-sized mixed feeders and large browsers), was a primary, ancient, and fundamental agent shaping African savanna structure. This finding challenges the dominant hypothesis that C4 grasses and fire were the initial drivers of savanna expansion.

Crucially, the authors propose a revised timeline for savanna origin, suggesting that 'herbivore-adapted savannas' (spiny savannas) evolved several million years *before* 'fire-maintained savannas.' These two types of open ecosystems are ecologically distinct, existing under different environmental conditions: herbivore-maintained spiny savannas are found in drier climates with nutrient-rich soils, supporting high mammal biomass, whereas fire-maintained savannas occur in wetter, nutrient-poor areas. Therefore, the diversification of bovids and the subsequent convergent evolution of spiny defenses fundamentally changed the rules for woody plant persistence, establishing browser pressure as a major, overlooked driver in the origin and spread of open ecosystems from the late Miocene.

Key Quotes

  • "Spinescence is mainly associated with two functional types of mammals: large browsers and medium-sized mixed feeders."
  • "Our findings suggest that herbivore-adapted savannas evolved several million years before fire-maintained savannas and probably, in different environmental conditions."
  • "Spiny savannas with abundant mammal herbivores occur in drier climates and on nutrient-rich soils, whereas fire-maintained savannas occur in wetter climates on nutrient-poor soils."
  • "An alternative hypothesis, that mammal herbivory creates open ecosystems, was first proposed by Owen-Smith (13)."
  • "mesobrowsers (4–450 kg), such as deer, antelope, and caprids, are very effective at preventing woody plants from escaping the 'browse trap' and growing into larger size classes."
  • "Spines are considered a defense specific to mammal herbivores (21)."
  • "Spines function by reducing bite size of the browsing animal, thereby reducing food intake and driving the animal to move away to seek more rewarding targets (22)."
  • "Our results constitute evidence at large spatial scales that the abundance of mammals is the factor best related to the distribution of spiny communities."
  • "The origin and timing in acceleration of bovid lineage diversification match closely to the increase in spiny plant lineages with present day descendants in African savanna."
Supports Ecological Role and Nurse EffectsSoil and Biogeochemical CyclingPampas Ecosystem Degradation and Resilience

Competition and facilitation structure plant communities under nurse tree canopies in extremely stressful environments

Ali A. Al-Namazi, Magdy I. El-Bana, Stephen P. Bonser (2017)

Abstract

Nurse plant facilitation in stressful environments can produce an environment with relatively low stress under its canopy. These nurse plants may produce the conditions promoting intense competition between coexisting species under the canopy, and canopies may establish stress gradients, where stress increases toward the edge of the canopy. Competition and facilitation on these stress gradients may control species distributions in the communities under canopies. We tested the following predictions: (1) interactions between understory species shift from competition to facilitation in habitats experiencing increasing stress from the center to the edge of canopy of a nurse plant, and (2) species distributions in understory communities are controlled by competitive interactions at the center of canopy, and facilitation at the edge of the canopy. We tested these predictions using a neighbor removal experiment under nurse trees growing in arid environments. Established individuals of each of four of the most common herbaceous species in the understory were used in the experiment. Two species were more frequent in the center of the canopy, and two species were more frequent at the edge of the canopy. Established individuals of each species were subjected to neighbor removal or control treatments in both canopy center and edge habitats. We found a shift from competitive to facilitative interactions from the center to the edge of the canopy. The shift in the effect of neighbors on the target species can help to explain species distributions in these canopies. Canopy- dominant species only perform well in the presence of neighbors in the edge microhabitat. Competition from canopy- dominant species can also limit the performance of edge- dominant species in the canopy microhabitat. The shift from competition to facilitation under nurse plant canopies can structure the understory communities in extremely stressful environments.

Main Points

The study investigated the interplay of competition and facilitation structuring understory plant communities under the nurse tree *Acacia gerrardii* in the arid Sederah Natural reserve in western Saudi Arabia. This environment is characterized by high daily temperatures, low soil moisture, and nutrient-impoverished soil, but the nurse tree creates heterogeneous microhabitats. Previous findings (referenced here) established that the under-canopy areas have high soil nutrients and water availability, lower soil temperatures (37°C vs. 47°C outside), and significantly reduced PAR, creating a stress gradient that increases from the center to the edge of the canopy.

To test the predictions of the Stress Gradient Hypothesis (SGH) in this fine-scale gradient, the researchers conducted a neighbor removal experiment. Four common perennial herbaceous species with differing distribution patterns (two canopy specialists: *Salvia aegyptiaca* and *Fagonia indica*; and two edge specialists: *Farsetia aegyptia* and *Indigofera spinosa*) were selected as targets. Established individuals were subjected to either 'neighbors removed' (clearing a 50 cm plot) or 'neighbors left intact' treatments in both the canopy center (low stress) and the canopy edge (high stress) microhabitats, replicated ten times per species per location. Plant performance was measured using a growth index based on biomass accumulation.

The results strongly supported the predictions. The study documented a clear shift from competitive interactions (in the benign canopy center) to facilitative interactions (at the more stressful canopy edge). This shift explained the observed species distributions: canopy-dominant species required the presence of neighbors (facilitation) to perform well in the high-stress edge microhabitat. Conversely, competitive interactions originating from these canopy-dominant species limited the growth and performance of the edge-dominant species when they occurred in the canopy center microhabitat.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that the shifting balance between competition and facilitation, controlled by microhabitat stress gradients, fundamentally structures understory communities in extremely stressful arid environments. The nurse plant (Acacia gerrardii) ameliorates the harsh external conditions, creating a relatively benign zone beneath its canopy where resource competition among beneficiary species becomes intense. However, moving outward toward the canopy edge, where abiotic stress increases significantly, facilitation becomes the dominant interaction, allowing species to persist by buffering environmental harshness.

This small-scale heterogeneity is critical for understanding species coexistence and distribution. The observed shift from negative (competition) to positive (facilitation) interactions strongly supports the Stress Gradient Hypothesis. The findings underscore that within the overall positive 'nurse syndrome,' localized competition is a powerful force limiting the performance of certain species in the most protected areas, while facilitation is essential for the persistence of key species in the marginal, high-stress zones of the canopy. This mechanism demonstrates how nurse plant microhabitats promote both coexistence and localized dominance.

Key Quotes

  • "Nurse plant facilitation in stressful environments can produce an environment with relatively low stress under its canopy."
  • "These nurse plants may produce the conditions promoting intense competition between coexisting species under the canopy..."
  • "Competition and facilitation on these stress gradients may control species distributions in the communities under canopies."
  • "The prediction that interactions should shift from negative to positive across gradients of increasing stress has been formalized as the stress gradient hypothesis—SGH (Bertness & Callaway, 1994)."
  • "Arid and semi-arid lands are unproductive and stressful for the growth of plants."
  • "Nurse plants play an important role in ameliorating the environmental conditions and creating more benign microhabitats for the understory species in arid habitats..."
  • "The extreme environment around the canopies of nurse plants in arid environments presents heterogeneity at the microhabitat scale..."
  • "In a related study, we demonstrated that the under canopy microhabitats have high soil nutrients and water availability, and low light intensity and UV radiation relative to the surrounding areas..."
  • "The shift from competition to facilitation under nurse plant canopies can structure the understory communities in extremely stressful environments."
  • "The interplay between positive and negative interactions between plants should be important in understanding species distributions, community structure, and ecosystem function..."
