Aerial insectivorous bats in the Brazilian-Uruguayan savanna: Modelling the occupancy through acoustic detection

Cíntia Fernanda da Costa and Maria João Ramos Pereira (2022)

Abstract

The Pampa is the least protected and one of the least sampled for bats among the Brazilian domains. This leads to signiﬁcant Linnean and Wallacean shortfalls for bats in the Brazilian-Uruguayan savanna ecoregion. Here, we aimed to model the occupancy of aerial insectivorous bats in response to landscape structure at different scales, considering the inﬂuence of microclimate on bat detection. We acoustically monitored 68 locations during the spring and summer of 2019/2020, gathering data on temperature and humidity associated with each acoustic record using data loggers. We detected at least 11 species of the Molossidae and the Vespertilionidae families, of which 9 were used in the model. The response to landscape structure was species-speciﬁc: the occupancy probability of Eptesicus brasiliensis and Molossus cf. currentium increased with landscape connectivity at the 500 m scale while Eptesicus furinalis and Histiotus cf. velatus were negatively affected by landscape connectivity at the 5.0 km scale. Molossus occupancy probability responded negatively to landscape heterogeneity at the 3.0 km scale, while Promops centralis responded positively to landscape heterogeneity at the 5.0 km scale. Molossus rufus responded negatively to native vegetation cover and positively to landscape heterogeneity at the 5.0 km scale. Myotis albescens and Molossops temminckii did not respond signiﬁcantly to any of the evaluated landscape metrics. Our results show that different bat species perceive the landscape differently, regardless of the guild of use of space – edge- or open-space forager. Our estimate of projected occupancy for the areas contiguous to those sampled ranged from 0.45 to 0.70 for the whole of the bat taxa, suggesting that the landscape, particularly where it still maintains its native elements, is reasonably favourable to aerial insectivores.

Main Points

The study aimed to model the occupancy of aerial insectivorous bats in the Brazilian Pampa (Brazilian-Uruguayan savanna) in response to landscape structure (connectivity, heterogeneity, and native vegetation cover) across multiple scales, while also accounting for microclimatic influences on bat detection.

Methods involved acoustically monitoring 68 sites, located at least 1.5 km apart, during the spring and summer of 2019 and 2020, using automated sound detectors (AudioMoths) coupled with temperature and humidity sensors. The sites spanned areas of pristine habitat (native grasslands, gallery forests, shrub formations) and those heavily influenced by cattle raising and agriculture (rice and soybean crops). Bat activity was quantified using "bat passes" (sequences of at least three consecutive, good quality echolocation calls). Landscape metrics were extracted from land use rasters using seven circular buffers ranging from 500 m to 5.0 km radius around each monitoring site.

The researchers detected at least 11 species from the Molossidae and Vespertilionidae families. Results showed that the response to landscape structure was highly species-specific and scale-dependent. For instance, the occupancy of *Eptesicus brasiliensis* increased with landscape connectivity at the small 500 m scale, while *Eptesicus furinalis* was negatively affected by connectivity at the larger 5.0 km scale. Landscape heterogeneity also had varied effects, positively affecting *Promops centralis* at 5.0 km but negatively affecting Molossus species at 3.0 km. Critically, *Molossus rufus* exhibited a negative response to native vegetation cover, despite responding positively to landscape heterogeneity. Overall, the projected occupancy for the bat taxa (0.45 to 0.70) suggested the landscape, particularly where native elements persist, is still reasonably favorable.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that the Brazilian Pampa is an ecosystem under severe pressure, noting it is the least protected domain in Brazil, having lost 21.4% of its native vegetation cover between 1985 and 2020 due to the conversion of open habitats into monocultures and intensive use for agriculture and livestock production. This habitat loss and fragmentation significantly alter landscape structure and limit species persistence.

The core conclusion regarding bat ecology is that different species of aerial insectivores perceive and respond to changes in landscape structure in a species-specific and scale-dependent manner, regardless of their established foraging guild (edge- or open-space forager). This complexity underscores the limitation of simple guild classifications for conservation planning and necessitates fine-grained, species-specific analyses to understand biodiversity responses to human impact.

Despite the extensive degradation and habitat homogenization, the findings suggest that the remaining native elements of the Pampa landscape continue to support aerial insectivores effectively. The study strongly supports the conservation need for the Brazilian Pampa, emphasizing that maintaining and enhancing the existing landscape structure—such as connectivity and heterogeneity—is crucial for sustaining bat populations, which serve an important role as bioindicators and biological control agents.

Key Quotes

  • "The Pampa is the least protected and one of the least sampled for bats among the Brazilian domains."
  • "Habitat loss, fragmentation, and degradation are the most cited causes of species extinction and biodiversity decline globally..."
  • "Intensive land use changes alter the structure of the landscape and limit the potential occurrence, dispersal, and colonisation by diﬀerent species..."
  • "The conversion of open habitats into monoculture plantations of fast-growing trees or their degradation through intensive use for agriculture and livestock production has changed the dynamics and structure of the landscape..."
  • "The main consequence of intense land use change is habitat homogenisation."
  • "Patches and networks of distinct natural elements provide increased resource availability, including food, roosts and shelters, and niche space for other activities throughout the animals’ life histories, favouring increased coexistence, persistence, and species diversiﬁcation..."
  • "In recent decades, the Pampean landscape has suﬀered with the conversion of natural areas into monocultures and the introduction of exotic species in silviculture..."
  • "Recent estimates indicate that the Pampa lost 21.4% of the remaining native vegetation cover between 1985 and 2020, remaining only 46% of the original landscape (Souza et al., 2020)."
  • "Currently, only 46% of Brazilian Pampa remains covered by native vegetation and only 2% is legally protected by conservation units; this makes the Pampa the least protected domain in Brazil..."

A perspective on restoration with foundation plants across anthropogenic dry forests of the Southern Cone and the Sahel

Root-Bernstein M, Addo-Danso SD and Bestelmeyer B (2024)

Abstract

Rewilding is a flexible conservation approach that may be applicable to a wide variety of ecological, historical and socio-cultural contexts. We believe that comparative socio-ecological research on woodland habitat trajectories among contexts is an excellent opportunity to consider possible rewilding approaches. Here, we draw on a comparison between arid and seasonally dry woodlands of the Sahel region of Africa and the Southern Cone of South America. The two regions, while sharing a common Gondwanan floral origin, differ in terms of subsequent biogeographical processes and have different climatic gradients. Historically, both regions were colonised, although along different models, and the Southern Cone has experienced greater land-use change and agricultural modernisation. Culturally, both regions have indigenous populations with traditional management techniques and local ecological knowledge, although attention to these topics in research and conservation has had different emphases in each region. Rewilding, focusing on charismatic animals, has been proposed and implemented in some parts of the Southern Cone, but has hardly been mentioned for the Sahel. We discuss the applicability of potential rewilding models involving key plants for each region, and what a plant-focused rewilding practice could gain from a comparative approach in the two regions.

Main Points

This paper is a perspective analysis comparing the socio-ecological contexts of arid and seasonally dry woodlands in the Sahel and the Southern Cone to advocate for a specific conservation approach: rewilding with foundation plants.

The authors define foundation plants (or "megaflora") as primarily trees, shrubs, palms, and cacti that have ecologically structuring and culturally important roles, facilitating the natural restoration of ecological processes. This approach is distinct because its goal is not species composition or conservation status, but the recovery of ecological functions, explicitly addressing the efficiency of intervening directly on plants. They justify this by noting that in drylands with variable rainfall, bottom-up ecological processes are often dominant, making direct plant intervention appropriate, contrary to the typical trophic rewilding focus on top-down animal impacts.

In their comparative analysis, the authors highlight that the Southern Cone has experienced significantly greater land-use change and agricultural modernization, focusing on industrial production (soy, cattle ranching, eucalyptus/pine plantations). This history has led to restoration practices that are often industrial compensations, typically involving enclosed tree plantations. Crucially, the paper states that **techniques appropriate for arid and semi-arid habitats, such as nurse species or nucleation, are rarely applied** in the Southern Cone. In contrast, the Sahel's management is often farmer and community-led, focusing on economically valuable trees to improve smallholder agriculture and address land degradation drivers like overstocking and land clearing. The paper promotes foundation plant rewilding as a strategy that promotes species autonomy and self-regulation ("passive management") while providing the conditions for greater species’ autonomy, allowing for novel ecosystem trajectories, and potentially integrating sustainable resource harvesting.

Conclusions

The paper concludes that rewilding with foundation plants is a valuable and necessary approach for restoring ecological functions in dry forests, especially in the socio-ecologically complex contexts of the Southern Cone and the Sahel. This strategy shifts the restoration focus from species composition targets to recovering fundamental ecological processes using key structuring species (trees and shrubs).

For the Southern Cone, this perspective offers a robust critique of the prevailing industrial model of restoration. By emphasizing that arid-appropriate techniques like the use of nurse species are neglected, the paper indirectly validates the necessity of recognizing ecologically structuring species like *Vachellia caven*. The foundation plant model favors the recovery of naturalistic habitat structures and encourages spontaneous reassembly, differentiating it from traditional forestry or compensatory plantings. While management may still be required (e.g., control of invasives or fire), the preferred management style aims to restore functions naturally. Ultimately, recognizing and leveraging foundation plants provides a path toward functional resilience in environments heavily impacted by anthropogenic factors, defaunation, and land degradation.

Key Quotes

  • "Most of the focus in the rewilding literature has been on reintroducing animal species, usually megafauna (Bühne et al., 2022)."
  • "But key plant species can structure ecological interactions (Enquist et al., 2020; Schweiger and Svenning, 2020) and can have cultural importance and charisma (Hall et al., 2011; Yadav et al., 2024)."
  • "Our deﬁnition of rewilding with plants is that it focuses on these ecologically structuring and culturally important “megaﬂora”, or more generally, “foundation plants” (cf. foundation species; Ellison, 2019)."
  • "These are primarily trees and shrubs, palms, and cacti, which have foundational roles interacting with other species and abiotic ecosystem components (e.g. soil)."
  • "The focus on foundation plants has the goal of contributing to a natural restoration of ecological processes."
  • "If this were always the case or the whole story, there would be no argument for the efﬁciency of intervening directly to restore plant populations to achieve desired rewilding outcomes."
  • "However, the top down vs. bottom up debate in ecology has led to the understanding that both situations can exist in nature (Meserve et al., 2003), for example in drylands with variable rainfall, and in such bottom up cases and situations direct intervention on plants would be appropriate."
  • "Woodland restoration is in many cases carried out as industrial compensation for habitat destruction, and mainly consists of enclosed tree plantations."
  • "Techniques appropriate for arid and semi-arid habitats such as nurse species or nucleation are rarely applied (Root-Bernstein and Silva, 2022)."
  • "The form of management favored by a rewilding approach would, however, tend as much as possible towards management through reintroduction of natural ecological functions and processes."

FLORA DO PARQUE ESTADUAL DO ESPINILHO – BARRA DO QUARAÍ /RS

Franncisco Renato Galvani, Luis Rios de Moura Baptista (2003)

Abstract

Surveys of the flora were realized on an area of 1.617.14 ha pertaining to Unidade de Conservação - Parque Estadual do Espinilho. The area studied is located at Barra do Quarai, between the coordinates UTM 6654600 (N) 446.500 (E) 6666400 (N) 463,800 (E). The weather on the region is of type Cfa de Köeppen, with annual temperature means of 23.4 ºC and precipitation of 1300 mm. The local soils are predominantly Gleissolo Melânico Carbonático Solódico, hydromorphics and have medium texture. The survey on the vegetation covered collection in the whole area, and classification of species by vegetative habits. For the arboreal/shrub/lianas and epiphyte vegetations, 72 species were registered; 124 herbaceous species and 58 aquatic plants species. The Prosopis affinis, P.nigra and Acacia caven association, which characterize the region physiognomy, are prominence as the most expressive elements in the arboreal flora. The occurrence of Aspidosperma quebrach-blanco is restricted to the area of study. Prosopis affinis represents the most important species, being registered on all the lots, followed by Acacia caven. The Fabaceae and Myrtaceae family representatives compound most of the riparian wood of the hydro network in the Parque, and the aquatic vegetation presents more diversity in the swamps area, and in places with short streams. The Parque area represents the only environment for occurrence of P.nigra and P.affinis in Rio Grande do Sul and in Brazil.

Main Points

The study was conducted to update previous floristic surveys (Bastos, 1997; Massia, 1997) within the 1,617.14 ha Parque Estadual do Espinilho (Barra do Quarai, RS), a conservation unit characterized by a Cfa Köeppen climate and predominantly Gleissolo Melânico soils. The researchers used the expedited method to inventory all vegetative habits (herbaceous, arboreal, lianas, epiphytes, and aquatic macrophytes) across the entire area, categorizing herbaceous species based on topography (lowland, slope, summit).

Results showed a total of 72 species of arboreal/shrub/lianas/epiphytes, 124 herbaceous species, and 58 aquatic macrophyte species. The woody vegetation was dominated by Fabaceae and Myrtaceae (the latter forming most of the riparian forest). Crucially, the park's characteristic vegetation physiognomy is defined by a simple association of three arboreal Fabaceae species: *Prosopis affinis*, *P. nigra*, and *Acacia caven* (Espinilho). *Acacia caven* was noted as part of the transition vegetation, often found at the edge of the riparian forest, while *Prosopis* species are strictly heliophtes restricted to drier, higher ground on Melânico Gleissolo soils. This finding reinforced that the distribution of these key species is highly dependent on edaphic factors and localized water scarcity. The park was confirmed to be the single known location in Rio Grande do Sul and Brazil where the critical association of *P. nigra* and *P. affinis* naturally occurs, highlighting the restricted and unique nature of this disjunction of Chaquenha vegetation.

Conclusions

The authors successfully compiled and updated fragmented information on the flora of the Parque Estadual do Espinilho, achieving the objective of correcting previous descriptive inaccuracies and providing necessary data for the elaboration of the Park’s Management Plan (Plano de Manejo). The research confirmed the importance of this conservation unit, whose vegetation is characterized as a 'Savana Estépica' or 'Estepe Gramíneo-Lenhosa' (Grass-Woody Steppe)—a disjunction of Chaquenha vegetation.

The final conclusions emphasized that the arboreal flora defining the park's landscape is overwhelmingly dominated by the unique association of *Prosopis affinis*, *P. nigra*, and *Acacia caven*. The study supports the view that the restricted occurrence of these species, particularly the *Prosopis* species, is fundamentally tied to specific edaphic characteristics (Gleissolo Melânico soils and localized water stress) rather than broader climatic factors. By documenting this unique, naturally occurring, and highly restricted formation defined by these thorny legumes, the study underscores the necessity of protecting this environment, recognizing its irreplaceable ecological role in the regional flora of Rio Grande do Sul and Brazil.

  • "The Parque area represents the only environment for occurrence of P.nigra and P.affinis in Rio Grande do Sul and in Brazil."
  • "Veloso et al. (1991) consideram a vegetação do Parque do Espinilho como formação tipo “Savana Estépica”, sendo uma disjunção da vegetação chaquenha."
  • "Waechter (2002) considera a presença de Acacia caven (Mol.) Mol. (espinilho) e Prosopis affinis Griseb. (inhaduvai), como a expressão máxima da flora chaquenha, no extremo sudoeste do Rio Grande do Sul."

Prosopis nigra (Griseb.) Hieron. e Acacia caven (Mol.) Mol., o que determina uma composição bastante simples caracterizado pelas suas associações."

Hieron. e Acacia caven (Mol.) Mol, com o aparecimento esporádico e aleatório de outras espécies arbóreas..."

Landscape change under indirect effects of human use: the Savanna of Central Chile

Eduardo R. Fuentes, Reinaldo Avilds and Alejandro Segura (1989)

Abstract

The Chilean Intermediate Depression to the north of Santiago has experienced a physiognomical transformation from a Prosopis chilensis woodland to an Acacia caven savanna. Today P. chilensis trees are scarce and belong mostly to the larger size classes. By contrast A. caven seems to reproduce frequently and its populations consist of individuals of all size classes. In this paper we document these changes and report the results of tests aimed at determining the causes of these physiognomical changes. We found that livestock, leporids, introduced Mediterranean forbs and agriculture account for differences in seed dispersal and survival of A. caven and P. chilensis, which can explain the documented changes in the Chilean landscape.

Main Points

The study documented a significant physiognomical transformation in the Central Chilean Valley, north of Santiago, from a historical *Prosopis chilensis* (*algarrobo*) woodland to an *Acacia caven* (*espino*) savanna. Methods utilized included temporal comparisons of tree densities using aerial photographs from 1954 and 1980, size-class analysis of current tree populations, and controlled germination and survival experiments to test the differential impacts of human land use factors.

Aerial photo comparisons showed that *Prosopis* density decreased by about 50% between 1954 and 1980. Size-class analysis indicated severe recruitment failure for *P. chilensis*, as younger size classes were absent. Conversely, *A. caven* showed robust reproduction with individuals across all size classes.

Crucially, experiments demonstrated that domestic herbivores (cows and goats) act as significant selective agents. Seed germination trials revealed that passing through the gut of these animals increased *A. caven* germination rates by about five times compared to controls, functioning as a scarification/dispersal mechanism. However, for *P. chilensis*, gut passage drastically lowered germination rates (to about 20% of controls), indicating that herbivores function primarily as seed predators for this species. Livestock also removed a substantial portion of the overall *Prosopis* seed crop.

Seedling survival experiments, using plots subjected to different disturbances (control, intermediate, vineyard conversion), confirmed that removal of mammalian herbivores and exotic herbaceous cover significantly increased recruitment for both species. Furthermore, *A. caven* exhibited high resilience to agricultural disturbance (cutting and plowing) through vigorous resprouting, a capability entirely absent in *P. chilensis* within the sampling area. The study thus concluded that the shift is driven by the differential response of the two species to chronic grazing and agriculture.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that the large-scale shift from *Prosopis chilensis* woodland to *Acacia caven* savanna is primarily a result of the long history of land use practices in the Central Valley, specifically the common sowing-grazing rotation practiced on the flat lands, rather than wood-cutting alone, since both species resprout vigorously after coppicing.

*Acacia caven*'s success in dominating the current landscape is attributed to its life history traits favoring survival under anthropogenic pressure. Its seeds are successfully dispersed and scarified by abundant domestic livestock, leading to high germination rates. Moreover, *A. caven* demonstrates vigorous resprouting after repeated disturbance associated with agricultural cycles (cutting and plowing).

In contrast, *Prosopis chilensis* recruitment is severely impeded by these same land use factors. Livestock consumption acts as a seed predator, significantly lowering germination rates, and *Prosopis* trees fail to resprout after agricultural disturbance, leading to recruitment failure and density reduction. The authors summarize that the indirect effects of human land use, particularly chronic grazing and agriculture, have fundamentally altered the ecological dynamics, favoring the persistence and spread of *A. caven* at the expense of *P. chilensis*, thereby explaining the profound landscape transformation.

Key Quotes

  • "The Chilean Intermediate Depression to the north of Santiago has experienced a physiognomical transformation from a Prosopis chilensis woodland to an Acacia caven savanna."
  • "Today P. chilensis trees are scarce and belong mostly to the larger size classes. By contrast A. caven seems to reproduce frequently and its populations consist of individuals of all size classes."
  • "We found that livestock, leporids, introduced Mediterranean forbs and agriculture account for differences in seed dispersal and survival of A. caven and P. chilensis, which can explain the documented changes in the Chilean landscape."
  • "But, during Spanish colonial times and until recently, the areas to the north of Santiago were not known as espinales but rather as 'algarrobales', or algarrobo woodlands (review in Looser 1962)."
  • "Prosopis density in 1980 was about 50% of the density in 1954."
  • "We found that on both flatlands and inselbergs the younger size classes of Prosopis are absent, indicating no recruitment in recent years."
  • "In comparison with the controls, cows and goats had a significant effect (ANOVA, P < 0.05) on increasing the germination of A. caven seeds."
  • "However, Prosopis germination was significantly lower (ANOVA, P < 0.01) for seeds passing through cows or goats than for the controls."
  • "The transformation... is clearly related to the long history of land use in the Central Valley, specifically the sowing-grazing rotation practiced on the flat lands."
  • "We did not find a single Prosopis resprout in our sampling."
Supports Conservation and Restoration StrategiesEcological Role and Nurse EffectsPampas Ecosystem Degradation and Resilience

Integrating datasets from herbarium specimens and images to treat a Neotropical myrtle species complex

Paulo Henrique Gaem, Giovani Carlos Andrella, Olivier Maurin, Volker Bittrich, Fiorella Fernanda Mazine, Eve Lucas and Maria do Carmo Estanislau do Amaral (2024)

Abstract

• Background and Aims Herbaria are the most important source of information for plant taxonomic work. Resources and technologies available today, such as digitized collections and herbarium DNA sequencing, can help accelerate taxonomic decisions in challenging plant groups. Here we employ an integrative methodology relying exclusively on herbarium specimens to investigate species boundaries in the Neotropical Myrcia neoobscura complex (Myrtaceae). • Methods We collected morphometric data from high-resolution images of herbarium sheets and analysed them using hierarchical clustering. We posteriorly tested the obtained morpho-groups with phylogenomics using the Angiosperms353 probe kit. We also gathered phenological and geographical information from specimen labels and built phenological histograms and ecological niche models to investigate ecological differences amongst taxa. • Key Results Current circumscriptions of Myrcia arenaria, Myrcia neoglabra and Myrcia neoregeliana are confirmed in this study. Conversely, the four pieces of evidence together support Calyptranthes langsdorffii var. grandiflora, Marlierea regeliana var. parviflora and Marlierea warmingiana as separate from Myrcia marliereana, Myrcia neoriedeliana and Myrcia neoobscura, respectively, contrary to arrangements proposed by previous authors. Integrated analyses also support separation between Myrcia excoriata and two similar, undescribed taxa. • Conclusions Our data reveal the need for major changes in the systematics of the group, with recognition of 12 species. The successful delivery of our study aims was possible due to obtaining robust, high-quality data from museum specimens. We emphasize the importance of maintaining botanical collections physically and digitally available for taxonomic work and advocate their use to accelerate taxonomic solutions of tropical species complexes hollistically. This is urgent, given the paucity of funds for fieldwork and unprecedented rates of habitat loss in the tropics.

Main Points

The study utilized an integrative methodology focused solely on herbarium specimens—including high-resolution images, metadata, and tissue—to resolve the systematics of the Neotropical *Myrcia neoobscura* species complex. The approach combined four types of data: morphology, phylogenomics, phenology, and Ecological Niche Models (ENMs).

For morphometrics, linear measurements and geometric morphometric data (outline analysis) were collected from 258 specimen images. These data were analyzed using hierarchical clustering to generate species hypotheses (morpho-groups). These initial groups were then tested using phylogenomics via 'herbariomics' (DNA sequencing using the Angiosperms353 probe kit).

Ecological context was provided by metadata: phenological histograms were built from collection dates, and ENMs were constructed using geographical coordinates to investigate ecological divergence and distribution preferences. The combined evidence confirmed the status of several accepted species (*M. arenaria*, *M. neoglabra*, *M. neoregeliana*) but resulted in major systematic changes. Notably, four lines of evidence supported the separation of taxa previously synonymized or complicated (*Calyptranthes langsdorffii var. grandiflora*, *Marlierea regeliana var. parviflora*, and *Marlierea warmingiana*) and confirmed the separation of *Myrcia excoriata* from two similar, yet undescribed taxa.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that their comprehensive data necessitate significant revisions in the systematics of the group, resulting in the recognition of 12 distinct species within the *Myrcia neoobscura* complex. They credit the successful completion of the study to the ability to extract robust, high-quality data exclusively from museum specimens, validating the integrative approach based on digitization and herbariomics.

The final conclusion strongly emphasizes the vital importance of maintaining botanical collections, both physically and digitally accessible, for taxonomic research. They advocate for this integrative use of collections to accelerate holistic taxonomic solutions for complex groups in the tropics. This work is deemed critically urgent, especially considering the current resource limitations for traditional fieldwork and the escalating, "unprecedented rates of habitat loss in the tropics."

Key Quotes

  • "Herbaria are the most important source of information for plant taxonomic work."
  • "Resources and technologies available today, such as digitized collections and herbarium DNA sequencing, can help accelerate taxonomic decisions in challenging plant groups."
  • "Here we employ an integrative methodology relying exclusively on herbarium specimens to investigate species boundaries in the Neotropical Myrcia neoobscura complex (Myrtaceae)."
  • "We also gathered phenological and geographical information from specimen labels and built phenological histograms and ecological niche models to investigate ecological differences amongst taxa."
  • "The successful delivery of our study aims was possible due to obtaining robust, high-quality data from museum specimens."
  • "We emphasize the importance of maintaining botanical collections physically and digitally available for taxonomic work and advocate their use to accelerate taxonomic solutions of tropical species complexes hollistically."
  • "This is urgent, given the paucity of funds for fieldwork and unprecedented rates of habitat loss in the tropics."
  • "The Anthropocene is marked by unprecedented human-induced biodiversity loss (Watson et al., 2016)."
  • "In general, information provided by herbarium specimens can help address biodiversity shortfalls, in particular the Darwinian (ignorance on evolutionary relationships), Linnean (ignorance on species numbers) and Wallacean (ignorance on species distributions) ones..."
  • "Because species are fundamental units of biodiversity, their accurate delimitations are critical to counter the ongoing biodiversity crisis, including the recognition of endemic taxa for prioritization in conservation initiatives."

Fire-mediated effects on polyploid biology

Kelsey L. Glennon, Hendrik J. Niemann, and Sally Archibald (2024)

Abstract

Cold temperatures have been pos- ited as a key driver of polyploidy (possession of multiple chromo- some sets). However, high temper- atures associated with ﬁre, and the indirect impact of post-ﬁre environ- ments in polypoid formation and es- tablishment deserve more attention for a comprehensive understanding of polyploid ecology, evolution, and current distributions.

Main Points

This Forum article proposes that fire, alongside cold temperatures, is a critical driver influencing the distribution, formation, establishment, and persistence of polyploid plants, particularly in fire-prone ecosystems like tropical savanna grasslands and temperate shrublands.

The authors analyze the mechanisms by which fire promotes polyploidy. For **Autopolyploid formation**, fire induces stress: high temperatures (30–50°C soil temperatures, common in surface fires) can disrupt meiosis and lead to unreduced gamete (UG) formation. Additionally, chemical stressors, such as mutagenic polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons (PAHs) released in smoke, cause spindle fiber aberrations, while liberated heavy metals and radioactive ions also induce mutations.

For **Allopolyploid formation**, fire often triggers synchronized post-fire flowering, which increases the chances of interspecific hybridization, especially in resprouter groups like grasses and geophytes. This enhanced hybridization, sometimes facilitated by fire-induced UG formation, promotes allopolyploidization.

Regarding **Establishment and Persistence**, fire plays a dual ecological role. Short-term effects include rapid recycling of burned biomass, increasing available soil nutrients (Nitrogen and Phosphorus) necessary for the initial establishment and growth of newly formed, nutrient-demanding polyploids. Long-term effects center on selection: fire acts as a strong ecological filter, selecting for stress-tolerant polyploids. Crucially, recurrent fire helps polyploid lineages overcome **Minority Cytotype Exclusion (MCE)** by providing a stochastic cue for synchronized formation. Fire regimes also select for the perennial resprouting life history, which ensures individual longevity, allows mutations to accumulate, and reduces mating bottlenecks, thereby promoting persistence.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that fire regimes are highly likely to be a crucial, underappreciated driver of polyploid ecology and evolution globally. They predict that frequent, low-intensity surface fire regimes—such as those dominating tropical grasslands and savannas (with short fire return periods, e.g., 1–2 years)—should exhibit the highest polyploidy rates. These conditions optimize temperature-induced UG formation and strongly select for perennial resprouting life forms, which are adapted to overcome MCE.

They suggest that examining polyploidy patterns across grasslands that vary in both cold exposure and fire frequency would help isolate the relative importance of these two major agents. The expectation that cold, fire-prone places (like the Eastern Canadian Shield taiga, shown in Box 1) would have the highest polyploidy instances is supported by existing data, suggesting cold and fire often act synergistically, both promoting resprouting life forms and providing nutrient pulses.

Given the rise of the 'Pyrocene' (increasing global fire frequency and intensity), the authors emphasize the urgency of experimental work to test specific fire-related mechanisms (intensity, nutrient availability, UG formation rates, and life history traits) to better understand fire's substantial role in shaping plant diversification and biogeographic patterns.

Key Quotes

  • "However, many tropical savanna grasslands and temperate shrublands also show high instances of polypoid species, despite rarely being exposed to extreme cold."

Savanna woody encroachment is widespread across three continents

N I C O L A S T E V E N S 1 , 2 , C A R O L I N E E . R . L E H M A N N 3 , B R E T T P . M U R P H Y 4 and G I S E L D A D U R I G A N 5 (2017)

Abstract

Tropical savannas are a globally extensive biome prone to rapid vegetation change in response to changing environ- mental conditions. Via a meta-analysis, we quantiﬁed savanna woody vegetation change spanning the last century. We found a global trend of woody encroachment that was established prior the 1980s. However, there is critical regional variation in the magnitude of encroachment. Woody cover is increasing most rapidly in the remaining uncleared savannas of South America, most likely due to ﬁre suppression and land fragmentation. In contrast, Aus- tralia has experienced low rates of encroachment. When accounting for land use, African savannas have a mean rate annual woody cover increase two and a half times that of Australian savannas. In Africa, encroachment occurs across multiple land uses and is accelerating over time. In Africa and Australia, rising atmospheric CO2, changing land management and rainfall are likely causes. We argue that the functional traits of each woody ﬂora, speciﬁcally the N-ﬁxing ability and architecture of woody plants, are critical to predicting encroachment over the next century and that African savannas are at high risk of widespread vegetation change.

Main Points

This paper presents a meta-analysis quantifying savanna woody vegetation change across Africa, Australia, and South America (specifically studies from the Brazilian cerrado) over the last century. The methodology involved compiling data from 94 studies covering 110 savanna sites, focusing on natural tropical and subtropical C4 grass-dominated systems, excluding areas subject to large-scale agriculture or afforestation/deforestation. Woody cover, biomass, or basal area recorded at two or more time points were used to calculate an annual increment of cover change. Basal area was converted to percent cover for Brazilian and Australian sites using established linear regressions.

The authors extracted metadata including climatic information (Mean Annual Precipitation, MAP), dominant land use (conservation, commercial/communal grazing, remnant), and functional traits of encroaching species, particularly recording N-fixing ability for plants in the Fabaceae family. Statistical analysis utilized Generalized Linear Models (GLMs), comparing candidate models using AICc, to test the effects of initial woody cover, continent, MAP, and study start year.

Results confirmed a global trend of woody encroachment in 84% of sites, established prior to the 1980s. Crucial regional variation was noted: Australia showed the lowest average rate (0.1% per year), while South America (Brazil) exhibited the highest mean rate of woody cover increase (0.7% per year, or 7% per decade). This rapid increase in South America was attributed primarily to fire suppression and land fragmentation. African savannas showed an average increase of 0.25% per year, accelerating over time, with rates 2.5 times higher than Australia when accounting for land use. The analysis found that local land use, such as heavy grazing and consequent reductions in fire frequency, promotes encroachment.

Conclusions

The study concludes that woody encroachment is a widespread symptom of alterations in ecosystem processes across the savanna biome, driven by global factors like rising atmospheric CO2 interacting critically with regional drivers such as changing land management, rainfall patterns, and the functional traits of the local woody flora. The rates of change are highly contingent on location and land-use history, leading to significant regional variation in the magnitude of encroachment.

Specifically, the authors argue that the functional traits of the encroaching species, particularly their N-fixing ability and architecture, are critical parameters for predicting the magnitude of vegetation change in the future. While global drivers like elevated CO2 can accelerate plant growth by increasing water-use efficiency, local factors such as fire suppression and heavy grazing facilitate encroachment by releasing woody plants from competition. The high rates observed in South America due to fire suppression and fragmentation, alongside accelerating encroachment in Africa, highlight that these regions face a high risk of widespread vegetation change. Understanding the interplay between land use, environmental setting, and species-specific traits is essential for predicting and managing the future state of the savanna biome.

  • "We argue that the functional traits of each woody ﬂora, speciﬁcally the N-ﬁxing ability and architecture of woody plants, are critical to predicting encroachment over the next century..."
  • "Local land use such as heavy grazing and consequent reductions in ﬁre frequency and intensity promotes encroachment by releasing woody plants from grass competition..."
  • "Woody encroachment, the increase in woody biomass, stem densities or woody cover, is a symptom of an alteration in ecosystem processes..."
  • "Savanna woody encroachment is widespread across three continents"
  • "We found a global trend of woody encroachment that was established prior the 1980s."
  • "The vulnerability of a region to encroachment will therefore depend on both the prevailing land-use history, the environmental setting, and the functional traits of woody plants that likely govern their responsiveness to both local (e.g. sensitivity to ﬁre) and global drivers (e.g. growth response to elevated [CO2])."
  • "If plants belonged to the family Fabaceae, we recorded if the plants had the potential to ﬁx atmospheric nitrogen (Sprent & Platzmann, 2001) (Appendix S4)."
  • "Rising [CO2] can accelerate woody plant growth through increasing available soil water through improving plant water-use efﬁciencies (Polley et al., 1997; Leakey et al., 2009)..."
  • "In Africa, the other predominant land use is communally owned subsistence farming (communal rangelands)... In communal rangelands, communities rely on the land for natural resources and grazing."

Woody Plant-Cover Dynamics in Argentine Savannas from the 1880s to 2000s: The Interplay of Encroachment and Agriculture Conversion at Varying Scales

Mariano Gonza´lez-Roglich, Jennifer J. Swenson, Diego Villarreal, Esteban G. Jobba´gy, and Robert B. Jackson (2015)

Abstract

Woody plant-cover dynamics can alter the provisioning of ecosystem services that humans rely on. However, our understanding of such dynamics today is often limited by the availability of reliable and detailed land-cover information in the past, before the onset of remote sensing technologies. In this study, we carefully extracted information from historical maps of the Caldenal savannas of central Argentina in the 1880s to generate a woody cover map that we compared to a 2000s dataset. Over about the last 120 years, woody cover increased across approximately 12,200 km2 (14.2% of the area). During the same period, about 5,000 km2 of the original woody area was converted to croplands and around 7,000 km2 to pastures, about the same total land area as was affected by encroachment. A smaller area, ﬁne-scale analysis between the 1960s and the 2000s revealed that tree cover increased overall by 27%, shifting from open savannas to a mosaic of dense woodlands along with additional agricultural clearings. Statistical models indicate that woody cover dynamics in this region were affected by a combination of environmental and human factors. Over about the last 120 years, increases in woody plant cover have stored signif-icant amounts of C (95.9 TgC), but not enough to compensate for losses from conversions to crop-lands and pastures (166.7 TgC), generating a regional net loss of 70.9 TgC. C losses could be even larger in the future if, as predicted, energy crops such as switchgrass, would trigger a new land-cover change phase in this region.

Main Points

The study analyzed the complex woody plant-cover dynamics in the Caldenal savannas of central Argentina (dominated by the caldén tree, *Prosopis caldenia*) over a period of 120 years (1880s–2000s). The researchers leveraged historical maps from systematic land surveys conducted in the 1880s to create a baseline woody cover map, employing a 'Points method' grid along township perimeters. This baseline was contrasted with modern land-cover data from the early 2000s National Forest Inventory.

Regionally, the results showed massive, simultaneous land-cover shifts. Woody plant encroachment (WPE) led to an increase in woody cover across 12,200 km² (14.2% of the study area). Concurrently, an almost identical total area (12,000 km²) of original woody cover was cleared and converted, specifically 5,000 km² to croplands and 7,000 km² to pastures. A finer-scale analysis comparing 1960s aerial photographs to 2000s CBERS images confirmed a 27% increase in tree cover overall, transforming open savannas into a mosaic of dense woodlands and agricultural clearings.

Statistical modeling indicated that these woody cover dynamics were driven by a combination of environmental variables and human factors, including local land use, the introduction of cattle, and changes in fire regimes. Crucially, the analysis of carbon stocks revealed that while WPE resulted in significant C storage (95.9 TgC), the C losses from conversion to agriculture (166.7 TgC) far exceeded this gain, resulting in a net regional carbon loss of 70.9 TgC. This finding underscores the highly negative impact of agricultural conversion on the regional ecosystem.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that the Argentine savannas are defined by highly active and simultaneous processes of woody plant encroachment and agricultural conversion. WPE, linked predominantly to human factors like the introduction of livestock and altered fire regimes, results in a shift toward denser woody cover and associated C sequestration. However, the expansive agricultural frontier aggressively cleared vast areas for crops and pastures.

The most significant consequence of these simultaneous dynamics is a net negative impact on regional carbon stocks. The substantial C losses caused by converting native woodlands to agriculture (166.7 TgC) could not be offset by the C stored through WPE (95.9 TgC), resulting in a net loss of 70.9 TgC over the 120-year period. The study highlights the vulnerability of the Caldenal ecosystem, concluding that future land-cover changes driven by economic pressures, such as the potential expansion of energy crops like switchgrass, pose an ongoing risk of further substantial C losses and ecosystem degradation.

  • "During the same period, about 5,000 km2 of the original woody area was converted to croplands and around 7,000 km2 to pastures, about the same total land area as was affected by encroachment."
  • "Statistical models indicate that woody cover dynamics in this region were affected by a combination of environmental and human factors."
  • "Over about the last 120 years, increases in woody plant cover have stored signif-icant amounts of C (95.9 TgC), but not enough to compensate for losses from conversions to crop-lands and pastures (166.7 TgC), generating a regional net loss of 70.9 TgC."
  • "WPE is considered one of the most extensive forms of rangeland land-cover change (Blaum and others 2007), affecting vast portions of arid and semiarid regions world-wide (Asner and others 2004)."
  • "It has been related to local land use and management (Lunt and others 2010), with the introduction of cattle and changes in ﬁre regimes being two of the most commonly cited drivers (Asner and others 2004)."
  • "Woody plant clearing and encroachment are two processes usu-ally studied independently, but active agriculture frontiers in semiarid regions, where both occur simultaneously, offer a unique opportunity for understanding complex woody plant-cover dynamics and their consequences."
  • "Changes in land cover affect the persistence of biodiversity, soil properties, and water cycles, and modify climate at varying spatial and temporal scales, ultimately affecting the provision of ecosystem services on which humans rely."
  • "The semiarid savannas of central Argentina offer a unique opportunity to study long-term woody plant-cover dynamics in an agriculture frontier region where both WPE and clearing for agriculture have occurred."
Supports Pampas Ecosystem Degradation and ResilienceGrazing and Land Use ImpactsConservation and Restoration Strategies

Holocene coastal ecosystem development in Eastern Uruguay: a multidisciplinary analysis of occurrences of Butia palms

Eduardo T. Salgado, Margot Guerra-Sommer, Ana C. R. de Albergaria-Barbosa, Débora S. Z. Watanabe, Anderson M. Araújo do Nascimento, Ana M. P. Mizusaki, Heloísa H. G. Coe, Laura del Puerto, Mercedes Rivas (2025)

Abstract

The coastal region of eastern Uruguay has a native and endangered ecosystem with Butia palms called butiazais. In order to find out about the formation of this vegetation, a multi-proxy study was done to analyse two sedimentary cores from this region, at Laguna de Castillos and Laguna Negra. Radiocarbon dating indicates that the analysed sediments were deposited over the last 12,000 years. The phytoliths, charcoal, sediments and geochemistry were studied to reconstruct environmental dynamics, showing the main geomorphological, climatic and ecological changes. The data suggest a complex interaction between the climate, sea level changes, vegetation dynamics and fires. At the Pleistocene–Holocene transition the climate was cold and wet, and trees and palms expanded accompanied by limited local fires with some evidence of regional fires. The temperature and precipitation increased in the early to mid-Holocene, coinciding with the Post-Glacial Marine Transgression (PMT) that led to a reduction in trees and Butia palms due to edaphic changes. Fire activity during this period suggests an interplay between vegetation changes and climate. In the late Holocene there was a change from C3 to C4 plants indicated by isotope data, with increased fire activity from natural and human causes. The expansion of grasslands probably led to fire-prone conditions, even with a wetter climate. The results provide new insights into the response of coastal ecosystems to past climatic events, with implications for predicting future environmental responses to ongoing climate change.

Main Points

The researchers conducted a multi-proxy study on two sedimentary cores (U01 and U02) from coastal Uruguay, covering the last 12,000 years, to reconstruct the environmental history and the development of the native *Butia* palm ecosystem (*butiazais*). The methodologies included radiocarbon dating, particle size analysis, phytolith and sponge spicule analysis (using indices like Pa/P for palm abundance and D/P for tree cover), and fire proxies (micro/macrocharcoal, PAHs). Stable isotope analysis of carbonates provided data on vegetation types (C3 vs. C4 plants).

Results revealed dynamic ecosystem changes. During the Pleistocene–Holocene transition, cold/wet conditions favored the expansion of trees and palms, with limited fire. The early to mid-Holocene saw warmer and wetter conditions, but the Post-Glacial Marine Transgression (PMT) caused edaphic changes (likely salinization) that resulted in a reduction of trees and *Butia* palms, demonstrating that sea level/soil conditions sometimes override climatic trends. The late Holocene was characterized by a shift from C3 to C4 dominance, signifying grassland expansion. This change, alongside increasing fire activity attributed explicitly to both **natural and human causes**, led to fire-prone conditions. The overall finding is a complex interaction where geomorphology, climate, fire, and human activity shaped the fate of these palms.

Conclusions

The study concludes that the development and current state of the *butiazais* are defined by a complex interplay of environmental factors and anthropogenic influence throughout the Holocene. A key finding is that the expansion of *Butia* palms was not synchronous with early Holocene climate warming; rather, it was curtailed by local edaphic changes caused by the Post-Glacial Marine Transgression (PMT).

Crucially, the late Holocene record indicates increasing human involvement, specifically linked to higher fire activity, alongside natural processes. The current threat faced by these palms—a significant reduction in extent and a lack of regeneration—is directly linked back to contemporary 'environmental changes and human land management.' By providing a detailed timeline of how the coastal ecosystem responded to past events (like sea-level change and shifting fire regimes), the research aims to inform conservation strategies necessary to protect the endangered *butiazais* from future climate change impacts.

  • "The temperature and precipitation increased in the early to mid-Holocene, coinciding with the Post-Glacial Marine Transgression (PMT) that led to a reduction in trees and Butia palms due to edaphic changes."
  • "In the late Holocene there was a change from C3 to C4 plants indicated by isotope data, with increased fire activity from natural and human causes."

  • "But despite their significance, they are currently threatened due to a lack of regeneration caused by environmental changes and human land management."

  • "This delay in vegetation expansion in the early Holocene appears to have been related to fluctuations in sea level."

Jornada de difusión de resultados sobre el avance y control de Espinillo (Vachellia caven) en áreas de aptitud pastoril.

INTA Regional Sabandía, Forraje Referente Regional de INTA en Río Negro, et al. (2023)

Abstract

Esta intervención territorial (INTA) forma parte de una propuesta interinstitucional que busca el aprovechamiento sostenible en áreas de espinillo (Vachellia caven) en suelos de aptitud pastoril/ganadera. Se apunta a obtener información consistente sobre el estado de situación del avance del espinillo y la eficacia de algunas prácticas de manejo en áreas donde esta especie se comporta como 'invasor'. La comparación de los datos permitirá identificar las técnicas que mejor se adaptan a las características biofísicas y sociales del territorio. A través de la difusión de los resultados, se busca sensibilizar a productores y productoras, técnicos y técnicas, sobre los manejos más eficientes y promover su adopción. El Forraje Referente Regional de INTA en Río Negro, nos cuenta sobre la actividad. Sirvió para intercambiar los conocimientos que se van generando al manejo sustentable de la especie, para ir cambiando el paradigma de considerarlo como un enemigo, a ir tomándolo como un aliado en la generación de áreas de pastoreo ganadero sustentable.

Main Points

The document reports on a territorial intervention project led by INTA, focused on balancing livestock production with the presence of *Vachellia caven* (espinillo) in pastoral zones. The project aims to achieve sustainable use in these areas, particularly where the species is perceived and managed as an 'invasive' problem species.

The core activity involved a technical workshop or 'Jornada' where preliminary data concerning various espinillo control treatment models (tratamiento de control de espinillo) were presented and compared. The goal was to identify management techniques best suited to the specific biophysical and social characteristics of the local territory. The initiative employed a participatory approach, involving producers (male and female), technical staff, and institutional representatives from the San Javier and Río Negro departments.

Specific results from the project were intended to serve as direct inputs for the productive planning of a local INTA property managed by a women's collective. The overall strategy emphasizes the dissemination of efficient management practices to producers and technicians to encourage their widespread adoption.

Conclusions

The primary conclusion highlighted is the necessity and opportunity for a fundamental shift in the regional perception of *Vachellia caven*. The knowledge exchange fostered during the activity was instrumental in moving 'changing the paradigm from considering it an enemy, to taking it as an ally' in the context of generating sustainable livestock grazing areas (pastoreo ganadero sustentable).

Despite this crucial change in perspective, the report acknowledges that the results presented are preliminary. The authors conclude that further field research is required, necessitating the continuation of the experimental trials beyond the initial established timeframe to draw conclusions with greater certainty and ensure the reliability of the recommended sustainable management strategies.

Supports Ecological Role and Nurse EffectsGrazing and Land Use ImpactsWoody Encroachment Dynamics

Cattle and nurse trees shape subtropical forest–grassland ecotones

Milena Holmgren, Edwin Bargeman, Rafael Bernardi, Alice Blok, Jasper Buijs, Ivan Raniero Hernández-Salmerón, Rocío Martínez-Cillero, Nestor Mazzeo, Bart Verdijck (2024)

Abstract

1. South American subtropical landscapes are dominated by open grasslands and mosaics of forest–grassland formations. Forests are often restricted to riverine margins with sharp forest–grassland ecotones. Understanding the mechanisms maintaining forest–grassland ecotones is important to anticipate the effects of changing climate and disturbance regimes on the extent of these biomes and the ecosystem services they provide. 2. We used a combination of field surveys and long- term field experiments to explore the mechanisms that explain tree cover expansion at the ecotone of riverine forests and grasslands in central Uruguay, within the South American Campos. We assessed the role of tree seed dispersal and seedling establishment limitations, and experimentally tested for the effects of cattle, nurse tree cover and grasses on the recruitment of forest and grassland tree species at the forest– grassland ecotone. 3. We found that forest expansion depends on the interplay between cattle and nurse trees. Vachellia caven trees colonize the grassland successfully and facilitate the formation of forest patches by enhancing seed accumulation and seedling establishment of forest tree species. Surprisingly, grass cover had mostly positive effects on early seedling survival of forest tree seedlings. However, cattle limits tree seedling growth and survival, especially of forest tree species. This results in a nucleated vegetation pattern of tree patches that ultimately limits forest expansion. 4. Synthesis and applications. Tree cover can potentially expand on the subtropical South American grasslands. Reductions in cattle densities and increases in rainfall levels associated with climate change could facilitate forest expansion in this region.

Main Points

The study investigated the mechanisms governing tree cover expansion at the forest-grassland ecotone in the South American Campos (central Uruguay) using combined field surveys and long-term experiments. The field surveys quantified the structure and composition of 1744 tree patches, ranging from solitary trees to large patches, and assessed seed rain and soil seed banks across these habitats. Functional traits like SLA, leaf toughness, and spinescence were measured to explore recruitment success.

Results showed that *Vachellia caven* is the dominant solitary tree initiating colonization in the grassland. Crucially, the study confirmed that *Vachellia caven* acts as an effective nurse plant, successfully enhancing both seed accumulation and the subsequent seedling establishment of forest tree species, thereby initiating the formation of multi-specific tree patches.

Experimental results identified the main constraints on forest expansion. While grass cover surprisingly had positive effects on early seedling survival, cattle browsing proved to be the major limiting factor. Cattle significantly reduced the growth and survival of tree seedlings, particularly those belonging to forest species. This dynamic—facilitation by nurse trees coupled with intensive limitation by cattle—results in a characteristic 'nucleated vegetation pattern' that prevents the widespread, continuous expansion of the forest boundary.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that forest expansion in the subtropical South American grasslands is critically dependent on the interaction between facilitation (provided by nurse trees like *Vachellia caven*) and high levels of disturbance (caused by cattle grazing). *Vachellia caven* plays a fundamental role in ecological succession by overcoming establishment limitations for less hardy forest species, validating its importance as a pioneer and facilitator species.

However, chronic cattle grazing intercepts this successional pathway. Herbivory limits the growth and survival of recruiting trees, preventing the coalescing of patches and thereby maintaining the sharp forest-grassland ecotone and the nucleated pattern of tree cover.

The synthesis suggests that tree cover possesses the potential for widespread expansion into these grasslands. The authors emphasize that this expansion is primarily constrained by anthropogenic disturbance, not climate or competition. Therefore, implementing management practices focused on reductions in cattle densities is crucial for allowing tree cover to expand and for the effective conservation and restoration of these forest systems. They also suggest that climate change (increased rainfall) could synergistically facilitate this expansion if grazing is controlled.

  • "Vachellia caven trees colonize the grassland successfully and facilitate the formation of forest patches by enhancing seed accumulation and seedling establishment of forest tree species."
  • "We found that forest expansion depends on the interplay between cattle and nurse trees."
  • "High livestock density across the South American Campos has been identified as a major driver keeping lower levels of tree cover..."
  • "However, cattle limits tree seedling growth and survival, especially of forest tree species. This results in a nucleated vegetation pattern of tree patches that ultimately limits forest expansion."
  • "Reductions in cattle densities and increases in rainfall levels associated with climate change could facilitate forest expansion in this region."
  • "We hypothesised that forest expansion in grasslands is facilitated by isolated nurse trees and limited by cattle browsing and grass competition."
  • "Vachellia caven (Fabaceae, ex. Acacia caven) is the most abundant solitary tree species growing in the grassland."
  • "Forest expansion on grasslands depends on the interplay of biotic processes, environmental conditions and disturbance regimes affecting the likelihood of trees to successfully establish and persist in the grassland."
  • "Under low cattle densities, forest–grassland ecotones become shrubbier as an early indicator of woody plant expansion (Etchebarne- Palla et al., 2022)."
  • "Livestock browsing and competition with grasses can strongly limit tree seedling establishment in subtropical grassy biomes (Macias et al., 2014)."
Supports Ecological Role and Nurse EffectsPampas Ecosystem Degradation and ResilienceGrazing and Land Use Impacts

Large- scale facilitative effects for a single nurse shrub: Impact of the rainfall gradient, plant community and distribution across a geographical barrier

Nicolás Velasco, Cristina Soto- Agurto, Lucas Carbone, Cesar Massi, Ramiro Bustamante, Christian Smit (2024)

Abstract

1. Importance of nurse plants structuring plant communities is well- appreciated at local scales, yet the effect of a single nurse on large scales has been neglected in analyses. So far, studies only use environmental gradients within one type of ecosystem and tend to generalize the nurse effects. 2. To assess how the effect of a single nurse species is modulated by different environ- mental settings, interactions between the shrub Vachellia caven and the surrounding plant communities were evaluated at 481 paired plots (outside vs. underneath the plant crown), in 39 sites across two distribution ranges, the Mediterranean west and the mostly subtropical east of the Andes Mountains (covering ca. 2 × 106 km2). 3. Cover, abundance and richness of perennial plants underneath and outside V. caven were used as response variables to estimate an index indicative of plant interactions (relative interaction index [RII]) and tested how this was affected by the rainfall gradient and distribution range. 4. Overall, RII responses to rainfall gradients had low conditional R2 (~0.25) in this large scale of analysis, but were significantly different between ranges: the RII followed a quadratic trend across the rainfall gradient in the western range, while this relationship was positive and close to linear at the eastern range. 5. Then, by projecting the RII models (i.e. for abundance, cover and richness) spa- tially through a consensus map, we show that most positive effects of V. caven are geographically found in dissimilar areas: the central part of Chile (western range) and across the Paraná River (eastern range). 6. When local fine- scale predictors (i.e. annual herbs' cover and height, and herbi- vores' faeces cover) were used to model each response variable at the plot level (underneath or outside V. caven), we observed similar trends as when we consid- ered only the large- scale predictors. 7. Synthesis. Here, we show that the effect of the same nurse species on neighbour- ing plant communities can be very different depending on ranges of distribution, stressing that its ecological function cannot be generalized and not only depends on local factors but also is large- scale context- dependent.

Main Points

The study was designed to evaluate the large-scale facilitative effects of the shrub *Vachellia caven* by comparing plant community metrics (cover, abundance, and richness of perennial plants) underneath versus outside the crown across 481 paired plots spread across 39 sites in South America. The study area spanned approximately $2 \times 10^6 \text{ km}^2$, encompassing two major distribution ranges separated by the Andes: the Mediterranean western range (Chile) and the mostly subtropical eastern range (Argentina and Paraguay).

The researchers calculated the Relative Interaction Index (RII) to quantify the net ecological effect. They tested RII response against large-scale variables (rainfall gradient, distribution range) and fine-scale variables (local herb cover, height, and herbivores' faeces cover). Results indicated that while the overall RII models had low explanatory power at this massive scale (conditional $R^2 \approx 0.25$), the interaction patterns were significantly different between the two ranges. In the western range, the RII displayed a quadratic (humped) trend against the rainfall gradient, aligning with modified Stress Gradient Hypothesis predictions. Crucially for the thesis, the eastern range exhibited a positive and nearly linear relationship between RII and rainfall, suggesting consistent facilitation under wetter conditions.

Spatially projecting these RII models showed that the most substantial positive effects of *V. caven* were concentrated in geographically distinct areas: central Chile (western range) and across the Paraná River (eastern range). Furthermore, the models incorporating fine-scale local factors showed trends consistent with those derived from large-scale predictors, indicating that local interactions are nested within and modulated by the broader geographical context.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that the ecological function of *Vachellia caven* as a nurse species is far from generalized; its effect on neighboring plant communities is highly context-dependent, varying significantly based on the distribution range. This underscores the importance of continental-scale analyses for understanding plant-plant interactions.

Specifically, the findings emphasize that *V. caven*'s ecological role is determined not only by local factors (like microhabitat modification and herbivore protection) but also by large-scale climatic context, such as the different rainfall seasonality patterns found east versus west of the Andes. The contrasting patterns—quadratic RII response in the west and positive/linear response in the east—demonstrate that the same species can follow different conceptual ecological frameworks (like the SGH) depending on its geographical location, reinforcing the view that generalized assumptions about its role should be avoided.

  • "Importance of nurse plants structuring plant communities is well- appreciated at local scales, yet the effect of a single nurse on large scales has been neglected in analyses."
  • "To assess how the effect of a single nurse species is modulated by different environmental settings, interactions between the shrub Vachellia caven and the surrounding plant communities were evaluated at 481 paired plots..."

  • "Then, by projecting the RII models (i.e. for abundance, cover and richness) spatially through a consensus map, we show that most positive effects of V. caven are geographically found in dissimilar areas..."
  • "We show that most positive effects of V. caven are geographically found in dissimilar areas: the central part of Chile (western range) and across the Paraná River (eastern range)."

Florística e fitofisionomias da planície de inundação do rio Piratini e a sua importância para conservação no Pampa do Rio Grande do Sul, Brasil

Ricardo V. Kilca, João A. Jarenkow, Júlio C. W. Soares, Élen N. Garcia (2012)

Abstract

A planície de inundação do rio Piratini (PIRP) localiza-se na região litorânea do Pampa no Rio Grande do Sul e recentemente foi considerada como uma área prioritária para conservação no bioma. Em vista das poucas informações sobre seus recursos naturais, o presente estudo objetiva inventariar a flora vascular, descrever, identificar e quantificar as principais fitofisionomias nativas e antrópicas e discutir a relevância dessas informações para a conservação da biodiversidade em escala regional no bioma. Os principais tipos fitofisionômicos foram localizados e identificados em fotografias aéreas utilizando critérios básicos de fotointerpretação, com verificações e correções feitas em campo, sendo, nessa etapa, ainda caracterizadas pelos modos de vida das espécies mais densas, pelo ambiente em que se encontram e pelo estado de conservação de cada fitofisionomia. Nas formações florestais, foram realizadas coletas botânicas. Foram definidas cinco fitofisionomias naturais e três antrópicas subdivididas em 10 subtipos fisionômicos distintos. A cobertura atual de florestas nativas na PIRP é de 4.376,83 ha, sendo a segunda maior área remanescente no litoral do Pampa. Nessas florestas, foram inventariadas 199 espécies vasculares, distribuídas entre 146 gêneros e 71 famílias, com espécies incluídas na categoria de ameaçadas, com distribuição restrita na região ou endêmicas. Na PIRP, estiveram representadas as principais fitofisionomias da região costeira do Pampa. Os resultados corroboram a indicação do Ministério do Meio Ambiente, de a área da PIRP constituir-se como de prioridade alta para conservação no bioma.

Main Points

The study aimed to characterize and quantify the vegetation (phytophysiognomies) of the Piratini River Floodplain (PIRP) to provide technical support for its conservation status within the Pampa biome, addressing the recognized lack of scientific information in the region.

**Methods:** The primary methods involved photointerpretation of 1:20,000 scale 1987 aerial photographs, where vegetation patterns were identified based on standard criteria (shape, size, texture, etc.). Field excursions (1999–2001) were used for verification, geographic location (GPS), assessing conservation status, and noting anthropogenic disturbances. Planimetric calculations derived from the photomosaic provided estimates of the extent of native forests and individual vegetation types. Floristic inventories, focusing on vascular species, were specifically conducted in forest communities (riparian and non-riparian) across varying soil humidity and successional stages.

**Results:** The researchers defined five native and three anthropic phytophysiognomies, subdivided into 10 distinct subtypes. Key categories included native flooded forests, native herbaceous-shrub (including 'matorral espinhoso' or spiny scrub), anthropic fields under grazing ('campos antrópicos sob pastejo'), and rice cultivation ('lavouras de arroz'). Quantitatively, the PIRP area showed that anthropic fields under grazing represented the largest land cover (3,040 ha), followed closely by periodically flooded riparian forests (2,861 ha) and rice crops (1,534 ha).

Native forest cover measured 4,376.83 ha in 2006, suggesting a 3.51% increase since 1987, although the authors note that the 'greater intensities of perturbation' likely occurred before the 1980s. The floristic survey within these forests documented 199 vascular species, distributed across 146 genera and 71 families, including species classified as threatened, restricted, or endemic. These results confirmed that PIRP hosts the major phytophysiognomies of the coastal Pampa.

Conclusions

The findings strongly corroborate the Ministry of Environment's recommendation that the Piratini River Floodplain (PIRP) constitutes an area of high priority for conservation in the Pampa biome. This importance is driven by several factors: PIRP hosts the main representative phytophysiognomies of the coastal Pampa region, ensuring regional ecological representation. Furthermore, the associated floodplain and riparian forests are identified as critical strategic areas, as they contain the 'last representatives of the tropical silvatic flora' that extend into the biome, highlighting their role as vital refuges.

Despite its ecological richness (199 vascular species, including threatened taxa), the region is severely impacted by intense land use conversion, evidenced by the vast areas dedicated to 'anthropic fields under grazing' (3,040 ha) and rice farming. The study successfully filled a crucial knowledge gap regarding the flora and vegetation structure, providing necessary technical subsidies to justify and guide future biodiversity conservation efforts within this highly vulnerable biome.

  • "Em nível de conservação, apresenta o menor percentual de área protegida e um dos mais carentes em informações científicas (MMA, 2000; 2002)."
  • "Essa região é de grande importância para conservação da biodiversidade, pois comporta, além das lagoas, distintos ecossistemas..."
  • "As planícies de inundação e as matas ciliares associadas são estratégicas para a conservação dos recursos florestais na região, pois essas comportam os últimos representantes da flora silvática tropical que chegam ao bioma..."
  • "A carência de informações a respeito da vegetação e flora na PIRP... levou ao desenvolvimento do presente estudo..."
  • "Os resultados corroboram a indicação do Ministério do Meio Ambiente, de a área da PIRP constituir-se como de prioridade alta para conservação no bioma."

Supports Conservation and Restoration StrategiesPampas Ecosystem Degradation and ResilienceWoody Encroachment Dynamics

A REGIÃO DO PARQUE DO ESPINILHO E A BACIA HIDROGRÁFICA DO RIO QUARAÍ, SEGUNDO O INVENTÁRIO FLORESTAL CONTÍNUO DO RIO GRANDE DO SUL. CONSIDERAÇÕES FINAIS

JOSÉ NEWTON CARDOSO MARCHIORI, FABIANO DA SILVA ALVES (2012)

Abstract

São apontados graves erros no Relatório Final do Inventário Florestal Contínuo do Rio Grande do Sul, no tocante aos capítulos da Região do Parque Espinilho e Bacia Hidrográfica do Rio Quaraí. Palavras-chave: Aspidosperma quebrachoblanco, Bacia Hidrográfica do Rio Quaraí, Inventário Florestal Contínuo, Parque Espinilho, Prosopis affinis, Prosopis nigra, Rio Grande do Sul, Vachellia caven.

Main Points

The study conducts a critical analysis of the 2001 Continuous Forest Inventory of Rio Grande do Sul (IFC-RS), focusing on the data presented for the Espinilho Parkland Region and the Quaraí River Hydrographic Basin, asserting that the report contains "grave errors."

Regarding the Espinilho Park Region (Table 1), the authors demonstrate that the IFC-RS provided "absolutely unreal" data, incorrectly indicating *Prosopis nigra* as the most abundant species (63.2 trees/ha), followed by *Vachellia caven* (42.0 trees/ha), when local reality confirms *Prosopis affinis* (inhanduvá) as the characteristic and most abundant woody species. This error is blamed on a primary failure of adequate sampling, possibly concentrating efforts only in small areas like the "Algarrobal."

For the Quaraí River Hydrographic Basin (Table 2), the errors are deemed "even more serious and unjustifiable." The IFC-RS claimed 28.73 *P. nigra* trees/ha (making it the fifth most abundant) and 27.27 *V. caven* trees/ha. The authors categorically reject this, noting that *P. nigra* is extremely rare, if not absent, in the vast majority of the basin, having never been found outside the ecologically small Pontal do Quaraí, where most natural vegetation has been replaced by rice paddies. The extrapolation suggests over a million *P. nigra* trees in the basin, confirming the data as fictional.

The most damning evidence is the discovery that the percentage ratio of *Prosopis affinis* abundance to *Prosopis nigra* abundance is exactly 56.3% (with decimal precision) in both the Espinilho Park data (Table 1) and the Quaraí Basin data (Table 2). This "extremely strange coincidence" proves that the flawed sample data from the small Park area was transposed (fabricated) to represent the entire large Hydrographic Basin, rendering the latter chapter entirely unreliable.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that the IFC-RS report, despite being a voluminous document used as a reference for general forest and vegetation studies in Rio Grande do Sul, contains data that is fundamentally incorrect, misleading, and in the case of the Quaraí Basin, amounts to a "mere piece of fiction."

They emphasize that species like *Vachellia caven* (espinilho) are widely conspicuous and abundant throughout the Campanha region, occurring across both sedimentary and basaltic substrates, which contradicts the low relative abundance indicated by the flawed inventory compared to the geographically restricted *Prosopis nigra*. This highlights a profound failure of the official inventory to accurately reflect the widespread natural ecological reality of key Pampas woody species. The authors challenge the IFC-RS creators to provide the geographic coordinates of the sample areas that supposedly generated the questionable data for the Quaraí Basin, asserting that such highly abundant *Prosopis nigra* areas simply do not exist there. The paper serves to invalidate this official government report and prevent its continued misuse as a reference for conservation and management strategies.

  • "O Inventário Florestal Contínuo do Rio Grande do Sul fornece dados absolutamente irreais sobre a 'Região do Parque Espinilho'."
  • "Os valores indicados contradizem toda a literatura existente, bem como a realidade local, facilmente apreendida até mesmo em rápida visita à região."

  • "No capítulo que trata da Bacia do Rio Quaraí, todavia, o Inventário Florestal Contínuo do Rio Grande do Sul não esconde erros ainda mais graves e injustificáveis..."
  • "O número de indivíduos de algarrobo (Prosopis nigra) que o documento afirma existir nesta bacia é extraordinariamente elevado, uma vez que a espécie nunca foi encontrada, no Rio Grande do Sul, fora do Pontal do Quaraí..."
  • "o espinilho (Vachellia caven) é o de mais ampla distribuição geográfica do Rio Grande do Sul, motivo pelo qual se pode afirmar que não tem fundamento o número de árvores/ha relativamente baixo indicado para a espécie na Tabela 2..."
  • "Na Bacia do rio Quaraí, o espinilho é espécie largamente conspícua e abundante, sobretudo na transição da mata ciliar para o campo limpo, e tanto em terrenos sedimentares como de origem basáltica."
