El Uso/Cobertura del suelo en Uruguay y los Pastizales del Río de la Plata: caracterización, análisis de sus cambios a lo largo del tiempo e impactos sobre el funcionamiento ecosistémico

Santiago Baeza (2016)

Abstract

El impacto humano sobre los ecosistemas ha alcanzado dimensiones tan grandes que es considerado uno de los factores principales del cambio global. En particular, los cambios en el uso del suelo son el principal agente de cambio debido a sus efectos sobre los ciclos biogeoquímicos, el clima y la biodiversidad. América Latina en general, y los Pastizales del Rio de la Plata (PRP) en particular, son una de las regiones del mundo con mayores tasas de cambio en el uso/cobertura del suelo en los últimos tiempos. A pesar de la magnitud de este proceso de cambio, las descripciones de uso/cobertura del suelo en los PRP distan mucho de ser completas, más aun aquellas que evalúan su cambio a través del tiempo. Para evaluar el impacto de dichos cambios es imprescindible describirlos con precisión. El objetivo de esta tesis es describir el uso/cobertura del suelo en Uruguay y los PRP, analizar sus cambios a lo largo del tiempo y evaluar el impacto de estos cambios sobre el funcionamiento de los ecosistemas en términos de las ganancias de carbono y su apropiación por los humanos. En primer lugar se desarrollaron metodologías para clasificar el uso/cobertura del suelo sobre grandes extensiones de territorio a partir de su fenología, utilizando extensas bases de datos de campo y series temporales de imágenes de satélite, generando un mapa de uso/cobertura del suelo para todo el territorio uruguayo. En segundo lugar, mediante un experimento manipulativo y el uso de sensores hiperespectrales, se desarrollaron modelos para discriminar y cuantificar densidades variables de arbustos en una matriz de pastizales naturales, un insumo fundamental para lograr cartografiar una categoría particularmente compleja que permitirá explorar la arbustización en pastizales, un proceso de gran interés ecológico, debido a sus impactos y a lo incierto de sus causas. En tercer lugar, se utilizan las técnicas desarrolladas para cartografiar el uso/cobertura del suelo sobre grandes superficies, para analizar el uso/cobertura del suelo en los PRP y sus cambios a lo largo del tiempo, en un momento en que la región está sometida a un fuerte proceso de intensificación de la producción agrícola. Finalmente se evalúa el impacto de dichos cambios sobre la Productividad Primaria Neta (PPN), uno de los atributos del funcionamiento ecosistémico más importantes e integradores, a través del cálculo de la Apropiación Humana de la PPN (AHPPN), un indicador muy completo del impacto antrópico sobre los ecosistemas. Los cálculos fueron realizados a partir de partir de los mapas generados y estimaciones de la PPN realizadas con estadísticas agropecuarias a nivel subnacional y modelación de datos de imágenes de satélite. La aproximación desarrollada permitió describir el uso/cobertura del suelo en Uruguay y los PRP, generalmente con altos niveles de exactitud, con resolución temporal anual y analizar sus cambios a lo largo del tiempo. Los PRP están inmersos en un fuerte proceso de cambios en el uso del suelo asociados a la intensificación de la producción agropecuaria y a expensas de pérdida de áreas de pastizales. La mayor expansión agrícola en el período estudiado se concentra en ambas márgenes de Rio Uruguay, en la Pampa Mesopotámica y en la porción oeste de Campos del Norte y del Sur, y en la porción oeste de la Pampa Interior. Esta nueva ola de agriculturización y el continuo avance del proceso de intensificación agrícola en los polos tradicionales han llevado a la casi total transformación de gran parte del área de estudio. Los modelos generados para cuantificar la densidad de arbustos permiten explicar porcentajes muy importantes de la varianza y poseen, en muchos casos, un poder predictivo razonablemente bueno. Sus resultados constituyen uno de los primeros antecedentes en la cuantificación de la cantidad de arbustos en la porción más húmeda del gradiente de precipitación en pastizales, donde ambos componentes del tapiz, el herbáceo y el arbustivo, son fotosintéticamente activos durante todo el año. La simulación de los modelos generados a partir de la información que recaban los sensores a bordo de satélites muestra el potencial para la generación de mapas que den cuenta de la densidad de arbustos. Mediante la cuantificación y la cartografía de los cambios producidos por los humanos en la PPN y su flujo en los ecosistemas, el concepto de AHPPN provee una estimación cuantificable de la intensidad del uso del suelo, a una escala y con un nivel de detalle nunca antes realizado. Los resultados muestran que el impacto humano sobre el flujo de energía en los ecosistemas de los PRP alcanza niveles muy altos en comparación con otras regiones del mundo, apropiándonos de más del 40% de la PPN de la vegetación potencial. Este impacto ocurre además a tasas crecientes debido al intenso proceso de cambio en el uso del suelo y está mayoritariamente concentrado en los grandes polos agrícolas de la región, donde la AHPPN es prácticamente total.

Main Points

The study employed a multi-step methodology to characterize Land Use/Cover Change (LUCC) and quantify its ecological impact, specifically focusing on ecosystem functioning and carbon appropriation in the Río de la Plata Grasslands (PRP).

The first methodological step involved developing remote sensing techniques using phenological data from MODIS temporal series and extensive field databases to classify LUCC over large territories, successfully generating a high-accuracy land use map for Uruguay.

The second critical technical contribution was focused on addressing woody encroachment (arbustización). The authors conducted a manipulative experiment utilizing hyperspectral sensors to build robust models for discriminating and quantifying varying shrub densities within natural grassland matrices. This was deemed essential for mapping this complex category and exploring the causes and impacts of shrubification.

The final phase quantified the impact on ecosystem functioning by calculating the Human Appropriation of Net Primary Production (AHPPN). This calculation integrated the newly generated LUCC maps, agricultural statistics, and satellite-derived NPP estimates.

The results demonstrated the intense nature of LUCC in the PRP, primarily driven by the intensification of agro-production, leading to significant loss of grasslands. The major agricultural expansion was mapped along the margins of the Uruguay River and specific sectors of the Pampas, culminating in the "almost total transformation" of large parts of the study area.

The quantification models for shrub density were successful, explaining significant variance and demonstrating reasonable predictive power. This provided pioneering data for quantifying shrubs in humid grasslands. The simulations confirmed the strong potential for using satellite information to create wide-scale maps of shrub density.

Ecosystem impact findings were alarming: the human impact on energy flow reached very high levels globally, with humans appropriating over 40% of the potential Net Primary Production (PPN). This appropriation is increasing and is nearly total in the primary agricultural poles of the region.

Conclusions

The thesis concludes that the Río de la Plata Grasslands (PRP) are undergoing a severe and accelerating transformation driven by agricultural intensification, resulting in extensive grassland loss. This 'new wave of agriculturalization' has led to the near-total modification of large study areas, affirming the urgent need for precise monitoring and analysis of land use changes.

Regarding the technical advances, the methods developed for quantifying shrub density are highly robust and represent a significant early achievement in mapping woody vegetation in humid grassland environments where both herbaceous and woody components are photosynthetically active. The success of the simulation using satellite sensor data validates the potential for generating broad, detailed maps of shrub density, which is crucial for understanding the complex process of arbustización.

Most critically, the evaluation of human impact confirmed that the anthropogenic pressure on PRP ecosystems is among the highest globally. The Human Appropriation of Net Primary Production (AHPPN) exceeds 40% of the potential NPP. This high level, which is rapidly increasing due to LUCC, indicates that human activity severely dictates the energy flow within the ecosystems. The impact is concentrated in agricultural centers, where AHPPN is 'practically total,' highlighting the extreme intensity of land use in these areas. The AHPPN concept proved to be an invaluable, quantifiable measure of land use intensity across unprecedented spatial and temporal scales.

Native tree and arborescent species for Agroforestry Systems in the Uruguayan-Brazilian Pampa

Guilherme Krahl de Vargas, Adriana Carla Dias Trevisan, Beatriz Marcela Sosa Calleja, Paulo Brack (2025)

Abstract

Tree species are vital to Agroforestry Systems (AFS), and selecting species that are suited to local conditions is essential for achieving this system’s objectives. In this study we characterized the phytophysionomies present in the Uruguayan-Brazilian Pampa Ecoregion in order to identify the most suitable species to include in AFS. In this sense we aimed to answer the following questions: i) Do the a priori defined phytophysionomies have a different species composition compared to the ecoregion? ii) Which environmental variables are related to species composition variation? iii) Which species are indicators of different phytophysiognomies? iv) Which native species are key for AFS implementation in these phytophysiognomies? We analysed floristic and environmental data from 106 sites across five phytophysiognomies using Non-metric Multidimensional Scaling (nMDS) and Analysis of Similarity (ANOSIM) and the Indicator Species Analysis (IndVal). Results revealed significant differences in species composition between the phytophysiognomies, indicating that the predefined formations have distinct species assemblages. Indicator Species Analysis IndVal identified 136 species, of wich 27 were prioritised for AFS use, contributing to a total of 84 priority native species, including those with multipurpose uses. These findings highlight the importance of considering local phytophysiognomies when planning AFS with native species in this ecoregion.

Main Points

The primary objective was to characterize the floristic composition of five major phytophysiognomies (Coastal Sandy Mosaic, Mixed Needle-broadleaved Forest, Savanoid-woodland Vegetation, Seasonal Semideciduous Forest of Atlantic Domain, and Seasonal Semideciduous Forest of Pampean Domain) within the Uruguayan-Brazilian Pampa Ecoregion to select native tree and arborescent species suitable for Agroforestry Systems (AFS).

Methods involved analyzing floristic and environmental metadata compiled from 106 sites using the NeoTropTree (NTT) database. The researchers applied multivariate statistical techniques, including Non-metric Multidimensional Scaling (nMDS) and Analysis of Similarity (ANOSIM), to compare species composition across the different phytophysiognomies. Additionally, Indicator Species Analysis (IndVal) was utilized to identify characteristic species for each ecological unit.

Results confirmed significant differences in species composition among the five predefined phytophysiognomies, suggesting that locally adapted AFS design is essential. The IndVal analysis successfully identified 136 indicator species. From this pool, 27 were specifically prioritized for AFS use based on their characteristics and multipurpose utility, contributing to a final, comprehensive list of 84 priority native species recommended for AFS implementation in the Pampa.

Conclusions

The study concludes that integrating native species into Agroforestry Systems is a vital strategy for fostering ecological transition and sustainability in the Uruguayan-Brazilian Pampa, especially when contrasted with the detrimental environmental and socio-economic impacts of exotic monocultures, particularly *Eucalyptus* plantations.

Crucially, the findings underscore the importance of local ecological specificity; AFS planning must consider the distinct species assemblages present within each phytophysiognomy, moving away from generalized restoration approaches. The rigorous scientific characterization performed provides the necessary technical groundwork—in the form of 84 priority native species—to guide practical implementation actions, such including seed collection, seedling production, and the design of context-appropriate AFS projects. This information is deemed indispensable for mitigating ongoing ecosystem conversion and supporting regional resilience.

  • "Implementing AFS with native species offers an alternative to eucalyptus monocultures."

Dispersal syndromes of Vachellia caven: Dismantling introduction hypotheses and the role of man as a conceptual support for an archaeophyte in South America

Nicolás Velasco, Ramiro Bustamante, Christian Smit (2023)

Abstract

Vachellia caven has a disjunct distribution at the southern cone of South America, occupying two major ranges: west of Andes (Central Chile) and east of them (mainly the South American Gran Chaco). For decades, the species has been subject to various ecological and natural history studies across its distribution, but questions concerning its origin in the western range remain unresolved. Thus far, it is unclear whether Vachellia caven was always a natural component of the Chilean forests, and "how" and "when" the species arrived in the country. In this study, we revised the dispersal syndromes of the species and contrast the two main hypotheses of dispersion to the west of Andes that have been proposed in the 90’s, namely animal versus human-mediated dispersal. For this, we reviewed all scientific literature on the species and explored the available information on morphology, genetics, fossil records and distribution patterns of closely related species. Here we illustrate how the collected evidence provides support for the human-mediated dispersal hypothesis, by including a conceptual synthesis that summarizes the outcomes of different dispersal scenarios. Lastly, and regarding the positive ecological effects this species has in the introduced area, we suggest reconsidering the (underappreciated) historical impacts of archaeophytes and rethinking the role that indigenous human tribes may have had in the dispersion of different plants in South America.

Main Points

This study conducts a review to determine the origin of *Vachellia caven* in its western distribution (Central Chile), resolving whether its disjunct distribution is explained by vicariance or long-distance dispersal (LDD), and which agent—animal or human—mediated the dispersal.

The methodological approach involved a scoped review of 133 sources, including research articles, reviews, and grey literature, focusing on literature related to the species' history, varietal patterns, and dispersal mechanisms (hydrochory and endozoochory). Key topics reviewed included phylogeny and fossil records, morphological varieties, and trans-Andean dispersal hypotheses (the guanaco/animal hypothesis versus the human-mediated hypothesis).

Results overwhelmingly supported the LDD hypothesis over vicariance. Phylogeny suggests *V. caven*'s clade diversified after the major uplift of the Andes. Crucially, palynological and fossil records show continuous presence of related Fabaceae species east of the Andes since the Holocene, but a complete absence of natural records for *V. caven* west of the Andes outside of anthropogenic settlement sites. Furthermore, while six morphological varieties exist in the eastern range, only one (*A. caven* var. *caven*) is found in the western range, consistent with a single introduction event rather than deep historical presence.

Regarding dispersal mechanisms, the review found hydrochory (water dispersal) implausible for many western localities where the species establishes on hill slopes. Endozoochory, while relevant today via introduced cattle, lacked strong support for crossing the Andes by native megafauna (like the guanaco). Consequently, the gathered evidence robustly supports the human-mediated dispersal hypothesis, suggesting indigenous tribes transported the species across the mountains, thereby classifying it as an archaeophyte in Chile.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that the presence of *Vachellia caven* in Central Chile is best explained by long-distance dispersal mediated by indigenous humans, confirming the species' status as an archaeophyte in its western range. This means the species was introduced thousands of years ago, predating European contact, and is not a naturally dispersed native nor a recent invader.

Based on this revised historical status, the authors argue for a fundamental shift in perception. They emphasize that *V. caven* provides "positive ecological effects" in the introduced area. Therefore, the final conceptual conclusion suggests that society should actively reconsider the ecological significance and historical impacts of archaeophytes, many of which have become integral to the local ecosystem structure. They propose rethinking the critical role that indigenous human populations played in shaping the distribution and composition of South American plant life.

  • "Vachellia caven is currently considered a native component in the Chilean flora and has shown positive effects on the Mediterranean-type plant communities [6–9], yet some studies have suggested that the species may be non-native, thus explaining the disjunct distribution [4,7,10–14]."
  • "In present times, Vachellia caven seeds are predominantly dispersed by cattle, specially in the western range [15]."
  • "All in all, from the above, we can conclude that there is more empirical support for a historical long-distance dispersal scenario than for a vicariant process for V. caven."
  • "In contrast, at the western distribution, there are no natural records (i.e. not from anthropogenic or culture settlements collecting sites), even in locations where the species is abundant today [43–45]."
  • "The case of Vachellia caven is even more striking, as it is one amids more than 20 species of closely related taxa (Acacia s.l) distributed in Argentina [20–22] but the only species occurring widely in Chile (Fig. 2) [23,24]."
  • "While all these varieties occur in the eastern distribution, only one variety (namely A. caven var. caven) has been documented for the western distribution (i.e. Chile, Fig. 1) [4]."
  • "The aims of this study are: i) summarise and propose the expected processes involved in the distribution pattern of the species (vicariance vs long-distance dispersal..."
  • "The dispersion unit in V. caven is the whole fruit (Fig. S2) making water a potentially important dispersal mechanism [4,17,18]. However, the problem with accepting hydrochory at least for the western range, is that there V. caven does not occur in many places with flooding-drought dynamics (such as rivers)."

Using worldwide edaphic data to model plant species niches: An assessment at a continental extent

Santiago Jose´ Elı´as Velazco, Franklin Galvão, Fabricio Villalobos, Paulo De Marco Ju´ nior (2017)

Abstract

Ecological niche modeling (ENM) is a broadly used tool in different fields of plant ecology. Despite the importance of edaphic conditions in determining the niche of terrestrial plant species, edaphic data have rarely been included in ENMs of plant species perhaps because such data are not available for many regions. Recently, edaphic data has been made available at a global scale allowing its potential inclusion and evaluation on ENM performance for plant species. Here, we take advantage of such data and address the following main questions: What is the influence of distinct predictor variables (e.g. climatic vs edaphic) on different ENM algorithms? and what is the relationship between the performance of different predictors and geographic characteristics of species? We used 125 plant species distributed over the Neotropical region to explore the effect on ENMs of using edaphic data available from the SoilGrids database and its combination with climatic data from the CHELSA database. In addition, we related these different predictor variables to geographic characteristics of the target species and different ENM algorithms. The use of different predictors (climatic, edaphic, and both) significantly affected model performance and spatial complexity of the predictions. We showed that the use of global edaphic plus climatic variables generates ENMs with similar or better accuracy compared to those constructed only with climate variables. Moreover, the performance of models considering these different predictors, separately or jointly, was related to geographic properties of species records, such as number and distribution range. The large geographic extent, the variability of environments and the different species’ geographical characteristics considered here allowed us to demonstrate that global edaphic data adds useful information for plant ENMs. This is particularly valuable for studies of species that are distributed in regions where more detailed information on soil properties is poor or does not even exist.

Main Points

The study employed a factorial experimental design using 125 Neotropical plant species to test the effectiveness of three predictor sets—Climate-only (C.models), Edaphic-only (E.models), and Climate-Edaphic combined (CE.models)—across four different Ecological Niche Modeling (ENM) algorithms. The climatic data consisted of 19 bioclimatic variables from CHELSA, and the edaphic data comprised 56 physical and chemical soil properties from the SoilGrids database. To address high collinearity among predictor variables, Principal Component Analysis (PCA) was used; the resulting PC scores explaining over 95% of the total variance were used as predictors (6 PCs for C and E models, 11 PCs for CE models).

The results demonstrated that the choice of predictor set (climatic, edaphic, or both) significantly influenced both model performance and the spatial complexity of the resulting predictions. Crucially, the authors found that combining global edaphic variables with climatic variables (CE.models) resulted in ENMs with accuracy similar to or superior to those constructed solely using climatic variables. Furthermore, the performance of the models—whether using predictors separately or jointly—was related to geographical characteristics of the target species, such as the total number of occurrence records and the species' distribution range.

Conclusions

The authors conclusively demonstrated that utilizing global edaphic data, specifically the SoilGrids dataset, significantly enhances the informational content available for building robust plant ENMs. This finding is crucial, especially when modeling species across large geographic extents characterized by high environmental variability.

This methodology provides substantial advantages for studies concerning plant species distributed in regions that traditionally lack detailed, localized soil property information, such as many areas within Latin America. The research reinforces the biological understanding that edaphic conditions are pivotal determinants of terrestrial plant niches. By demonstrating that combined climatic and edaphic variables improve predictive accuracy, the study challenges the common practice in plant ecology of limiting ENMs primarily to climatic variables, thereby providing a valuable methodological framework for conservation and ecological research.

Key Quotes

  • "Despite the importance of edaphic conditions in determining the niche of terrestrial plant species, edaphic data have rarely been included in ENMs of plant species perhaps because such data are not available for many regions."
  • "We showed that the use of global edaphic plus climatic variables generates ENMs with similar or better accuracy compared to those constructed only with climate variables."
  • "The large geographic extent, the variability of environments and the different species’ geographical characteristics considered here allowed us to demonstrate that global edaphic data adds useful information for plant ENMs."
  • "This is particularly valuable for studies of species that are distributed in regions where more detailed information on soil properties is poor or does not even exist."
  • "Choosing a particular environmental variable for ENM depends on the modeling purpose and its biological significance to the species under study [26]."
  • "For instance, variables related to soil properties are considered to be particularly important in determining the distribution of plant species, but have little direct effect on the distribution of the majority of animal species [27]."
  • "Consequently, it is clear that edaphic conditions play an important role in determining the niche of terrestrial plant species [25,42]."
  • "All of these studies reinforced the idea that plant ENMs could be improved by using a single or a group of edaphic variables."
  • "In contrast with climatic variables commonly used for ENMs... edaphic variables vary at local scales and with great complexity [30]."
  • "Even certain soil nutrients can determine the distributional transition from one vegetation type to another, such as that between Neotropical seasonal forests and savannas where the concentration of aluminum or potassium define the structure of these vegetation types [40,41]."

A dark scenario for Cerrado plant species: Effects of future climate, land use and protected areas ineffectiveness

Santiago José Elías Velazco, Fabricio Villalobos, Franklin Galvão, Paulo De Marco Júnior (2019)

Abstract

Aim: The anthropogenic climate change and land use change are considered two of the main factors that are altering biodiversity at the global scale. An evaluation that combined both factors can be relevant to detect which species could be the most vulnerable and reveal the regions of highest stability or susceptibility to biodiversity. We aimed to: (a) assess the effect of climate change and land use on the distribution of Cerrado plant species for different countries where they occur, (b) evaluate the effectiveness of the current network of protected areas (PAs) to safeguards species under different greenhouse–gas (GHG) emissions and land use scenarios, and (c) estimate the vulnerability of species based on protection effectiveness and habitat loss. Location: Bolivia, Brazil and Paraguay. Methods: We modelled the distribution of 1,553 plant species of Cerrado and evaluated species range loss caused by present and future land use and two GHG for 2050 and 2080. We assessed species vulnerability combining the representativeness of species within conservation units with the loss of species’ ranges outside PAs. Results: We found that climate change and land use will cause great damage to Cerrado flora by 2050 and 2080, even under optimistic conditions. The greatest impacts of land use will occur in the regions where the greatest richness will be harboured. The conservation of the species will be seriously affected since the PA network is not as effective in safeguarding them under current or future conditions. Main conclusions: The low level of protection together with the losses caused by the advance of agricultural lands will lead most species being highly vulnerable. Due to the distinct impacts of climate and land use over the three countries, conservation strategies should be implemented at transboundary and national levels.

Main Points

The study utilized ensemble ecological niche models (ENMs) combining six algorithms, using both edaphic and climatic variables (reduced to 9 principal components) to project the distribution of 1,553 Cerrado plant species. The modeling focused on two future time periods (2050 and 2080) and two climate scenarios (RCP 4.5 and RCP 8.5), simultaneously evaluating species range loss due to future land use change. Vulnerability was defined by combining the species' protection degree (representativeness within Protected Areas, PAs) with the anticipated loss of range occurring outside PAs.

The results project significant biodiversity decline, indicating that climate change and land use will inflict great damage on the Cerrado flora by 2050 and 2080, even under optimistic conditions. Crucially, the analysis revealed that the greatest impacts stemming from land use conversion are predicted to occur specifically in the areas harboring the highest concentration of species richness. Furthermore, the PA network was found to be ineffective in safeguarding these species under both current and future threat scenarios, highlighting a systemic failure in existing conservation efforts.

Conclusions

The primary conclusion is that the low degree of protection afforded by the current PA network, coupled with substantial habitat losses driven by the expansion of agricultural lands, will render the majority of Cerrado plant species highly vulnerable to extinction.

The authors stress that conservation actions are fundamentally compromised because PAs are inadequate, and unprotected ranges are simultaneously exposed to climate drift and extensive land conversion. Given the heterogeneity of climate and land use impacts across the three countries (Bolivia, Brazil, and Paraguay) where the Cerrado occurs, the authors conclude that effective biodiversity conservation necessitates strategic planning and implementation of policies at both **transboundary** and **national levels**.

Key Quotes

  • "The anthropogenic climate change and land use change are considered two of the main factors that are altering biodiversity at the global scale."
  • "A new climate and the expansion of production lands already challenge biodiversity conservation worldwide."
  • "Habitat loss due to anthropic land use is an important factor that drives current biodiversity to a worldwide crisis (Newbold et al., 2016)."
  • "The low level of protection together with the losses caused by the advance of agricultural lands will lead most species being highly vulnerable."
  • "The conservation of the species will be seriously affected since the PA network is not as effective in safeguarding them under current or future conditions."
  • "The greatest impacts of land use will occur in the regions where the greatest richness will be harboured."
  • "In order to halt the loss of biodiversity, international conservation targets and agreements have been established, such as the United Nation's Sustainable Development Goals..."
  • "The PAs are conceived to protect biodiversity and, consequently, to safeguard a proportion of species geographical distribution from disturbances..."
  • "In Brazil, the advance of agribusiness over large areas has caused the rapid disappearance of ecosystems and habitats characteristic of Cerrado, leaving it as a highly threatened and fragmented ecoregion with <20% of its remaining area undisturbed (Strassburg et al., 2017)."
  • "The proper management and conservation of biodiversity should take into account the impact of climate change and trends in land use (Pecl et al., 2017; Pouzols et al., 2014)."
Neutral Conservation and Restoration StrategiesGrazing and Land Use ImpactsPampas Ecosystem Degradation and Resilience

Transboundary conservation opportunities for Cerrado's plant species

Santiago Jos´e Elías Velazco, Fabricio Villalobos, Franklin Galvão, Paulo De Marco Júnior (2023)

Abstract

Cerrado is considered one of the world's most threatened and floristically diverse tropical savannas. Distribution of the Cerrado flora comprises three countries: Bolivia, Brazil, and Paraguay; consequently, opportunities to conserve it require a transboundary effort. We aimed to (i) explore the relationship between a representative portion of Cerrado flora (8611 species), natural cover loss, and three country boundaries; and (ii) identify priority areas to expand protected areas at national and transboundary levels considering the different species conservation status, countries' governance, and natural cover loss. We modeled the distribution of Cerrado species for the three countries and overlapped species ranges with country boundaries to calculate species ranges shared between countries and the degree of endemicity. We identified national and transboundary priority regions to expand protected areas. We found that the areas with the highest plant richness are distributed in the central part of the Cerrado and Campos Rupestres Mountain Savanna ecoregions in Brazil (>8500 for the whole country). Bolivia ranked second in species richness (>4300), followed by Paraguay (>3700). The most anthropized areas in Brazil overlapped with regions predicted to foster the highest biodiversity. Conversely, such patterns were opposite in Bolivia and Paraguay, which harbored regions with high opportunities for Cerrado flora conservation. Transboundary prioritization suggested slightly higher species protection, including threatened and endemic species. Restoring some regions primarily in Brazil will be necessary to protect 30 % or 50 % of the study area. Together, the three countries can support more comprehensive conservation actions for the great diversity of the Cerrado flora.

Main Points

### Methods

The study utilized Species Distribution Models (SDMs) for 8611 plant species across the Cerrado ecoregion, which spans Bolivia, Brazil, and Paraguay. The modeling process included 9240 species initially, filtered to those with high accuracy (TSS ≥ 0.7) and adequate records (>5 occurrences), utilizing algorithms like Maximum Entropy and Random Forest, based on climate (CHELSA) and edaphic variables (SoilGrids). To address geographical overprediction, models were constrained using a minimum convex hull polygon plus a 200 km buffer. Land-use data (MapBiomas) was reclassified into natural and transformed cover to quantify the proportion of natural cover loss (anthropization) for each analysis cell.

The core method involved spatial conservation prioritization using Zonation v4 (ADMU mode 2), which ranks cells based on complementarity and irreplaceability. This analysis compared national-level prioritization against transboundary prioritization, taking into account species conservation status, governance, and land-use change. Species were categorized by endemicity degree (low, medium, high) based on their range within the study area versus the whole distribution.

### Results

Plant species richness was unevenly distributed, with the highest concentration (>8500 species) found in Brazil, particularly in the central Cerrado and Campos Rupestres Mountain Savanna. Bolivia followed with over 4300 species, and Paraguay with over 3700. A critical finding was the misalignment between biodiversity and human impact: the most anthropized areas in Brazil overlapped significantly with regions of the highest plant richness. In contrast, Bolivia and Paraguay exhibited high-biodiversity regions that presented greater opportunities for conservation due to lower natural cover loss.

Transboundary prioritization yielded superior results, providing slightly higher protection outcomes for all species, including threatened and endemic species, compared to separate national efforts. The authors concluded that achieving ambitious protection goals (30% or 50% of the study area) necessitates restoration efforts, especially in the highly degraded regions of Brazil, highlighting the combined challenge of conservation and recovery in the biome.

Conclusions

The conservation of the Cerrado, one of the world's most threatened and diverse tropical savannas, urgently requires a transboundary approach due to its distribution across Bolivia, Brazil, and Paraguay. The reliance on standard national conservation strategies often fails to optimize ecological outcomes because geopolitical units operate independently of ecological boundaries and face unique political, economic, and land-use pressures.

The geographical analysis demonstrated severe conservation challenges driven by land-use change. While Brazil harbors the greatest diversity, it also faces the most intense anthropization, necessitating large-scale restoration projects to meet conservation targets. Conversely, the less degraded portions of the biome in Bolivia and Paraguay offer immediate high-opportunity areas for effective, proactive conservation.

Ultimately, the study advocates for integrated spatial conservation planning, confirming that transboundary prioritization yields more comprehensive protection, particularly for species with restricted ranges or threatened status. By coordinating conservation actions, the three countries can leverage their distinct conservation opportunities—combining proactive protection in Bolivia/Paraguay with restoration focus in Brazil—to ensure the resilience and recovery of the Cerrado flora.

Key Quotes

  • "Cerrado is considered one of the world's most threatened and floristically diverse tropical savannas."
  • "Distribution of the Cerrado flora comprises three countries: Bolivia, Brazil, and Paraguay; consequently, opportunities to conserve it require a transboundary effort."
  • "The most anthropized areas in Brazil overlapped with regions predicted to foster the highest biodiversity."
  • "Conversely, such patterns were opposite in Bolivia and Paraguay, which harbored regions with high opportunities for Cerrado flora conservation."
  • "Restoring some regions primarily in Brazil will be necessary to protect 30 % or 50 % of the study area."
  • "Conservation is challenging when species, ecosystems, or ecoregions are geographically distributed over many geopolitical units (e.g., countries or provinces..."
  • "Usually, species conservation status and priority areas for conservation are prioritized at the country level, where political and economic decisions determine the outcome of conservation plans..."
  • "Agriculture and other economic activities are responsible for habitat loss in the Cerrado (~55 % of its original native vegetation remains), making it a highly threatened ecoregion..."
  • "Because the countries where the Cerrado occurs may exhibit different patterns of land-use change and conservation governance, the three nations could jointly offer opportunities to conserve Cerrado flora more effectively."
  • "planning is often defined by national governments and carried out within geopolitical units and not within ecological boundaries..."
Supports Ecological Role and Nurse EffectsConservation and Restoration StrategiesPampas Ecosystem Degradation and Resilience

Ecological and functional differentiation in a plant species with disjunct distribution: morphology, niche and role of Acacia caven at west and east of the Andes

Nicolás Velasco Saragoni (2023)

Abstract

The historical processes that explain the trans-Andean disjunct distribution of Vachellia caven (Molina) Seigler & Ebinger (formerly Acacia caven) have been a long-standing issue in South American biogeography. Based on phylogenetic studies, the genus Vachellia likely originated in the Pampa region (Argentina), implying a potential dispersal of V. caven across the Andes to Chile. In this chapter, we review and analyze the available evidence concerning the dispersal strategies of V. caven (morphological, ecological, and ethnobotanical) to assess the feasibility of the proposed trans-Andean dispersal hypotheses: vicariance, zoochory (Lama guanicoe), and human-mediated dispersal. The review suggests that vicariance is unlikely due to the relatively recent geological uplift of the Andes. Furthermore, while zoochory by the guanaco is often cited, its effectiveness as a long-distance disperser across the highest Andean passes is highly questionable, considering the specific ecological needs of the guanaco and the short gut-retention time of the seeds. Conversely, the evidence strongly supports a scenario of human-mediated long-distance dispersal (archaeophyte), likely occurring between 10,000 and 1,000 YBP, associated with indigenous migratory routes from the Argentine side. This introduction hypothesis is consistent with the archaeological records of the species in Chile, the absence of specific dispersers, and the species' strong association with ancient human settlements. This framework is essential to understand the current morphological and ecological differences across the range and has major implications for conservation management.

Main Points

This double PhD thesis investigates the ecological and functional differentiation of *Acacia caven* (now *Vachellia caven*) across its major disjunct distribution ranges separated by the Andes mountains (Chile - West; Pampas/Cuyo - East). The overall methodology involves integrating biogeographic analysis, detailed morphometric studies, niche modeling, and large-scale ecological fieldwork.

Chapter 2 focused on the biogeographic puzzle of the species' origin in Chile. Using a comprehensive review of phylogenetic, fossil, morphological, and ethnobotanical evidence, the study evaluated the likelihood of vicariance, zoochory (specifically by *Lama guanicoe*), and human-mediated dispersal. The analysis concluded that vicariance is unlikely due to the Andes' relatively recent uplift, and natural long-distance dispersal by animals like the guanaco is physiologically questionable for traversing the highest passes. The key finding was strong support for the 'human-mediated dispersal' hypothesis, suggesting *A. caven* is an archaeophyte in Chile, introduced by indigenous populations between 10,000 and 1,000 years before present (YBP) from its likely origin in the Pampa region of Argentina.

Chapter 3 employed morphometric comparison using herbaria specimens, field sampling, and a common garden experiment to assess infraspecific differentiation across the disjunct ranges and among known varieties. This work aimed to quantify the extent of divergence in traits like fruit and leaf morphology, necessary for understanding adaptation and taxonomic status.

Chapter 4 utilized Species Distribution Models (SDMs) and climatic niche analysis to predict the source area and timing of historical introduction, further reinforcing the biogeographical narrative established in Chapter 2. This chapter quantified niche overlap and assessed how the historical dispersal event influenced the current distribution.

Chapter 5 investigated the species' crucial functional role by assessing its large-scale facilitative (nurse) effects across the geographic barrier. The study calculated the Relative Interaction Index (RII) for understory communities, examining how this nurse effect varies across steep environmental gradients, particularly rainfall, and differences in plant community composition between the west and east ranges. This chapter provides quantitative evidence for the species' ecological function, which is often controversial in management contexts.

Conclusions

The thesis provides a fundamental re-framing of the biogeographic history and ecological status of *Acacia caven* in its western distribution. The evidence overwhelmingly supports the classification of the species in Chile as an archaeophyte, stemming from a human-mediated dispersal event from the Argentine Pampas/Cuyo region. This conclusion significantly challenges narratives that might frame the species as recently invasive or lacking deep evolutionary establishment in the western range.

The research confirmed that despite its disjunct distribution and probable human introduction into Chile, the species has undergone significant morphological and niche differentiation across the Andes. This differentiation is crucial for conservation and management, suggesting that populations in the west and east are not ecologically or morphologically interchangeable.

Crucially for the context of ecological management, the thesis demonstrated that *A. caven* acts as a vital nurse species across its broad range, providing large-scale facilitative effects to associated plant communities. Chapter 5's finding of variable facilitative effects dependent on environmental stressors (like rainfall gradients) and plant community structure highlights the indispensable role of this species in ecosystem resilience and succession. The overall finding—that this species is an anciently established, ecologically functional nurse plant, irrespective of the range—has major implications for conservation, advocating for its protection and inclusion in restoration strategies rather than viewing it purely as a target for removal due to perceived woody encroachment.

Key Quotes

  • "Ecological and functional differentiation in a plant species with disjunct distribution: morphology, niche and role of Acacia caven at west and east of the Andes."
  • "Disjunct distributions are intriguing patterns where populations or ranges within a species are separated and isolated by some kind of barrier..."
  • "The case of Acacia caven [Vachellia caven] is a perfect example of a species that exhibits this type of distribution, separated by a geographic barrier, the Andes mountain range."
  • "The historical processes that explain the trans-Andean disjunct distribution of Vachellia caven... have been a long-standing issue in South American biogeography."
  • "The review suggests that vicariance is unlikely due to the relatively recent geological uplift of the Andes."
  • "Furthermore, while zoochory by the guanaco is often cited, its effectiveness as a long-distance disperser across the highest Andean passes is highly questionable..."
  • "Conversely, the evidence strongly supports a scenario of human-mediated long-distance dispersal (archaeophyte), likely occurring between 10,000 and 1,000 YBP, associated with indigenous migratory routes from the Argentine side."
  • "This introduction hypothesis is consistent with the archaeological records of the species in Chile, the absence of specific dispersers, and the species' strong association with ancient human settlements."
  • "This framework is essential to understand the current morphological and ecological differences across the range and has major implications for conservation management."
  • "Large-scale facilitative effects for a single nurse shrub species: impact of the rainfall gradient, plant community and distribution across a geographic barrier."

A VEGETAÇÃO DO PARQUE ESTADUAL DO ESPINILHO. 2 - ORIGEM DO NOME E CONSIDERAÇÕES FITOGEOGRÁFICAS

JOSÉ NEWTON CARDOSO MARCHIORI FABIANO DA SILVA ALVES LEONARDO PAZ DEBLE ANABELA SILVEIRA DE OLIVEIRA DEBLE (2014)

Abstract

A origem do nome e as tipologias de vegetação reconhecidas no Parque Estadual do Espinilho (Rio Grande do Sul, Brasil) são presentemente investigadas, com vistas ao esclarecimento de questões fitogeográficas pendentes na literatura. Palavras-chave: Barra do Quaraí, Fitogeografia, Parque Estadual do Espinilho, Rio Grande do Sul.

Main Points

The academic text analyzes the historical and phytogeographical context of the Espinilho State Park (PEE) in Rio Grande do Sul, focusing on clarifying taxonomic and nomenclatural confusion surrounding its characteristic woody species, primarily *Vachellia caven* (espinilho) and *Prosopis affinis* (inhanduvá/nhandubai).

The authors highlight that the term "Parque Espinilho," coined by Balduíno Rambo (1942), was based on a brief observation and contained significant botanical errors, mainly confusing the common names and scientific identification of *V. caven* and *P. affinis*. Rambo initially labeled *V. caven* as "nhanduvaí" and *P. affinis* as "algarrobo," despite the regional consensus that *V. caven* is the 'espinilho.' While Rambo later corrected the common names in a 1946 report, the original error influenced subsequent official naming.

The core finding is that the PEE in Barra do Quaraí is structurally dominated by *Prosopis affinis* (Inhanduvá Park or Nandubaysal) and should be phytogeographically differentiated from true *Vachellia caven* parks (Espinillal). The authors distinguish two main silvatic-campestral typologies: the *Parque de Inhanduvá*, which is associated with specific sedimentary formations (and characterizes the PEE), and the true *Parque Espinilho*, dominated by *Vachellia caven*, which is widely distributed across the state and not constrained by those formations. The article also notes the presence of Mata Ciliar and macrophyte communities within the park boundaries.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that the official name "Parque Estadual do Espinilho" is flawed and derived from a hurried, inaccurate reading of Rambo's foundational work, immortalizing one of his botanical errors. They argue that if the goal was to honor Rambo, the chosen name fails, as it highlights his mistake rather than his accuracy.

Phytogeographically, the article emphasizes the necessity of recognizing distinct vegetation typologies based on dominant species: the *Parque de Inhanduvá* (characterized by *Prosopis affinis* and *Vachellia caven*, specific to the PEE's location) and the true *Parque Espinilho* (dominated by *Vachellia caven*, widely distributed). They assert that recognizing the singularity of the *Prosopis affinis*-dominated vegetation is crucial for accurate fitogeographical studies and, implicitly, for conservation strategies, advocating for the name "Parque de Inhanduvá" for the unit.

Key Quotes

  • "A origem do nome e as tipologias de vegetação reconhecidas no Parque Estadual do Espinilho (Rio Grande do Sul, Brasil) são presentemente investigadas, com vistas ao esclarecimento de questões fitogeográficas pendentes na literatura."
  • "O presente artigo visa a analisar a origem do nome oficial da unidade de conservação, bem como descrever, resumidamente, as diferentes tipologias de vegetação nela existentes, com base em coletas e estudos desenvolvidos ao longo de mais de trinta anos."
  • "Esta ausência [do nome espinilho para Vachellia caven] se toma ainda mais evidente quando se observa que Lindman, autor de A Vegetação no Rio Grande do Sul, registra o espinillo (Acacia farnesiana) como espécie principal do pasto espinillo..."
  • "No Rio Grande do Sul, todavia, o nome espinilho é invariavelmente aplicado à Vachellia caven, e o de inhanduvá à Prosopis affinis."
  • "O equívoco de Rambo reside, basicamente, no nome atribuído às duas árvores chaquenhas: Vachellia caven foi chamada de 'nhanduvaí', e Prosopis affinis de 'algarrobo'."
  • "No texto acima transcrito, vê-se, claramente, que Balduíno Rambo não mais confunde a identidade das duas Leguminosas, chamando-as pelos verdadeiros nomes comuns (espinilho e inhanduvá ou nhandubai, respectivamente)."
  • "Ocorre que o termo 'Parque Espinilho' firmou-se na linguagem botânica e fitogeográfica do Rio Grande do Sul por dar nome, justamente, a essa unidade de conservação."
  • "Foi o 'grande gregarismo' das espécies de Prosopis nos arredores de Barra do Quaraí, que levou os autores do Projeto Madeira do Rio Grande do Sul a considerar mais adequado o termo Parque do Inhanduvaí para a vegetação ali encontrada..."
  • "Verdadeiros parques de espinilhos (ou Espinillares) [...] apresentam ampla distribuição nas áreas campestres do Estado, sobretudo nas regiões fisiográficas da Campanha, Serra do Sudeste, Depressão Central e Planalto Médio, devido ao fato de Vachellia caven, a sua espécie característica, não estar vinculada a formações sedimentares, ao contrário do inhanduvá (Prosopis affinis)."

CONTROLE DE PLANTAS NATIVAS INDESEJÁVEIS DOS CAMPOS NATURAIS DO RIO GRANDE DO SUL

Leonardo Araripe Crancio, Paulo César de Faccio Carvalho, Carlos Nabinger e Ilsi Iob Boldrini (2007)

Abstract

A conservação do campo nativo é importante para o Rio Grande do Sul, pois, além do aspecto econômico, tem também importantes implicações ambientais. O manejo inadequado da pastagem natural pode causar o aumento da freqüência de espécies nativas indesejáveis. Elas podem causar uma diminuição da produção do campo,pela da competição exercida com as plantas forrageiras de interesse, ou afetar diretamente a produção animal por causarem redução na ingestão de alimento ou mesmo efeitos tóxicos que podem, inclusive, ser letais. As espécies nativas indesejáveis, mais freqüentes no Rio Grande do Sul do ponto de vista da produção animal, são o caraguatá (Eryngium horridum Malme), a carqueja (Baccharis trimera Less.), a chirca (Eupatorium buniifolium Hook.), o mio-mio (Baccharis coridifolia DC.) e o alecrim (Vernonia nudiflora Less.). Dentre as estratégias disponíveis para controlar estas espécies podemos citar o controle mecânico, por meiode roçadas ou arraste de vigas de ferro; o controle químico, com a utilização de herbicidas; o controle biológico, por meio do pastejo, além de outras interferências que favorecem o campo nativo nas relações de competição intra-específicas, como a adubação. Algumas dessas intervenções influenciam a dinâmica da vegetação, como por exemplo o pastejo e o fogo. Obviamente , a eficácia de todos esses métodos depende das características morfofisiológicas e fenológicas, dentre as espécies. Portanto, é importante que sejam observadas as épocas corretas de utilização. Experiências demonstram que a interação entre os métodos pode promover uma maior eficiência no controle das espécies. Esta revisão tem como objetivo reunir as informações existentes sobre as principais plantas nativas indesejáveis do Rio Grande do Sul e seus métodos de controle.

Main Points

This article serves as a literature review focused on identifying and detailing control strategies for five native plant species (Caraguatá, Carqueja, Chirca, Mio-mio, and Alecrim) prevalent in the natural grasslands of Rio Grande do Sul (RS) that negatively impact livestock production. The central premise is that inadequate management of natural pastures leads to an increase in the frequency of these native species.

The review characterizes the biological threats posed by each species. *Eryngium horridum* (Caraguatá) causes significant competitive loss; studies showed 40% to 70% cover reduced forage production by 43%. Control methods must target the depletion of rhizome reserves, particularly during inflorescence development. *Eupatorium buniifolium* (Chirca) also causes severe competition and shading, evidenced by reports from Argentina where its infestation reduced meat production from 90–95 kg/ha/year down to 25 kg/ha/year.

*Baccharis coridifolia* (Mio-mio) presents a different problem: extreme toxicity, rather than competition. Toxicity varies greatly based on phenology (highest during flowering, March) and sex (feminine plants are up to 32 times more toxic). Ingestion causes lethal symptoms in livestock due to macrocyclic trichothecenes. *Baccharis trimera* (Carqueja) and *Vernonia nudiflora* (Alecrim) are noted for competition and minor toxic effects, respectively.

Control strategies analyzed include:

1. **Mechanical Control:** Mowing (roçadas) or dragging heavy implements (vigas de ferro).
2. **Chemical Control:** Application of specific herbicides.
3. **Biological/Management Control:** Optimized grazing intensity (pastejo) and fertilization, aimed at favoring desired forage species over the undesirable ones. The authors emphasize that the success of any control method (including interventions like grazing and fire) is highly dependent on the correct timing, which must be aligned with the specific morphophysiological and phenological characteristics of the target species. Combining different methods is suggested to achieve optimal efficiency.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that maintaining the economic productivity and conservation value of the Campos Sulinos ecosystem requires proactive management against native species deemed undesirable from a livestock production standpoint. These species—which increase due to inadequate pasture management—reduce carrying capacity either through direct competition for resources (water, light, nutrients) or through toxicity.

Effective management necessitates a detailed understanding of the biology, phenology, and reserve accumulation points of the target species. Control methods are species-specific, and timing is crucial, for example, hitting rhizomatous plants when their reserves are lowest. While acknowledging the environmental implications of the native ecosystem, the primary focus remains on securing a favorable balance for domestic herbivore production.

Ultimately, the authors assert that sustainable production management must incorporate strategies like regulated grazing, fertilization, and, when necessary, mechanical or chemical controls, often in combination, to influence the vegetation dynamics and prevent the competitive suppression of high-value forage species. The goal of this review is to provide the necessary information base to implement these targeted control measures effectively.

  • "O manejo inadequado da pastagem natural pode causar o aumento da freqüência de espécies nativas indesejáveis."
  • "Elas podem causar uma diminuição da produção do campo,pela da competição exercida com as plantas forrageiras de interesse, ou afetar diretamente a produção animal por causarem redução na ingestão de alimento ou mesmo efeitos tóxicos que podem, inclusive, ser letais."
  • "Conceitualmente, uma planta nativa indesejável para produção animal é aquela que, embora fazendo parte do ecossistema, ou não integra de forma constante a dieta do animal ou, mesmo fazendo parte dela, não contribui a longo prazo com o pleno atendimento dos requerimentos nutricionais dos animais."
  • "Além disso, diminui a proporção de espécies de interesse forrageiro pela ocupação de área, pela competição por água, luz e nutrientes ou pela interação destes dois fatores, podendo ou não apresentar efeitos tóxicos sobre os animais."
  • "A conseqüência direta das plantas indesejáveis é a diminuição da capacidade de suporte do campo..."
  • "A sustentabilidade de ecossistemas para a produção animal requer um manejo que mantenha os recursos do solo e assegure um balanço favorável entre plantas desejáveis e indesejáveis."
  • "Dentre as estratégias disponíveis para controlar estas espécies podemos citar o controle mecânico, por meiode roçadas ou arraste de vigas de ferro; o controle químico, com a utilização de herbicidas; o controle biológico, por meio do pastejo..."
  • "Algumas dessas intervenções influenciam a dinâmica da vegetação, como por exemplo o pastejo e o fogo."
  • "Montefiori e Vola (1990) demonstraram que uma cobertura de 40 a 70% de caraguatá pode provocar uma diminuição na produção de forragem da ordem de 43%."
  • "Na Argentina, onde a produção de carne alcança valores próximos a 90-95 kg/ha/ano, a infestação dessa espécie pode determinar uma redução da produção de carne para aproximadamente 25 kg/ha/ano, demonstrando ser necessária a aplicação de técnicas de melhoramento e manejo (MARCHESINI, 2004)."

Controle de Acacia farnesiana e de Mimosa pteridofita em pastagem

Ricardo Carmona, Bernardo Sayão Carvalho Araujo Neto e Roberto Carvalho Pereira (2001)

Abstract

Conduziu-se um experimento com o objetivo de estudar a eficácia agronômica e econômica de herbicidas para o controle de duas sérias plantas daninhas de pastagens: Acacia farnesiana e Mimosa pteridofita. Os produtos utilizados, por meio de pincelamento no toco, foram o óleo diesel, óleo lubrificante usado de trator, solução aquosa de 2,4-D + picloram e solução oleosa de 2,4-D + picloram. À exceção do óleo lubrificante, os herbicidas foram testados em dois tamanhos de planta daninha e duas alturas de corte. Avaliaram-se a porcentagem de controle e o vigor de brotação das plantas não-controladas. Concluiu-se que o corte das plantas só é eficiente no controle das duas espécies, quando realizado no nível do solo e seguido da aplicação de herbicida específico, como o 2,4-D + picloram. O óleo diesel também controla totalmente ambas as espécies, e com menores custos que o 2,4-D + picloram, porém apenas quando aplicado nas plantas mais jovens. Há incompatibilidade entre o óleo diesel e o 2,4-D + picloram no controle das duas espécies. O óleo lubrificante usado não apresenta nenhum efeito herbicida em plantas adultas destas espécies.

Main Points

The study investigated the agronomic and economic efficiency of various herbicides applied to the stump, immediately following cutting, to control two serious pasture weeds: *Acacia farnesiana* and *Mimosa pteridofita* in the Cerrado region of Formosa, GO. The target species are perennial shrubs common in degraded pastures, known to reduce forage productivity.

Five treatments were tested: diesel oil (pure), used engine lubricant oil (pure), 2,4-D + picloram in water (4% v/v), 2,4-D + picloram in diesel oil (4% v/v), and a control (cutting only). The herbicides (excluding lubricant oil) were tested factorially across two species, two plant sizes (smaller/younger and larger/adult), and two cutting heights (0 cm/soil level and 20 cm). Efficacy was measured by control percentage (absence of sprouting) and sprouting vigor, 90–95 days after application.

Results indicated that cutting the plants without subsequent herbicide application was generally ineffective, yielding a maximum unacceptable control of 50% for *M. pteridofita* (smaller plants cut at 0 cm) and near 0% control for *A. farnesiana*. Effective control required both cutting and chemical application.

2,4-D + picloram in aqueous solution achieved total control (100%) for both species, regardless of plant size, provided the cut was made at soil level (0 cm). Cutting at 20 cm significantly reduced efficacy. Pure diesel oil achieved 100% control, comparable to the chemical mixture, but **only** on younger/smaller plants cut at soil level. Diesel oil showed decreased effectiveness on larger plants. Additionally, the study noted an antagonistic effect when 2,4-D + picloram was diluted in diesel oil. Used lubricant oil was found to be ineffective as an herbicide.

Conclusions

The authors concluded that mechanical cutting of *Acacia farnesiana* and *Mimosa pteridofita* is only an efficient control method when performed at soil level (0 cm) and immediately followed by the application of a specific herbicide, such as 2,4-D + picloram.

While 2,4-D + picloram offered reliable 100% control across plant sizes when applied to stumps cut at soil level, pure diesel oil also achieved total control for the younger/smaller plants in the same cutting scenario. Crucially, pure diesel oil accomplished this control at lower costs than the 2,4-D + picloram mixture, positioning it as a viable and economical option for managing early infestations.

Conversely, cutting plants at a height of 20 cm significantly decreased the efficacy of all tested herbicides, suggesting the proximity to the soil surface is critical for product absorption and control. The study also determined that used engine lubricant oil holds no herbicide effect on these species. Furthermore, attempting to combine diesel oil and 2,4-D + picloram resulted in an antagonistic effect, reducing overall control efficiency.

  • "A Acacia farnesiana predomina nas áreas mais úmidas de baixadas, e a M. pteridofita apresenta ocorrência mais acentuada nos locais mais secos e altos. Ambas pertencem à família botânica Leguminosae (Mimosaceae)."

  • "An experiment was carried out to study the efficacy of herbicide treatments in controlling two serious pasture weeds: Acacia farnesiana and Mimosa pteridofita."
  • "Apesar da falta de recomendação oficial da pesquisa, alguns pecuaristas vêm utilizando, de forma empírica, o óleo diesel para o controle de plantas perenes em pastagem."

  • "O corte das plantas sem aplicação posterior de herbicida não foi eficiente no controle de A. farnesiana, pois praticamente todas as plantas emitiram brotações, três meses após esse corte."

  • "There is an antagonistic effect between diesel oil and 2,4-D + picloram to control both species."

Challenges in the conservation and management of legal reserve areas in Brazilian grassland and savanna ecosystems in the face of global climate change

TOMAS, W.M.; BAGGIO, R.; BERLINCK, C.N.; CAMILO, A.R.; CUNHA, C.N. da; DAMASCENO-JUNIOR, G.; DURIGAN, G.; DUTRA-SILVA, R.; FIDELIS, A.; GARCIA, L.C.; HERRERA, H.M.; LIBONATI, R.; MARENGO, J.A.; OLIVEIRA, M. da R.; OVERBECK, G.E.; PEREIRA, A. de M.M.; PILLAR, V.D.P.; PIVELLO, V.R.; RIBEIRO, D.B.; RIBEIRO, J.F.; SAMPAIO, A.B.; SANTOS JUNIOR, A. dos; SCHMIDT, I.B.; SORIANO, B.M.A.; TIEPOLO, L.M.; TIMO, T.P. de C. e; URBANETZ, C.; VIEIRA, D.L.M.; WALTER, B.M.T. (2024)

Abstract

Resumo – As áreas de reserva legal (ARLs) são parte fundamental da estratégia brasileira de conservação, juntamente com as áreas de preservação permanente. As ARLs são destinadas à manutenção da biodiversidade e podem ser manejadas de forma sustentável. Quando essas áreas abrigam ecossistemas dependentes de fogo e pastejo, como os campos nativos e as savanas, devem ser adotadas práticas de manejo adequadas à sua conservação e ao enfrentamento dos efeitos das mudanças climáticas globais. No entanto, esse assunto ainda é pouco discutido no Brasil, e as políticas públicas não são claras a esse respeito. Este artigo de revisão descreve os ecossistemas campestres e savânicos no Brasil, os aspectos legais relacionados com o manejo das ARLs, os cenários climáticos atuais e futuros, e a relação entre clima e risco de incêndios. Também apresenta uma revisão sobre o uso do fogo e do pastejo em ecossistemas campestres e savânicos, os desafios legais relativos à sua aplicação nas ARLs e o uso de geotecnologias no monitoramento destas práticas. Conclui-se que o pastejo e o fogo, como instrumentos de manejo, são adequados às funções das ARLs, desde que praticados segundo normas legais e cientificamente embasadas para evitar os efeitos negativos do seu uso equivocado.

Main Points

This article serves as a comprehensive review analyzing the ecological and legal challenges involved in conserving and managing Legal Reserve Areas (ARLs) in Brazilian grassland and savanna ecosystems, particularly in the context of increasing global climate change impacts. The authors establish that these open ecosystems (which include the Pampa and Cerrado) are characterized by a continuous herbaceous layer and that their evolution and maintenance are fundamentally dependent on disturbance regimes, specifically **fire and herbivory/grazing**.

Critically, the review highlights that Brazilian legislation (LPVN) lacks clarity regarding the management practices permissible within ARLs that contain non-forested vegetation, creating considerable challenges for conservation managers. The paper integrates the increasing risks posed by climate change—such as higher incidence of catastrophic fires due to increased biomass accumulation—to argue that active management is crucial. The authors emphasize that fire and grazing are not merely optional activities but are essential ecological processes that maintain biodiversity, cycle nutrients, and reduce fuel load.

Specific findings related to the Southern regions (Pampa/Campos Sulinos) note that these areas, due to their climatic regime and coexistence of tropical and winter grasses, possess an “excellent aptitude for grazing.” The authors assert that cattle ranching in the Pampa remains economically relevant and actively contributes to the maintenance of high biodiversity and ecosystem dynamics, often alongside prescribed burning practices.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that Legal Reserve Areas (ARLs) in Brazilian grasslands and savannas, which are naturally dependent on disturbance factors like fire and grazing, require a serious shift in management policy to ensure effective conservation, especially as they face global climate change.

The central conclusion is that **grazing (pastejo) and fire are suitable management instruments for fulfilling the core functions of ARLs**, which include conserving biodiversity and rehabilitating ecological processes. However, this suitability is conditional upon these practices being strictly regulated by legal norms and scientifically grounded guidelines. The authors stress that rigorous, knowledge-based application is necessary to avoid the negative consequences associated with the misuse or inappropriate application of these powerful ecological tools. The overall goal is to promote sustainable use and ensure the long-term resilience of these open ecosystems against environmental stressors and climate extremes.

Tropical forests and global change: ﬁlling knowledge gaps

Pieter A. Zuidema, Patrick J. Baker, Peter Groenendijk, Peter Schippers, Peter van der Sleen, Mart Vlam, and Frank Sterck (2013)

Abstract

Tropical forests will experience major changes in environmental conditions this century. Understanding their responses to such changes is crucial to predicting global carbon cycling. Important knowledge gaps exist: the causes of recent changes in tropical forest dynamics remain unclear and the responses of entire tropical trees to environmental changes are poorly understood. In this Opinion article, we argue that ﬁlling these knowledge gaps requires a new research strategy, one that focuses on trees instead of leaves or communities, on long-term instead of short-term changes, and on understanding mechanisms instead of documenting changes. We propose the use of tree-ring analyses, stable-isotope analyses, manipulative ﬁeld experiments, and well-validated simulation models to improve predictions of forest responses to global change.

Main Points

The authors analyze the current state of research on tropical forest responses to global change factors (CO2, climate, nutrients) and identify critical knowledge gaps that hinder accurate forecasting of forest dynamics and carbon cycling.

**Critique of Current Methods and Knowledge Gaps:**
Existing empirical evidence, largely derived from Permanent Sample Plots (PSPs), has documented changing biomass and tree growth rates over decades. However, PSPs often fail to identify the *causes* of these changes because they lack the necessary temporal resolution (remeasurement usually every 5 years) and do not allow the separation of effects from different global change factors. Understanding of physiological responses is strong at the *leaf level*, but poor at the level of the *mature tree* and the *population/community* level, particularly regarding the integrated effects of multiple stressors.

**Proposed New Research Strategy:**
The paper argues that major advances require a strategic shift in focus:
1. **Focus on Individual Trees:** Moving from leaves or entire communities to the individual tree level, where climate-induced physiological shifts translate into changes in growth or mortality.
2. **Focus on Long-Term Changes:** Analyzing data across multiple decades to centuries, aligning with typical tree lifespans and acclimation timescales, thereby overcoming the 'scale gap'.
3. **Focus on Mechanisms:** Prioritizing the understanding of causal mechanisms over merely documenting patterns of change.

**Four Key Methodological Approaches:**
To implement this strategy, the authors propose four underutilized methods:
1. **Tree-ring analyses:** To provide centennial-scale, annual-resolution data on tree growth, superior to decadal PSP data.
2. **Stable-isotope analyses:** Using isotopes (e.g., $\delta^{13}$C, $\delta^{18}$O, $\delta^{15}$N) from tree rings as an archive to reconstruct long-term physiological changes, such as intrinsic water-use efficiency (iWUE).
3. **Mechanistic Simulation Models:** Utilizing well-validated mechanistic tree-growth models and individual-based models to simulate tree and population responses to complex environmental changes and test hypotheses on acclimation.
4. **Manipulative Field Experiments:** Conducting large-scale, long-term experiments (like FACE or controlled warming/drought studies) on mature trees, which are currently lacking but are essential for testing hypotheses like $\text{CO}_2$ fertilization effects.

Conclusions

The authors conclude that the inability to predict future tropical forest dynamics stems directly from a fundamental lack of understanding regarding how individual mature trees and tree populations respond mechanistically to long-term environmental changes. The existing reliance on short-term, pattern-documenting approaches (like standard PSP monitoring) is insufficient for determining causality—a critical need given the profound implications for global carbon cycling and forest dieback risks.

To move forward, the field must aggressively adopt and integrate four specific, high-resolution research approaches: tree-ring dating, stable-isotope analysis, manipulative field experiments (e.g., $\text{CO}_2$ enrichment), and mechanistic simulation modeling. This integration is necessary to address the knowledge gaps at the individual tree level and link physiological responses (leaf level) to demographic outcomes (population level).

Ultimately, by focusing on individual trees, long-term historical records, and mechanistic understanding, researchers can develop more accurate forecasts of tropical forest resilience and inform necessary adaptation policies in the face of rapid global change.
